The Linguistic Argument from Surah Yunus 26
Razi presents the first face of the proof from Surah Yunus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses linguistic analysis of the definite article in Surah Yunus to prove that the 'more' (ziyada) must be the vision of Allah.

Core Idea
'Al-husna' cannot include everything; therefore 'more' must be beyond Paradise: the vision of Allah.

Imam al-Razi presents a subtle linguistic argument from the Quran. The verse promises 'the best reward and more' to those who do good. By analyzing the definite article in 'al-husna', Razi shows that the 'more' cannot be part of Paradise but must be a distinct, higher gift. This argument is a cornerstone of the Ash'ari proof for the beatific vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and first face
Read the Arabic verse and Razi's first argument carefully. Notice the role of the definite article.
2
Understand the two possibilities for 'al'
The definite article in 'al-husna' can indicate universal inclusion (istighraq) or refer to something already known (ma'hood).
These are standard Arabic grammatical concepts.
3
Eliminate universal inclusion
If 'al-husna' meant universal inclusion, it would already encompass the 'more', contradicting the word 'and' which implies addition.
Thus it must be the known reward.
4
Identify the known reward
The known reward among Muslims is Paradise and its pleasures (benefit and honor).
5
Conclude that 'more' is the vision
Since 'more' is additional to Paradise, and the only such additional thing promised for the Hereafter is the vision of Allah, it must be that.
This is the conclusion of the first face.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: قوله تعالى: { لِّلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَىٰ وَزِيَادَةٌ } [يونس: 26]. والاستدلال به من وجهين: الأول: إن الألف واللام في (الحسنى) إما أن يكون للاستغراق أو للمعهود. ولا يجوز حملهما على الاستغراق، وإلا لدخلت الزيادة فيها. وذلك يمنع من الزيادة عليها. فوجب حملها على المعهود. ولا معهود بين المسلمين إلا الجنة، وما فيها من الثواب المشتمل على المنفعة وعلى التعظيم. وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن تكون الزيادة شيئا مغايرا للثواب المشتمل على المنفعة وعلى التعظيم. وكل من أثبت شيئا زائدا على المنفعة وعلى التعظيم الموعود به في يوم القيامة، قال: إنه هو الرؤية. فوجب أن يكون المراد من هذه الزيادة الرؤية.
الثاني: إن النقل المستفيض صح عن رسول الله ﷺ أنه قال: «الزيادة هي النظر إلى الله تعالى».
The second proof: His saying, the Exalted: 'For those who have done good is the best [reward] and more' [Yunus: 26]. And the deduction from it is from two faces:
The first: The definite article 'al' in 'al-husna' is either for universal inclusion (al-istighraq) or for the known (al-ma'hood). It is not permissible to carry it to universal inclusion, otherwise the 'more' (ziyadah) would be included in it, and that would prevent the increase. So it must be carried to the known. And there is nothing known among the Muslims except Paradise and what it contains of reward that includes benefit and honor. If this is the case, then the 'more' must be something other than the reward that includes benefit and honor. And everyone who affirms something additional to the benefit and honor promised on the Day of Resurrection says that it is the vision (ru'ya). So it must be that the intended meaning of this 'more' is the vision.
The second: Indeed, the widely transmitted (mustafid) report has been established from the Messenger of Allah ﷺ that he said: 'The 'more' is looking (al-nazar) at Allah, the Exalted.'
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the beatific vision, based on the verse: 'For those who have done good is the best [reward] and more' (Surah Yunus 26). The proof is twofold.

First, Razi analyzes the word 'al-husna' (the best reward). The definite article here must either indicate universal inclusion (istighraq) or refer to something previously known (ma'hood). If it meant universal inclusion, then 'al-husna' would encompass everything good, including the 'ziyada' (increase), which would contradict the explicit mention of an additional thing. Therefore, 'al-husna' must be the known reward, which, by consensus, is Paradise and its pleasures—benefit and honor. Consequently, the 'ziyada' must be something beyond Paradise, something beyond mere benefit and honor. The only thing that fits this description, according to the scholars, is the vision of Allah. Hence, the 'ziyada' is the vision.

Second, Razi cites a hadith that explicitly interprets the 'ziyada' as looking at Allah. This hadith is described as mustafid (widely transmitted), which in the terminology of hadith indicates a strong level of transmission, though not as strong as mutawatir. The hadith thus confirms the interpretation derived from the first rational-linguistic argument.

Key Terms
الحسنى
الزيادة
الاستغراق
المعهود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'ziyada' refers to additional degrees of Paradise or physical pleasures.
Correction: The linguistic argument shows 'ziyada' must be something entirely beyond Paradise, which scholars have identified as the vision of Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the first face of the proof, why must 'al-husna' be understood as the known reward rather than universal inclusion?
A) Because the definite article always indicates the known
B) Because universal inclusion would make 'al-husna' include the 'ziyada', contradicting the word 'and more'
C) Because the word 'husna' is a proper noun
D) Because the Prophet said so

Key Takeaway
The linguistic analysis of 'al-husna' proves that the 'more' in Surah Yunus must be something beyond Paradise — and that is the vision of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If seeing Allah is the ultimate reward, does your longing for Paradise focus more on His gifts or on Him? What does your daily du'a reveal about that?
?
This argument shows that a small linguistic detail can overturn a major belief. Are there any beliefs you hold that you have not examined carefully with the Quran's words?
?
If the 'more' is purely relational—seeing Allah—then your worship is meant to culminate in intimacy, not just transaction. What one change can you make this week to shift your worship from seeking benefits to seeking the Giver?

The Prophetic Confirmation of the 'More' as Vision
Razi cites the hadith as the second face of the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a widely transmitted hadith (mustafid) from the Prophet explicitly confirms that the 'more' (ziyada) in Surah Yunus 26 is the vision of Allah.

Core Idea
The Prophet explicitly said: 'The more is looking at Allah.'

After presenting the rational-linguistic argument, Imam al-Razi strengthens it with a direct prophetic narration. The hadith, described as mustafid (widely transmitted but not mutawatir), leaves no ambiguity: the 'more' is the vision of Allah. This second face of the proof grounds the interpretation in both reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the second face
Read the short Arabic passage where Razi cites the hadith. Note that he describes it as 'mustafid' and 'sahih'.
2
Understand the term 'mustafid'
A mustafid hadith is widely transmitted but not quite mutawatir. It still carries strong authority.
Razi uses this term to indicate the hadith's reliability.
3
Connect to the first face
This hadith provides direct textual confirmation for the conclusion reached by the linguistic argument: the 'more' is the vision of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: قوله تعالى: { لِّلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَىٰ وَزِيَادَةٌ } [يونس: 26]. والاستدلال به من وجهين: الأول: إن الألف واللام في (الحسنى) إما أن يكون للاستغراق أو للمعهود. ولا يجوز حملهما على الاستغراق، وإلا لدخلت الزيادة فيها. وذلك يمنع من الزيادة عليها. فوجب حملها على المعهود. ولا معهود بين المسلمين إلا الجنة، وما فيها من الثواب المشتمل على المنفعة وعلى التعظيم. وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن تكون الزيادة شيئا مغايرا للثواب المشتمل على المنفعة وعلى التعظيم. وكل من أثبت شيئا زائدا على المنفعة وعلى التعظيم الموعود به في يوم القيامة، قال: إنه هو الرؤية. فوجب أن يكون المراد من هذه الزيادة الرؤية.
الثاني: إن النقل المستفيض صح عن رسول الله ﷺ أنه قال: «الزيادة هي النظر إلى الله تعالى».
The second proof: His saying, the Exalted: 'For those who have done good is the best [reward] and more' [Yunus: 26]. And the deduction from it is from two faces:
The first: The definite article 'al' in 'al-husna' is either for universal inclusion (al-istighraq) or for the known (al-ma'hood). It is not permissible to carry it to universal inclusion, otherwise the 'more' (ziyadah) would be included in it, and that would prevent the increase. So it must be carried to the known. And there is nothing known among the Muslims except Paradise and what it contains of reward that includes benefit and honor. If this is the case, then the 'more' must be something other than the reward that includes benefit and honor. And everyone who affirms something additional to the benefit and honor promised on the Day of Resurrection says that it is the vision (ru'ya). So it must be that the intended meaning of this 'more' is the vision.
The second: Indeed, the widely transmitted (mustafid) report has been established from the Messenger of Allah ﷺ that he said: 'The 'more' is looking (al-nazar) at Allah, the Exalted.'
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the beatific vision, based on the verse: 'For those who have done good is the best [reward] and more' (Surah Yunus 26). The proof is twofold.

First, Razi analyzes the word 'al-husna' (the best reward). The definite article here must either indicate universal inclusion (istighraq) or refer to something previously known (ma'hood). If it meant universal inclusion, then 'al-husna' would encompass everything good, including the 'ziyada' (increase), which would contradict the explicit mention of an additional thing. Therefore, 'al-husna' must be the known reward, which, by consensus, is Paradise and its pleasures—benefit and honor. Consequently, the 'ziyada' must be something beyond Paradise, something beyond mere benefit and honor. The only thing that fits this description, according to the scholars, is the vision of Allah. Hence, the 'ziyada' is the vision.

Second, Razi cites a hadith that explicitly interprets the 'ziyada' as looking at Allah. This hadith is described as mustafid (widely transmitted), which in the terminology of hadith indicates a strong level of transmission, though not as strong as mutawatir. The hadith thus confirms the interpretation derived from the first rational-linguistic argument.

النظر إلى الله

Third Proof: Veiling of Disbelievers Implies Vision of Believers
Razi begins the third proof citing the verse about the veiling of the disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse about the disbelievers being veiled from their Lord on the Day of Judgment is used as a proof that believers will see Allah.

Core Idea
If disbelievers are veiled, believers are not; thus believers see Allah.

Imam Razi presents the third proof for the beatific vision, grounding it directly in the Quran. The verse from Surah Al-Mutaffifin explicitly states that the disbelievers will be veiled from their Lord on that Day. This lesson unpacks the logical implication of that verse for the believers and how it functions as a scriptural argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic verse along with its literal translation. Notice the key term 'mahjubun' (veiled).
The verse is from Surah Al-Mutaffifin, verse 15.
2
Understand the implied contrast
The verse says the disbelievers are veiled. By contrast, the believers are not veiled. In the context of the Day of Judgment, this absence of veiling suggests direct sight.
This is the core inference that Razi uses.
3
Connect to the broader proof
Reflect on how this verse serves as a proof for the beatific vision. The argument hinges on the principle that being veiled prevents vision, so not being veiled allows vision.
The full kalam argument would include additional premises, but this verse is the foundation.

The third proof: His saying, the Exalted: 'No! Indeed, they will be veiled from their Lord that Day' [Al-Mutaffifin: 15]

Commentary
This passage presents the beginning of the third proof for the beatific vision. The verse from Surah Al-Mutaffifin states that the disbelievers will be veiled from their Lord on the Day of Judgment. The proof, though not fully developed in the excerpt, is based on the implication that if the disbelievers are veiled, then the believers are not veiled, and therefore they see Allah. The complete argument typically includes the principle that absence of veiling implies possibility of seeing, and since the believers are promised proximity to Allah, they will see Him. However, the text as given only quotes the verse, and the rest of the reasoning is presumably continued on the next page, which is not included in this window. Thus, this lesson introduces the scriptural evidence, but the full kalam argument is not present.

Responding to Objections on 'Nazirah'
Razi responds to an objection about the interpretation of 'ناظرة'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the metaphorical interpretation of 'ناظرة' by presenting alternative readings grounded in Arabic rhetoric and usul al-fiqh principles.

Core Idea
Not all metaphors are equal — Razi shows that his reading is superior to the opponent's by applying principles of usul al-fiqh.

In this passage, Razi engages in a detailed objection-and-answer exchange. The opponent argues that because the literal meaning of 'nazar' cannot apply to Allah, it must mean vision. Razi counters by offering two alternative metaphorical interpretations and then rebuts the opponent's responses using established principles of Arabic rhetoric and usul al-fiqh. This lesson unpacks the layers of argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial objection
Begin by reading the opponent's claim: the word 'nazar' literally means turning the pupil, and since that cannot apply to Allah, it must be taken as its necessary concomitant—vision.
This is a common argument in the vision debate.
2
Identify Razi's two alternative metaphors
Razi proposes two other metaphorical possibilities: suppression of the genitive (meaning 'looking at the reward'), and the idea that turning the pupil implies waiting (a type of benefit).
These are not arbitrary; they are grounded in Arabic rhetorical conventions.
3
Follow the rebuttal chain
The opponent responds that if 'ila' means 'with' or 'boon', then 'nazirah' would mean 'waiting', which entails grief and is unworthy of divine speech. Razi then rebuts from two angles: metaphor is better than suppression, and needing to add extra suppressions is against principle.
The argument relies on usul al-fiqh principles about prioritizing metaphor over suppression.
4
Reflect on the outcome
Understand why Razi's interpretation stands as more defensible within the framework of kalam reasoning.
This shows how theological arguments are intertwined with linguistic and legal principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للرؤية. قوله: (لفظ النظر لتقليب الحدقة، ولا يمكن حمله ههنا على هذا المعنى، فوجب حمله على لازمه، وهو الرؤية). قلنا: حصل ههنا وجهان آخران من المجاز: الأول: إضمار المضاف. والتقدير إلى ثواب ربها ناظرة. والثاني: أن تقليب الحدقة إلى جهة. كما يلزمه الرؤية، فكذلك يلزمه نوع النفع، وهو الانتظار. فلم كان حمل اللفظ على المجاز الذي ذكرتم أولى من حمله على أحد هذين المجازين؟ والجواب: أما حمل لفظ (إلى) على واحد الآلاء، أو على معنى عند، فإنه يقتضي حمل قوله (ناظرة) على الانتظار. وذلك غير جائز لأن الانتظار يلزمه الغم، كما قيل: الانتظار الموت الأحمر، والبشارة بما يوجب الغم غير لائقة بالحكمة. قوله: المراد من الآية إلى ثواب ربها ناظرة. قلنا: هذا مدفوع من وجهين: الأول: إن ما ذهبتم إليه يوجب الإضمار في الآية، وما ذهبنا إليه يوجب المجاز. والمجاز خير من الإضمار على ما بيناه في أصول الفقه. والثاني: أن النظر إلى الثواب لا يفيد الفرح. فلا بد من إضمار زيادة أخرى، وهي كون ذلك الثواب واصلاً إليه. وتكثير الإضمارات خلاف الأصل. قوله: نحمله على الانتظار. قلنا: إنه غير جائز.
For the vision (ru'ya). He said: 'The word nazar (looking) is for turning the pupil, and it cannot be carried here upon this meaning, so it must be carried upon its necessary concomitant, which is vision (ru'ya).' We said: There arise here two other aspects of metaphor (majaz): First: suppression of the genitive (idmar al-mudaf) — the estimate is 'looking at the reward of her Lord'. Second: that turning the pupil to a direction — just as it necessarily entails vision, so too it necessarily entails a type of benefit, which is waiting. So why is the carrying of the word upon the metaphor you mentioned more appropriate than carrying it upon one of these two metaphors? And the answer: As for carrying the word (ila) upon one of the boons, or upon the meaning of 'with', it necessitates carrying His saying 'nazirah' upon waiting. And that is not permissible because waiting necessarily entails grief — as it is said: 'Waiting is the red death' — and giving glad tidings of what necessitates grief is not befitting of wisdom. He said: 'The intended meaning of the verse is looking at the reward of her Lord.' We said: This is rebutted from two aspects: First, what you have gone to necessitates suppression (idmar) in the verse, and what we have gone to necessitates metaphor (majaz). And metaphor is better than suppression, as we have explained in Usul al-Fiqh. Second, looking at the reward does not bring joy, so there must be another suppression, which is that the reward reaches him. And multiplying suppressions is contrary to the principle. He said: 'We carry it upon waiting.' We said: It is not permissible.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure concerning the interpretation of the word 'ناظرة' (nazirah) in the verse describing believers looking at their Lord. The objector argues that the word 'nazar' literally means turning the pupil, but since that is impossible for Allah, it must be taken as its necessary concomitant, which is vision (ru'ya). The respondent (Razi) presents two alternative metaphorical interpretations: first, that it involves suppression of the genitive, so the meaning is 'looking at the reward of her Lord'; second, that turning the pupil toward a direction can entail not only vision but also a benefit like 'waiting'. He then asks why the objector's metaphor should be preferred over these two. The objector answers that if the word 'إلى' (ila) is taken to mean 'to one of the boons' or 'with', it would require 'nazirah' to mean 'waiting', which is impermissible because waiting causes grief, and giving glad tidings that cause grief is not wise. Razi then rebuts the objector's claim that the verse means 'looking at the reward' from two angles: first, the objector's interpretation requires suppression (idmar) of part of the phrase, while Razi's requires metaphor (majaz), and metaphor is preferred over suppression in usul al-fiqh; second, looking at the reward does not itself bring joy, so an additional suppression is needed (that the reward reaches them), and multiplying suppressions is against the principle. Finally, the objector suggests carrying it on 'waiting', but Razi dismisses this as impermissible.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
مجاز (majaz)
إضمار (idmar)
رؤية (ru'ya)
انتظار (intizar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate over 'ناظرة' is merely a wordplay without substantive theological or methodological basis.
Correction: Razi's argument relies on established principles of Arabic rhetoric and usul al-fiqh, such as the preference for metaphor over suppression, and the avoidance of multiplying suppressions.

Checkpoint
How many alternative metaphorical interpretations does Razi initially propose for the word 'ناظرة'?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Razi uses principles of usul al-fiqh to defend his metaphorical reading of 'ناظرة' over the opponent's interpretation requiring multiple suppressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a difficult verse of the Quran that challenges your understanding, do you accept the interpretation that brings you most comfort, or the one that is most sound according to the principles of Islamic scholarship? Which would you be willing to stand by before Allah?
?
Razi argues that waiting implies grief and that glad tidings of grief are not befitting of divine wisdom. Have you ever expected from Allah's promise something that actually caused you anxiety or impatience? What does that reveal about your trust in His timing?
?
The opponent's interpretation required multiple suppressions to make sense. In your own relationship with Allah, have you added unnecessary complications to your faith instead of accepting the straightforward meanings of His words? What would it take for you to simplify your faith and rely on the clear principles of usul?

The Second Proof: Verse of al-Husna wa Ziyadah
Razi presents the second proof for ru'ya from Surah Yunus, with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses Surah Yunus 10:26 to argue for the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife.

Core Idea
The 'increase' in the verse must be vision of Allah, distinct from Paradise's rewards.

Imam Razi presents his second scriptural proof for ru'ya, based on the verse "For those who have done good is the best (reward) and an increase." The verse pairs 'al-husna' (the best) with 'ziyadah' (increase). Razi argues that 'al-husna' must refer to Paradise and its rewards of benefit and veneration, so the 'increase' must be something beyond that—identified as the vision of Allah. He also cites a hadith where the Prophet explicitly interprets 'increase' as looking at Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the two arguments
Start by reading the verse from Surah Yunus and then the two aspects of Razi's inference. Notice that the first is linguistic, the second is based on hadith.
The verse is short but packed with meaning.
2
Understand the linguistic analysis
The first argument examines the definite article 'al' in 'al-husna'. Razi argues it cannot be for generalization because then 'ziyadah' would be included, making the mention of increase redundant. Therefore it must refer to something known (al-ma'hood), which is Paradise.
This is a key logical move: if 'al-husna' already includes everything, 'ziyadah' would be meaningless.
3
Connect the conclusion
If 'al-husna' is Paradise's rewards (benefit and veneration), then 'ziyadah' must be something else. Scholars who affirm an extra reward identify it as ru'ya. Thus the verse points to vision of Allah.
The increase is not just more reward but a different kind of reward.
4
Consider the hadith support
The second argument is a widely transmitted hadith where the Prophet (PBUH) explicitly says the 'increase' is looking at Allah. This confirms the linguistic conclusion.
The hadith adds prophetic authority to the interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: قوله تعالى: {لِلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَى وَزِيَادَةٌ} [يونس: ٢٦]. والاستدلال به من وجهين: الأول: إن الألف واللام في (الحسنى) إما أن يكون للاستغراق أو للمعهود. ولا يجوز حملهما على الاستغراق، وإلا لدخلت الزيادة فيها. وذلك يمنع من الزيادة عليها. فوجب حملها على المعهود. ولا معهود بين المسلمين إلا الجنة، وما فيها من الثواب المشتمل على المنفعة وعلى التعظيم. وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن تكون الزيادة شيئاً مغايراً للثواب المشتمل على المنفعة وعلى التعظيم. وكل من أثبت شيئاً زائداً على المنفعة وعلى التعظيم الموعود به في يوم القيامة، قال: إنه هو الرؤية. فوجب أن يكون المراد من هذه الزيادة الرؤية. الثاني: إن النقل المستفيض صح عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: «الزيادة هي النظر إلى الله تعالى».
The second proof: Allah's saying: {For those who have done good is the best (reward) and an increase} [Yunus: 26]. The inference from it is from two aspects: First: The definite article 'al' in 'al-husna' is either for generalization or for the known. It is not permissible to carry it on generalization, for then the increase would enter into it, and that would prevent increase upon it. So it must be carried on the known. And there is nothing known among the Muslims except Paradise and what is in it of reward comprising benefit and veneration. If so, then the increase must be something other than the reward comprising benefit and veneration. And everyone who affirms something additional to the benefit and veneration promised on the Day of Resurrection says: it is the vision (ru'ya). So it must be that the meaning of this increase is the vision. Second: The widely transmitted report is authentic from the Messenger of Allah (PBUH) that he said: 'The increase is looking at Allah Almighty.'
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. It is a classification of two scriptural arguments based on the verse 'For those who have done good is the best (reward) and an increase' (Surah Yunus 10:26). The first argument analyzes the word 'al-husna' (the best). It states that the definite article must refer to something already known (al-ma'hood), because if it were for generalization, the 'increase' (ziyadah) would be included in 'al-husna', making the extra mention of increase meaningless. The known referent among Muslims is Paradise and its rewards of benefit and veneration. Therefore, the 'increase' must be something distinct from both benefit and veneration. Since all scholars who affirm an extra reward beyond these identify it as the vision of Allah, the verse must be referring to ru'ya. The second argument is a hadith: the Prophet Muhammad (PBUH) explicitly interpreted 'the increase' as looking at Allah, which is a widely transmitted (mustafid) report, lending further support.

Key Terms
حسنى (husna)
زيادة (ziyadah)
رؤية (ru'ya)
الاستغراق (al-istighraq)
المعهود (al-ma'hood)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second proof relies only on linguistic analysis of the verse.
Correction: Razi also cites a hadith in which the Prophet explicitly interprets 'increase' as looking at Allah, making the proof a combination of Quranic exegesis and prophetic tradition.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the first argument about 'al-husna'?
A) It is for generalization
B) It refers to Paradise and its rewards
C) It includes the increase
D) It means vision of Allah

Key Takeaway
The second proof uses the verse from Surah Yunus to show that the 'increase' must be the vision of Allah, because 'al-husna' refers to Paradise's rewards, so the increase is something beyond.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe the increase is seeing Allah on the Day of Resurrection. Have you ever stopped to ask yourself: does that hope truly move you today? How much time do you spend imagining that moment compared to worldly pleasures? What is one specific habit you can change tonight to make your heart more attached to that vision?
?
The Prophet explicitly stated that the increase is looking at Allah. Reading this, do you feel a pull in your heart to long for that gaze? Or has the world dulled your desire? If you were to die tomorrow, would seeing Allah be your greatest hope? Start your next prayer by sincerely asking Allah to grant you that vision.
?
This lesson shows that Allah reserves a special reward beyond Paradise for the righteous. If you truly internalized that, would you live differently? Are you more concerned with avoiding Hell or with the honor of seeing Allah? This week, when you recite Surah Yunus, pause at verse 26 and let the weight of 'and an increase' sink in—then make dua for it.

The Third Proof: Verse of al-Hijab
Razi gives the third proof for ru'ya from Surah al-Mutaffifin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the verse about disbelievers being veiled as an indirect proof that believers will see Allah on the Day of Judgment.

Core Idea
Veiling the disbelievers implies the believers see.

Imam Razi presents a third proof for the beatific vision, this time from the Quran. The verse states that the disbelievers will be veiled from their Lord, and this is mentioned to belittle them. The logical implication is that the honored believers are not veiled — and to be unveiled before God is to see Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic verse from Surah al-Mutaffifin and its literal translation. Notice that the verse does not explicitly mention vision.
The key phrase is 'mahjubun' (veiled).
2
Understand Razi's inference
Razi points out that the veiling is mentioned in a context of belittling (tahqir). If being veiled is a punishment for disbelievers, then the opposite (not being veiled) must be an honor for believers. Not being veiled in the divine presence means seeing Allah.
This is an argument from the negation of the opposite.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Razi uses this indirect argument. It shows that the Quranic text implies the vision even when not stated outright. Reflect on how this method strengthens the overall case for ru'ya.
This is one of several proofs; each complements the others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: قوله تعالى: {كَلَّا إِنَّهُمْ عَن رَّبِّهِمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ لَّمَحْجُوبُونَ} [المطففين: ١٥]. ذكر كون الكفار محجوبين عن الرب سبحانه وتعالى في معرض التحقير لشأنهم. وهذا يقتضي أن يكون المؤمن المعظم مبرأ منه.
The third proof: Allah's saying: {No! Indeed, they from their Lord that Day are veiled} [Al-Mutaffifin: 83:15]. He mentions that the disbelievers are veiled from the Lord, Exalted is He, in the context of belittling their status. And this necessitates that the honored believer be free from it.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This is a straightforward proof from a Quranic verse (Surah Al-Mutaffifin 83:15). Razi explains that the verse states that the disbelievers will be veiled from their Lord on the Day of Judgment, and this is mentioned as a form of degradation (tahqir) for them. The implication is that the believers, who are honored (mu'azzam), must be exempt from being veiled. If they are not veiled, they must be able to see Allah, because the opposite of being veiled is seeing. Thus, the verse indirectly proves that believers will see Allah in the afterlife.

Key Terms
حجاب (hijab)
محجوبون (mahjubun)
تحقير (tahqir)
رؤية (ru'ya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse is a direct statement that believers will see Allah.
Correction: The verse does not explicitly mention vision. Razi uses an inference: veiling is a punishment for disbelievers, so not veiling (and thus seeing) is implied for believers.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of mentioning the veiling of disbelievers in the verse?
A) To describe a physical barrier
B) To belittle their status
C) To affirm divine transcendence
D) To indicate their ignorance

Key Takeaway
The veiling of disbelievers in the afterlife implies by contrast that believers will see Allah, as their honored status excludes being veiled.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the ultimate honor on the Day of Judgment is to see Allah, and the disbelievers are veiled as a punishment, what veil of sin or heedlessness in your life right now might be blocking your closeness to Him in this world? Will you remove it today, starting with one concrete step?
?
You claim to desire Allah's vision, but does your daily schedule reflect that longing? Is your pursuit of honor from people distracting you from the honor of seeing your Lord? What is one activity you can replace today with an act that brings you nearer to that vision?
?
The verse belittles the disbelievers by mentioning their veiling. Knowing that believers are exempt, do you live with the certainty that you will see your Lord? If not, what doubt resides in your heart, and how will you combat it with the conviction this verse provides?

The First Mu'tazilite Doubt: 'No Vision Can Grasp Him'
Razi presents the first Mu'tazilite doubt from verse 6:103.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazilite arguments from verse 6:103 against the possibility of seeing Allah.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites twist praise of transcendence into proof of impossibility.

The Mu'tazilites frequently cite Quranic verses to support their denial of ru'ya (seeing Allah). Among their strongest proofs is verse 6:103, 'No vision can grasp Him.' In this lesson, we examine how Imam Razi presents their twofold reasoning—first equating 'idrak' with 'ru'ya', then using the logic of praise and deficiency. Understanding this doubt is essential before we study Razi's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the English translation. Notice the two distinct arguments labeled 'first aspect' and 'second aspect'.
The passage is dense but structured.
2
Identify the first argument
The first argument equates idrak (perception) with ibsar (seeing) using linguistic evidence. Find the key sentence where Razi explains why they are synonymous.
Look for the statement about not being able to separate them.
3
Identify the second argument
The second argument uses the praise-versus-deficiency principle. Understand that Allah praising Himself for not being seen implies that being seen would be a deficiency.
This argument aims to make vision impossible, not just absent.
4
Reflect on the structure
Notice that the Mu'tazilites use the same verse for two different purposes: direct negation ('no one sees Him') and impossibility ('cannot see Him'). Reflect on why this distinction matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى: التمسك بقوله تعالى: {لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ} [الأنعام: ۱۰۳]. اعلم أنهم تارة يتمسكون بهذه الآية في بيان أنه تعالى لا يراه أحد، وأخرى في بيان أنه يمتنع أن يراه أحد. أما الوجه الأول: فتقريره أن نقول: الإدراك المضاف إلى البصر هو الإبصار، بدليل أنه لا يصح إثبات أحدهما مع نفي الآخر، فلا يصح أن يقال: رأيته وما أدركته بعيني، وأن يقال: أدركته بعيني وما رأيته. وهذا يدل على أن إدراك البصر والرؤية شيء واحد. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إنه تعالى نفى أن يدركه الأبصار. وذلك يقتضي أن لا يراه أحد. وهذا يتناول جميع الأبصار في جميع الأوقات. وأما الوجه الثاني: فهو أنه تعالى يمدح نفسه بأنه لا يدركه شيء من الأبصار، وكل ما كان عدمه مدحاً كان وجوده نقصاً، والنقص على الله تعالى محال، فوجب أن تكون الرؤية ممتنعة على الله تعالى.
The first doubt: Adhering to His saying: {No vision can grasp Him} [Al-An'am: 6:103]. Know that sometimes they cling to this verse to demonstrate that no one sees Him, and at other times to demonstrate that it is impossible that anyone sees Him. As for the first aspect: its explanation is that we say: Perception (idrak) attributed to sight is seeing (ibsar), as evidenced by the fact that it is not valid to affirm one while negating the other—so it is not correct to say: 'I saw him but did not perceive him with my eye,' nor to say: 'I perceived him with my eye but did not see him.' This indicates that visual perception and vision are one thing. Once this is established, we say: He, exalted, has negated that vision can grasp Him. That necessitates that no one sees Him. And this encompasses all sights at all times. As for the second aspect: it is that He, exalted, praises Himself by saying that no vision can grasp Him, and everything whose absence is praise, its existence would be a deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Allah, so vision must be impossible for Allah.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents the first Mu'tazilite doubt against ru'ya, based on the Quranic verse 'No vision can grasp Him' (Surah Al-An'am 6:103). Razi explains that the Mu'tazilites use this verse in two ways. The first argument equates idrak (perception) with ibsar (seeing), arguing that they are synonymous based on linguistic evidence—you cannot say 'I saw but did not perceive' or vice versa. Therefore, the verse's negation of idrak implies negation of ru'ya for all sights at all times. The second argument focuses on the fact that Allah praises Himself for not being perceived. If the absence of a thing is a ground for praise, then its existence would be a deficiency (naqs). Since deficiency is impossible for Allah, vision (which would involve idrak) cannot be attributed to Him.

Key Terms
إدراك (idrak)
أبصار (absar)
رؤية (ru'ya)
مدح (madh)
نقص (naqs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse simply means 'no one sees Allah' without considering the deeper argument about impossibility.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites actually have two distinct arguments: one of simple negation (no one sees) and one of impossibility (it cannot happen). The second is more philosophically robust.

Checkpoint
According to the first Mu'tazilite argument, why does the verse 'No vision can grasp Him' prove that no one sees Allah?
A) Because Allah is beyond physical sight.
B) Because idrak and ru'ya are synonymous.
C) Because the verse is a command.
D) Because vision would be a deficiency.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites leverage the same Quranic verse both to deny the occurrence of seeing Allah and to argue its rational impossibility, using linguistic and logical principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'No vision can grasp Him,' do you stop at the surface meaning, or do you consider how others—like the Mu'tatilites—might interpret it differently? What does that reveal about your approach to understanding Allah's words, and are you willing to dig deeper to defend the truth even when it challenges your initial understanding?
?
The Mu'tazilites used rational arguments to deny ru'ya, relying on logical principles. Have you ever let your own reasoning override a revealed truth? In what area of your faith are you most tempted to say 'this doesn't make sense' rather than submitting to what Allah and His Messenger have said?
?
If the absence of a thing is praise, then its existence would be deficiency. Apply this principle to your own concept of Allah: Do you ever describe Allah in ways that actually strip away His attributes under the guise of 'transcendence'? Examine your own heart – are you truly affirming what Allah affirms of Himself, or are you subtly denying some of His beautiful names and attributes because you think it's more 'sophisticated'?

The Mu'tazilite Doubt from 'You Will Not See Me'
Razi presents the second Mu'tazilite doubt from the verse to Moses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that the word 'lan' in the Quranic verse to Moses proves that Allah cannot be seen, and the reasoning they use.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites use 'lan' as a linguistic proof for eternal negation of seeing Allah.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents one of the most famous Mu'tazilite objections to the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. They base their argument on Allah's words to Moses: 'You will not see Me' (Quran 7:143). They claim that the Arabic particle 'lan' indicates eternal negation, and this principle, when applied to all believers, denies any vision of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the key terms: 'lan' and 'ta'bid'.
The passage is a concise presentation of the doubt.
2
Identify the linguistic claim
The Mu'tazilites claim that 'lan' (لن) indicates eternal negation (ta'bid). They support this with another verse: 'Say: you will not follow us' (Quran 48:15).
3
Understand the consensus argument
They argue that since this verse was addressed to Moses, and there is consensus that no one differentiates between prophets and others, the ruling applies to all people.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument challenges the Sunni belief that believers will see Allah in the hereafter. The debate hinges on the meaning of 'lan'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية: تمسكوا بقوله تعالى لموسى: {لَن تَرَانِي} [الأعراف: ١٤٣]. وهذه الكلمة للتأبيد، بدليل قوله تعالى: {قُل لَن تَتَّبِعُونَا} [الفتح: ١٥]. وإذا ثبت هذا في حق موسى ثبت في حق غيره، لانعقاد الإجماع على أنه لا قائل بالفرق. فثبت: أن موسى عليه السلام لا يراه.
The second doubt: They clung to His saying to Moses: {You will not see Me} [Al-A'raf: 7:143]. And this word is for eternal negation (ta'bid), as evidenced by His saying: {Say: you will not follow us} [Al-Fath: 48:15]. And if this is established with respect to Moses, it is established with respect to others, due to the consensus that there is no one who makes a distinction. So it is established that Moses, peace be upon him, does not see Him.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite doubt, based on Allah's statement to Moses: 'You will not see Me' (Surah Al-A'raf 7:143). The Mu'tazilites argue that the word 'lan' in Arabic indicates eternal negation (ta'bid)—it negates the action forever. They support this by citing another verse: 'Say: you will not follow us' (Surah Al-Fath 48:15), where 'lan' is taken to mean permanent prohibition. Since this is said to Moses, who is a prophet, and there is consensus that no one differentiates between Moses and others (i.e., what applies to him applies to all), they conclude that no one can see Allah, not even in the afterlife.

Key Terms
لن (lan)
تأبيد (ta'bid)
موسى (Musa)
إجماع (ijma')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite argument is based on the claim that 'lan' always indicates eternal negation, which is a settled linguistic fact.
Correction: The interpretation of 'lan' as eternal negation is debated among scholars; many argue it can indicate temporary negation depending on context.

Checkpoint
What evidence do the Mu'tazilites provide for 'lan' indicating eternal negation?
A) A hadith
B) Another Quranic verse
C) Rational analysis
D) Consensus of grammarians

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argue that the verse 'You will not see Me' uses 'lan' to negate seeing forever, and by consensus this applies to all people.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a theological claim because it seemed linguistically logical, without checking whether it aligns with the consensus of the Salaf? How will you respond the next time you encounter a verse that seems to contradict what you know about Allah's attributes?
?
The Mu'tazilites built a whole argument on the meaning of one word, 'lan'. Are you confident that your understanding of Arabic is sufficient to interpret the Quran, or do you rely on the scholars? What step can you take today to deepen your reliance on sound tafsir?

The Third Mu'tazilite Doubt: Speech and Vision
Razi presents the third Mu'tazilite doubt from Surah Al-Shura 42:51.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument from Qur'an 42:51 that divine speech precludes vision, and why it is considered a doubt.

Core Idea
A veil implies no sight—so Mu'tazilites argue speech and vision cannot coexist.

The Mu'tazilites base their third doubt on a well-known verse. They claim that the mode of divine speech described in the Qur'an necessarily excludes seeing Allah. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the underlying assumption about veils and vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Start by reading the Arabic verse and its literal translation. Notice the phrases 'except by revelation or from behind a veil'.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite inference
The Mu'tazilites argue that 'from behind a veil' means the person spoken to does not see the speaker. This is the key inference.
Consider: does a veil always prevent vision?
3
Extend the argument to all times
They claim if vision is negated at the time of speech, it is negated at all times, because no one differentiates between moments.
4
Reflect on the underlying assumption
The argument assumes that 'behind a veil' necessarily implies non-vision. Could one see through a veil? This is the point of doubt.
This is why Ash'aris consider it a weak argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة: تمسكوا بقوله تعالى: {وَمَا كَانَ لِبَشَرٍ أَن يُكَلِّمَهُ اللَّهُ إِلَّا وَحْيًا أَوْ مِن وَرَاءِ حِجَابٍ} [الشورى: ٥١]. دلت هذه الآية على أن كل من يكلم الله تعالى، فإنه لا يراه. وإذا ثبت عدم الرؤية في وقت الكلام ثبت عدم الرؤية في غير وقت الكلام، ضرورة أنه لا قائل بالفرق.
The third doubt: They clung to His saying: {And it is not for any human being that Allah should speak to him except by revelation or from behind a veil} [Al-Shura: 42:51]. This verse indicates that everyone whom Allah speaks to does not see Him. If the negation of vision is established at the time of speech, it is established at other times, necessarily because there is no one who makes a distinction.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This is the third Mu'tazilite doubt, based on Surah Al-Shura 42:51, which states that Allah only speaks to humans either by revelation (wahy) or from behind a veil (hijab). The Mu'tazilites argue that 'from behind a veil' implies that the person spoken to does not see the speaker. Since this is the only mode of direct speech (without an intermediary) besides revelation, they deduce that whenever Allah speaks to someone, that person does not see Him. If vision is negated at the moment of speech, it must also be negated at other times, because there is no rational basis to differentiate between moments (no one claims that vision becomes possible after speech ends). Therefore, vision is impossible for anyone whom Allah has addressed or will address.

Key Terms
بشر
تكليم
وحي
حجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves that no one can see Allah because speech always occurs without vision.
Correction: The verse only describes modes of speech; it does not explicitly state that vision is impossible. The veil could allow vision through it, so the inference is not conclusive.

Checkpoint
What conclusion do the Mu'tazilites draw from the phrase 'from behind a veil'?
A) The person speaks to Allah
B) The person does not see Allah
C) The person is a prophet
D) The speech is indirect

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites interpret 'from behind a veil' as negating vision, forming their third doubt against seeing Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Qur'an 42:51, do you accept it as describing Allah's actions as He wills, or do you, like the Mu'tazilites, impose your own logic to limit what Allah can do? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in His absolute power?
?
The Mu'tazilites used a verse to deny seeing Allah because they assumed a veil must block sight. Are there truths about Allah that you reject because your mind cannot comprehend them? Will you humble yourself and believe what the Qur'an and authentic hadith promise, even if you cannot fully grasp it?
?
If you were in the place of the Mu'tazilites, would you have the courage to admit that your interpretation might be wrong, or would you cling to your reasoning out of pride? Ask Allah to protect you from letting your intellect become a veil between you and the truth.

The Fourth Mu'tazilite Doubt: Astonishment at Ru'ya in the Quran (Incomplete)
Razi begins the fourth Mu'tazilite doubt citing the first of three verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that the Quran always portrays the request to see Allah as astonishing, and why this is problematic for the Sunni belief in ru'ya.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites argue: every Quranic mention of 'seeing Allah' is met with astonishment.

The Mu'tazilites present a scriptural argument against the possibility of seeing Allah in the hereafter. They claim that whenever the Quran refers to seeing Allah, it does so in a context of disapproval or impossibility. This lesson introduces the fourth doubt and its first proof text from Surah Al-Baqarah. However, the passage is incomplete — only the first of three verses is given, and no refutation is provided.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Note that it presents a claim and then cites a verse. Do not worry about the incomplete nature yet.
The passage says 'in three verses' but only gives one.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
The claim is that Allah never mentions ru'ya in the Quran except by expressing astonishment (isti'zam). Identify the key term 'isti'zam' and reflect on what it implies.
3
Examine the first verse
Look at the verse: the Israelites demand to see Allah plainly. How does the Quran treat this demand? Does it show it as praiseworthy or blameworthy?
The verse is in the context of reprimand.
4
Recognize the incomplete argument
The Mu'tazilites claim three verses, but only one is given here. This lesson only covers the beginning of the doubt. The other verses and the refutation are missing from this passage.
Later lessons will complete the argument and provide the Sunni response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة: أنه تعالى ما ذكر الرؤية في القرآن إلا وقد استعظمها. وذلك في ثلاث آيات: أولها قوله تعالى: {وَإِذْ قُلْتُمْ يَا مُوسَى لَن نُّؤْمِنَ لَكَ حَتَّى نَرَى اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً} [البقرة: ٥٥].

Commentary
This is the beginning of the fourth Mu'tazilite doubt, though it is incomplete on this page. The Mu'tazilites argue that the Quran always presents the request for seeing Allah as something astonishing or blameworthy. They cite three verses where ru'ya is mentioned in contexts of reprimand or impossibility. Here, Razi only gives the first example: the Israelites' demand to see Allah openly (jahratan), which was met with divine anger. The implication is that such a request is inherently inappropriate, so ru'ya cannot be a blessed reward for believers. The other two verses are not provided here, and the discussion presumably continues on the next page(s).

Key Terms
استعظام (isti'zam)
جهرة (jahratan)

Correction: The Israelites' demand was blameworthy because of their stubbornness and lack of faith, not because seeing Allah is inherently impossible. The Sunni belief is that believers will see Allah in the hereafter as a mercy, not as a demand.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The Israelites said 'we will never believe until we see Allah plainly.' In what areas of your life are you holding out for a clear, undeniable sign before you fully submit? Are you willing to believe without seeing, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) taught?
?
When you read this verse, do you join the Mu'tazilites in misinterpreting it to deny the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife? Or do you recognize that the problem was not the request itself, but the attitude behind it? Examine your heart: do you approach Allah with humility or entitlement?

Introduction and First Transmitted Doubt Against Ru'ya
Razi introduces the chapter and presents the first transmitted doubt with its two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first transmitted doubt against ru'ya, which is based on the verse 'And vision perceives Him not', and its two rational aspects.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argued that negating perception means negating vision entirely.

Imam al-Razi begins his discussion of the Mu'tazili rejection of seeing Allah in the afterlife by first acknowledging that they use both rational and scriptural arguments. He then presents the first of four scriptural proofs: the verse 'And vision perceives Him not' (al-An'am 103). This passage details how the Mu'tazila derive two distinct arguments from this single verse, one linguistic and one theological.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Razi introduces the chapter and then immediately presents the first doubt.
Pay attention to the phrase 'أما الوجه الأول' and 'وأما الوجه الثاني'.
2
Identify the two aspects of the first doubt
The passage splits the first doubt into two aspects. Find where each begins and summarise each in your own words.
The first aspect is linguistic (idrak = ru'ya), the second is theological (praise implies impossibility).
3
Understand the linguistic claim about 'idrak' and 'ru'ya'
The Mu'tazila claim that idrak al-basar (perception of the eye) is identical to ru'ya (vision). They support this by saying people use both expressions interchangeably. Grasp this as the key premise.
If idrak = ru'ya, then negating idrak negates ru'ya.
4
Reflect on the theological praise argument
The second aspect argues that Allah praises Himself for not being perceived. If perception were possible, it would be a deficiency. Think about why this implies impossibility.
Deficiency is impossible for Allah, so ru'ya must be impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس : في حكاية شبه المعتزلة في إنكار الرؤية والجواب عنها اعلم أنهم يتمسكون بوجوه عقلية، وبوجوه نقلية.
أما الشبه النقلية فأربع :
الشبهة الأولى : التمسك بقوله تعالى : ولا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] اعلم أنهم تارة يتمسكون بهذه الآية في بيان أنه تعالى لا يراه أحد، وأخرى في بيان أنه يمتنع أن يراه أحد.
أما الوجه الأول : فتقريره أن نقول : الإدراك المضاف إلى البصر هو رأيته وما أدركته بعيني وأن يقال : أدركته بعيني وما رأيته وهذا يدل على أن إدراك البصر والرؤية شيء واحد. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنه تعالى نفى أن يدركه أحد الأبصار في جميع الأوقات. وذلك يقتضي أن لا يراه أحد من الأبصار في جميع الأوقات.
وأما الوجه الثاني: فهو أنه تعالى يمدح نفسه بأنه لا يدركه شيء من الأبصار، وكل ما كان عدمه مدحا كان وجوده نقصا والنقص على الله تعالى محال فوجب أن تكون الرؤية ممتنعة على الله تعالى.
الشبهة الثانية تمسكوا بقوله تعالى لموسى : لن تراني [الأعراف : 143] وهذه الكلمة للتأبيد، بدلیل قوله تعالى : قُل لَن تَتَّبِعُونَا ﴾ [الفَتْح : ١٥] اه قط، وإذا ثبت هذا في حق موسى ثبت في حق غيره لانعقاد الإجماع على أنه لا قائل بالفرق.
الشبهة الثالثة : تمسكوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كَانَ لِبَشَرٍ أَن يُكَلِّمَهُ اللهُ إِلَّا وَحْيًّا أَوْ مِن وَرَاى حجاب [الشورى: ٥١] دلت هذه الآية على أن كل من يكلم الله تعالى، فإنه لا يراه وإذا ثبت عدم الرؤية في وقت الكلام ثبت عدم الرؤية في غير وقت الكلام ضرورة أنه لا قائل بالفرق.
الشبهة الرابعة : أنه تعالى ما ذكر الرؤية في القرآن إلا وقد استعظمها. وذلك في ثلاث آيات: أولها قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذْ قُلْتُمْ يَمُوسَى لَن تُؤْمِنَ لَكَ حَتَّى نَرَى اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً فَأَخَذَتْكُمُ الصَّاعِقَةُ وَأَنتُمْ تَنظُرُونَ ) [البقرة : ٥٥] وثانيها : قوله تعالى : ويَسْتَلْكَ أَهْلُ الْكِتَابِ أَن تُنَزِّلَ عَلَيْهِم كِتَابًا مِّنَ السَّمَاءِ فَقَدْ سَأَلُوا مُوسَى أَكْبَرَ مِن ذَلِكَ فَقَالُوا أَرِنَا اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً فَأَخَذَتْهُمُ الصَّاعِقَةُ بِظُلْمِهِمْ ﴾ [النساء : ۱۵۳] وثالثها: قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَقَالَ الَّذِينَ لَا يَرْجُونَ لِقَاءَنَا لَوْلَا أُنزِلَ عَلَيْنَا الْمَلَائِكَةُ أَوْ نَرَى رَبَّنَا لَقَدِ اسْتَكْبَرُوا فِي أَنفُسِهِمْ وَعَتَوْ عُتُوًّا كَبِيرًا [الفرقان: ۲۱] وهذا الاستعظام يدل على أن رؤية الله تعالى ممتنعة.
Chapter Six: Concerning the narration of the doubts of the Mu'tazila in denying the vision (ru'ya) and the answer to them. Know that they adhere to rational arguments and to transmitted arguments. As for the transmitted doubts, they are four: The first doubt: holding fast to His statement, the Most High: 'And vision perceives Him not' [al-An'am 103]. Know that they sometimes hold fast to this verse in explaining that the Most High is not seen by anyone, and sometimes in explaining that it is impossible for anyone to see Him. As for the first aspect: its formulation is that we say: perception (idrak) attributed to sight is 'I saw him and I did not perceive him with my eye' and that one says 'I perceived him with my eye and I did not see him'—and this indicates that perception of sight and vision are one thing. If this is established, we say: the Most High negated that any of the eyes perceive Him at all times. And that necessitates that no one of the eyes see Him at all times. As for the second aspect: it is that the Most High praises Himself that nothing of the eyes perceives Him, and whatever's absence is praise, its existence is deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Allah the Most High, so it is necessary that vision be impossible for Allah the Most High. The second doubt: they held fast to His statement, the Most High, to Moses: 'You shall not see Me' [al-A'raf 143]. And this word [lan] indicates eternity, based on His statement, the Most High: 'Say: you shall not follow us' [al-Fath 15] etc. And if this is established regarding Moses, it is established regarding others due to the consensus that there is no one who makes a distinction. The third doubt: they held fast to His statement, the Most High: 'And it is not for any human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation or from behind a veil' [al-Shura 51]. This verse indicates that everyone whom Allah speaks to, he does not see Him. And if the absence of vision at the time of speech is established, the absence of vision at other than the time of speech is established by necessity, because there is no one who makes a distinction. The fourth doubt: that the Most High did not mention vision in the Qur'an except that He considered it great. And that is in three verses: the first of them is His statement, the Most High: 'And when you said, O Moses, we will not believe you until we see Allah openly, so the thunderbolt took you while you were looking' [al-Baqara 55]; the second of them is His statement, the Most High: 'The People of the Scripture ask you to bring down to them a book from the heaven. They asked Moses something greater than that, and said, Show us Allah openly, so the thunderbolt took them for their wrongdoing' [al-Nisa 153]; the third of them is His statement, the Most High: 'And those who do not expect the meeting with Us say, Why are not angels sent down to us, or why do we not see our Lord? They have certainly become arrogant within themselves and become insolent with great insolence' [al-Furqan 21]. And this considering great indicates that the vision of Allah the Most High is impossible.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents four transmitted (naqli) arguments used by the Mu'tazila to deny the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). The author, Imam al-Razi, introduces the chapter as a narration of their doubts and the responses to them, though the responses are not given in this section. He states that the Mu'tazila rely on both rational and transmitted proofs; here he lists the transmitted ones. The first doubt uses the verse 'And vision perceives Him not' (al-An'am 103). The Mu'tazila derive two aspects: (1) Since 'perception' (idrak) of the eye is identical to 'seeing' (ru'ya), the negation of perception implies negation of vision entirely, at all times. (2) The verse praises Allah by negating perception, so if perception were possible, it would be a deficiency, impossible for Allah. The second doubt invokes Allah's words to Musa, 'You shall not see Me' (al-A'raf 143). The particle 'lan' indicates eternal negation, as in another Quranic verse. Since this applies to Musa, it applies to all, as there is consensus that no one distinguishes between prophets and others in this ruling. The third doubt uses the verse that Allah only speaks to a human from behind a veil, implying that the one spoken to does not see Him; this negation of vision at the time of speech extends to all times by necessity. The fourth doubt points out that every time the Quran mentions the request to see Allah, it treats it with condemnation and punishment, indicating that such a vision is impossible. The author's presentation is expository, setting up the Mu'tazili position before refuting it.

Key Terms
رؤية
إدراك
بصر
شبهة نقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila only used rational arguments to deny ru'ya.
Correction: This passage shows they also heavily relied on Quranic verses as transmitted proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the first transmitted doubt, how do the Mu'tazila argue from the verse 'And vision perceives Him not'?
A) They argue that perception is different from vision, so the verse only negates perception.
B) They argue that perception and vision are identical, so negating perception negates vision entirely.
C) They argue that the verse is abrogated by other verses.
D) They argue that the verse only applies to disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila used the verse 'And vision perceives Him not' to argue that seeing Allah is impossible, both because perception equals vision and because its absence is a source of praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'And vision perceives Him not,' do you feel a distance from Allah? How does this verse affect your longing to see Him in the Hereafter? Are you content with just knowing Him without seeing, or does this stir a deeper desire?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that if something's absence is praised, its existence would be a deficiency. Applying this to your own life, what is something you take pride in that might actually be a deficiency if it were missing? Is your relationship with Allah based on His perfection or your own perceived strengths?
?
The Mu'tazila used a linguistic argument to deny seeing Allah. Have you ever used your own reasoning to downplay a promise of Allah, such as the vision of Him? What is the state of your heart when you trust Allah's words over your own understanding?

Second Transmitted Doubt: The Eternal Negation of 'Lan'
Razi presents the second doubt based on the verse 'You shall not see Me' and the argument from the eternal meaning of 'lan'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's second transmitted doubt: the verse 'You shall not see Me' (lan tarani) and their claim that 'lan' implies eternal negation.

Core Idea
The word 'lan' in Arabic indicates permanent negation, so 'You shall not see Me' means never.

The Mu'tazila's second scriptural argument comes from Allah's words to Prophet Musa in Surah al-A'raf. They interpret the particle 'lan' as denoting eternal negation, meaning that Musa will never see Allah. They then extend this to all people by consensus, as no one distinguishes between prophets and others in this matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the argument
Read the Arabic text and translation. Note the key phrase 'لن تراني' and the claim that 'لن' indicates eternity.
The passage cites another Quranic verse to support the eternal meaning of 'lan'.
2
Analyze the claim about 'lan'
The Mu'tazila assert that 'lan' is used for permanent negation. Reflect on how this interpretation affects the meaning of the verse.
If 'lan' is eternal, then the negation of seeing Allah applies forever.
3
Understand the extension to others
The argument then extends from Musa to all humans by consensus. Ask yourself: why is this extension valid according to them?
There is no one who claims a distinction between prophets and others regarding this ruling.

Third Transmitted Doubt: The Veil of Divine Speech
Razi presents the third doubt based on the verse about divine speech from behind a veil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's third transmitted doubt: that the verse about divine speech (al-Shura 51) implies that no one can see Allah at any time.

Core Idea
If speaking to someone excludes seeing Him, then seeing is impossible beyond that moment.

The third doubt uses the Quranic verse that Allah only speaks to humans from behind a veil. The Mu'tazila reason that since the one spoken to does not see Allah at that moment, and there is no distinction between times, seeing Allah is always impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its interpretation
Read the Arabic text and translation. Pay attention to how the Mu'tazila interpret the phrase 'min warā' hijāb' (from behind a veil).
They infer that the person being spoken to does not see Allah.
2
Understand the inference from speech to vision
The argument is that during divine speech, the recipient does not see Allah. Since there is no reason to distinguish between times, vision is negated at all times.
The key is that no distinction is made between the time of speech and other times.
3
Reflect on the logical extension
Ask yourself: how does the principle 'no one makes a distinction' strengthen the argument?
This makes the negation of vision universal and permanent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أما الشبه النقلية فأربع :
الشبهة الأولى : التمسك بقوله تعالى : ولا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] اعلم أنهم تارة يتمسكون بهذه الآية في بيان أنه تعالى لا يراه أحد، وأخرى في بيان أنه يمتنع أن يراه أحد.
أما الوجه الأول : فتقريره أن نقول : الإدراك المضاف إلى البصر هو رأيته وما أدركته بعيني وأن يقال : أدركته بعيني وما رأيته وهذا يدل على أن إدراك البصر والرؤية شيء واحد. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنه تعالى نفى أن يدركه أحد الأبصار في جميع الأوقات. وذلك يقتضي أن لا يراه أحد من الأبصار في جميع الأوقات.
وأما الوجه الثاني: فهو أنه تعالى يمدح نفسه بأنه لا يدركه شيء من الأبصار، وكل ما كان عدمه مدحا كان وجوده نقصا والنقص على الله تعالى محال فوجب أن تكون الرؤية ممتنعة على الله تعالى.
الشبهة الثانية تمسكوا بقوله تعالى لموسى : لن تراني [الأعراف : 143] وهذه الكلمة للتأبيد، بدلیل قوله تعالى : قُل لَن تَتَّبِعُونَا ﴾ [الفَتْح : ١٥] اه قط، وإذا ثبت هذا في حق موسى ثبت في حق غيره لانعقاد الإجماع على أنه لا قائل بالفرق.
الشبهة الثالثة : تمسكوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كَانَ لِبَشَرٍ أَن يُكَلِّمَهُ اللهُ إِلَّا وَحْيًّا أَوْ مِن وَرَاى حجاب [الشورى: ٥١] دلت هذه الآية على أن كل من يكلم الله تعالى، فإنه لا يراه وإذا ثبت عدم الرؤية في وقت الكلام ثبت عدم الرؤية في غير وقت الكلام ضرورة أنه لا قائل بالفرق.
الشبهة الرابعة : أنه تعالى ما ذكر الرؤية في القرآن إلا وقد استعظمها. وذلك في ثلاث آيات: أولها قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذْ قُلْتُمْ يَمُوسَى لَن تُؤْمِنَ لَكَ حَتَّى نَرَى اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً فَأَخَذَتْكُمُ الصَّاعِقَةُ وَأَنتُمْ تَنظُرُونَ ) [البقرة : ٥٥] وثانيها : قوله تعالى : ويَسْتَلْكَ أَهْلُ الْكِتَابِ أَن تُنَزِّلَ عَلَيْهِم كِتَابًا مِّنَ السَّمَاءِ فَقَدْ سَأَلُوا مُوسَى أَكْبَرَ مِن ذَلِكَ فَقَالُوا أَرِنَا اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً فَأَخَذَتْهُمُ الصَّاعِقَةُ بِظُلْمِهِمْ ﴾ [النساء : ۱۵۳] وثالثها: قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَقَالَ الَّذِينَ لَا يَرْجُونَ لِقَاءَنَا لَوْلَا أُنزِلَ عَلَيْنَا الْمَلَائِكَةُ أَوْ نَرَى رَبَّنَا لَقَدِ اسْتَكْبَرُوا فِي أَنفُسِهِمْ وَعَتَوْ عُتُوًّا كَبِيرًا [الفرقان: ۲۱] وهذا الاستعظام يدل على أن رؤية الله تعالى ممتنعة.
Chapter Six: Concerning the narration of the doubts of the Mu'tazila in denying the vision (ru'ya) and the answer to them. Know that they adhere to rational arguments and to transmitted arguments. As for the transmitted doubts, they are four: The first doubt: holding fast to His statement, the Most High: 'And vision perceives Him not' [al-An'am 103]. Know that they sometimes hold fast to this verse in explaining that the Most High is not seen by anyone, and sometimes in explaining that it is impossible for anyone to see Him. As for the first aspect: its formulation is that we say: perception (idrak) attributed to sight is 'I saw him and I did not perceive him with my eye' and that one says 'I perceived him with my eye and I did not see him'—and this indicates that perception of sight and vision are one thing. If this is established, we say: the Most High negated that any of the eyes perceive Him at all times. And that necessitates that no one of the eyes see Him at all times. As for the second aspect: it is that the Most High praises Himself that nothing of the eyes perceives Him, and whatever's absence is praise, its existence is deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Allah the Most High, so it is necessary that vision be impossible for Allah the Most High. The second doubt: they held fast to His statement, the Most High, to Moses: 'You shall not see Me' [al-A'raf 143]. And this word [lan] indicates eternity, based on His statement, the Most High: 'Say: you shall not follow us' [al-Fath 15] etc. And if this is established regarding Moses, it is established regarding others due to the consensus that there is no one who makes a distinction. The third doubt: they held fast to His statement, the Most High: 'And it is not for any human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation or from behind a veil' [al-Shura 51]. This verse indicates that everyone whom Allah speaks to, he does not see Him. And if the absence of vision at the time of speech is established, the absence of vision at other than the time of speech is established by necessity, because there is no one who makes a distinction. The fourth doubt: that the Most High did not mention vision in the Qur'an except that He considered it great. And that is in three verses: the first of them is His statement, the Most High: 'And when you said, O Moses, we will not believe you until we see Allah openly, so the thunderbolt took you while you were looking' [al-Baqara 55]; the second of them is His statement, the Most High: 'The People of the Scripture ask you to bring down to them a book from the heaven. They asked Moses something greater than that, and said, Show us Allah openly, so the thunderbolt took them for their wrongdoing' [al-Nisa 153]; the third of them is His statement, the Most High: 'And those who do not expect the meeting with Us say, Why are not angels sent down to us, or why do we not see our Lord? They have certainly become arrogant within themselves and become insolent with great insolence' [al-Furqan 21]. And this considering great indicates that the vision of Allah the Most High is impossible.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents four transmitted (naqli) arguments used by the Mu'tazila to deny the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). The author, Imam al-Razi, introduces the chapter as a narration of their doubts and the responses to them, though the responses are not given in this section. He states that the Mu'tazila rely on both rational and transmitted proofs; here he lists the transmitted ones. The first doubt uses the verse 'And vision perceives Him not' (al-An'am 103). The Mu'tazila derive two aspects: (1) Since 'perception' (idrak) of the eye is identical to 'seeing' (ru'ya), the negation of perception implies negation of vision entirely, at all times. (2) The verse praises Allah by negating perception, so if perception were possible, it would be a deficiency, impossible for Allah. The second doubt invokes Allah's words to Musa, 'You shall not see Me' (al-A'raf 143). The particle 'lan' indicates eternal negation, as in another Quranic verse. Since this applies to Musa, it applies to all, as there is consensus that no one distinguishes between prophets and others in this ruling. The third doubt uses the verse that Allah only speaks to a human from behind a veil, implying that the one spoken to does not see Him; this negation of vision at the time of speech extends to all times by necessity. The fourth doubt points out that every time the Quran mentions the request to see Allah, it treats it with condemnation and punishment, indicating that such a vision is impossible. The author's presentation is expository, setting up the Mu'tazili position before refuting it.

Key Terms
حجاب
تكليم
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse about speaking from behind a veil only applies to the specific prophets mentioned, not to all people.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argued that the verse applies to any human whom Allah speaks to, and by extension to all, since no distinction is made.

Checkpoint
According to the third doubt, why does the verse about divine speech imply that no one can see Allah?
A) Because the veil blocks vision.
B) Because the one spoken to does not see Him, and no distinction can be made for other times.
C) Because seeing would require direct speech.
D) Because the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila used the 'from behind a veil' verse to argue that vision of Allah is impossible at all times.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila used a single verse to deny the possibility of seeing Allah. Have you ever allowed one verse to weaken your conviction in a core belief? What does that reveal about the depth of your study of the Qur'an?
?
If you were challenged by someone using this very argument, would you be able to refute it? What steps will you take today to ensure your faith is not shaken by such doubts?
?
The verse speaks of Allah's speech 'from behind a veil.' How does your heart respond to the idea that the closest communion with Allah in this life still involves a veil? Does that make you long for the unveiled vision in the Hereafter? What are you doing to prepare for that moment?

Fourth Transmitted Doubt: Quranic Condemnation of Seeking Ru'ya
Razi presents the fourth doubt based on three verses where requests to see Allah are condemned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's fourth transmitted doubt: that the Quran's condemnation of requests to see Allah proves that seeing Him is impossible.

Core Idea
Every time the Quran mentions someone asking to see Allah, it recounts punishment, showing the request is impossible.

The fourth doubt surveys three Quranic passages where people demanded to see Allah and were met with divine punishment. The Mu'tazila argue that if seeing Allah were possible, such requests would not have been condemned so severely. This condemnation (isti'dhām) indicates that the vision itself is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the three Quranic verses cited
Read each verse in Arabic and translation. Notice the common theme: the request to see Allah is followed by punishment or condemnation.
The verses are from al-Baqara 55, al-Nisa 153, and al-Furqan 21.
2
Understand the concept of 'isti'dhām'
The passage says that Allah 'considered it great' (ista'dhama) every time ru'ya is mentioned. This means He treats the request as serious and repugnant.
The Mu'tazila interpret this as evidence that ru'ya is impossible.
3
Reflect on the inference of impossibility
Why does condemnation imply impossibility? Think about the logic: if something is good or possible, requesting it would not be condemned. The severity of the response suggests the thing requested is inherently impossible.
This is a key point in the Mu'tazili argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أما الشبه النقلية فأربع :

?

?

?
