Answering Two Textual Objections on Seeing Allah
Imam Razi answers two textual objections against the possibility of seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi responds to two Qur'an-based objections against the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'yah), using careful definition and analogy.

Core Idea
Textual objections to seeing Allah are answered by precise definitions and intention-based interpretation.

Imam Razi continues his defense of the possibility of seeing Allah by addressing two further textual objections from the Qur'an. The first objection cites the verse that Allah speaks only through revelation; Razi clarifies the meaning of revelation. The second objection points to divine astonishment at the request for vision; Razi argues that the astonishment was due to the requesters' obstinacy, not the impossibility of the request itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice that Imam Razi answers two separate objections.
The Arabic uses a concise style common in Kalam texts.
2
Understand the third objection and answer
Identify the third objection based on the verse about revelation. Notice how Razi defines 'wahy' as hearing speech quickly, and then argues that this definition does not imply being veiled from seeing Allah.
This is a semantic argument: the word 'wahy' does not exclude vision.
3
Understand the fourth objection and answer
Identify the fourth objection based on Allah's astonishment at the request for vision. Razi argues that the astonishment was due to the requesters' obstinacy, not because the request itself is impossible. He supports this by analogy with the request to send down angels.
The analogy shows that the same type of request can be acceptable or blameworthy depending on intention.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you can recall Razi's definition of revelation. Then reflect on the method: using precise definition and analogy to defend a theological position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة - وهي التمسك بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كَانَ لِبَشَرٍ أَن اللهُ إِلَّا وَحْيَّا [الشورى: ٥۱] - فنقول : الوحي هو أن يسمع ذلك الكلام بسرعة، وليس فيه أن يكون محجوبا عن رؤية الله تعالى أم لا؟ وأما الجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة: أن نقول لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الاستعظام لأجل أنهم طلبوا الرؤية على سبيل التعنت والعناد؟ والدليل عليه : أنه تعالى استعظم أيضا طلبهم لإنزال الملائكة. ولا نزاع في جواز ذلك، إلا أنهم لما طلبوه على سبيل العناد، استعظم الله ذلك فكذا في سؤال الرؤية.
And as for the answer to the third objection - which is the adherence to His statement, the Exalted: 'And it is not for a human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation' [Al-Shura 51] - we say: revelation is that he hears that speech quickly, and there is nothing in it that he is veiled from seeing Allah, the Exalted, or not. And as for the answer to the fourth objection: that we say why is it not permissible that that astonishment was because they requested the vision out of obstinacy and stubbornness? And the evidence for it is that the Exalted also considered their request for sending down angels as great. And there is no dispute about the permissibility of that, except that when they requested it out of stubbornness, Allah, the Exalted, considered that great. Similarly for the request of vision.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an answer to two textual objections against the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'yah). The first objection (shubah 3) cites the Qur'anic verse that Allah does not speak to a human except by revelation (wahy), implying that seeing Allah is impossible because revelation is not vision. Razi answers by defining wahy as hearing the speech quickly; this does not entail being veiled from seeing Allah or not. The second objection (shubah 4) points to the Qur'anic astonishment at the Israelites' request to see Allah, suggesting it is impossible. Razi counters that the astonishment may be because they asked obstinately and stubbornly, not because the request itself is impossible. He draws an analogy: Allah also considered the request to send down angels as great, but that is not inherently impossible; it was their stubborn intention that caused the rebuke. Thus the textual objections do not preclude the possibility of seeing Allah.

Key Terms
الشبهة
الرؤية
الوحي
التعنت
العناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'anic verse 'Allah does not speak to a human except by revelation' (42:51) proves that humans cannot see Allah because revelation is a lesser form of communication than direct vision.
Correction: Razi clarifies that revelation is simply the act of hearing speech quickly; it does not address whether one can see Allah or not. The verse does not negate ru'yah.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's answer to the third objection (based on Q. 42:51), what is his definition of 'revelation' (wahy)?
A) It is the direct vision of Allah.
B) It is hearing the speech quickly.
C) It is a state of being veiled from Allah.
D) It is an internal inspiration without sound.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes textual objections to ru'yah by defining 'revelation' as a mode of hearing, and by showing that divine astonishment at the request for vision was due to obstinacy, not impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Quranic verse that seems to contradict a belief you hold, do you rush to reject the belief, or do you, like Imam Razi, pause to define the terms precisely? Which attitude reflects more taqwa in your heart right now?
?
The Israelites asked to see Allah out of obstinacy. Think of a recent time you asked Allah for something. Was your request driven by sincere need or by a sense of entitlement or challenge? How can you transform your supplications into acts of humility?
?
Imam Razi used the analogy of the angels to show that divine astonishment does not imply impossibility. Are there things in your life that you have labeled 'impossible' simply because they seemed difficult or because others disapproved? What would it take for you to see them as possible through Allah's power?

Third Argument: The Contradiction in Obligating Abu Lahab to Believe
Razi argues that obligating Abu Lahab to believe combines belief and disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the case of Abu Lahab to expose a logical contradiction in obligating faith in someone whom God has declared will not believe.

Core Idea
Faith in God's report contradicts the required belief in that report.

Imam Razi presents a sharp logical argument against the view that God can obligate what is impossible. By taking the example of Abu Lahab, he shows that requiring him to believe while simultaneously informing that he will not believe forces a contradiction. This passage illustrates a fundamental problem in the theology of human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice Razi's concise, logical style.
2
Identify the three premises
The passage presents a three-step argument: God obligated Abu Lahab to faith, faith includes affirming God's reports, and God reported that Abu Lahab will not believe. Find each premise in the text.
3
Reflect on the contradiction
Consider why the conclusion—that Abu Lahab must believe he will not believe—is logically impossible. It requires holding two contradictory beliefs simultaneously.
This connects to the broader issue of taklif ma la yutaq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : أنه تعالى كلف أبا لهب بالإيمان، ومن جملة الإيمان تصديق الله تعالى في كل ما أخبر عنه ومما أخبر عنه : أن أبا لهب لا يؤمن فقد صار أبو لهب مكلفا بأن يؤمن بأنه لا يؤمن وهذا تكليف بالجمع بين النقيضين.
The third aspect: that He, exalted, obligated Abu Lahab to faith, and part of faith is to affirm God, exalted, in everything He informed about, and among what He informed about is that Abu Lahab will not believe. So Abu Lahab became obligated to believe that he will not believe, and this is an obligation to combine two contradictories.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (al-wajh al-thalith) against the Ash'ari position on human capacity. Imam Razi argues that God obligated Abu Lahab to have faith (iman). Part of faith is affirming the truth of God's reports. Among God's reports is the verse (surah al-Baqarah 6) that Abu Lahab will not believe. Therefore, Abu Lahab is obligated to believe that he will not believe. This results in an obligation to hold two contradictory beliefs simultaneously—belief in God's report that he will not believe, and belief in the opposite (that he will believe). This is an impossible obligation (taklif ma la yutaq).

Key Terms
تكليف
الجمع بين النقيضين
أبو لهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument only applies to Abu Lahab's unique case.
Correction: The argument illustrates a general logical principle: if God informs that someone will not believe, then obligating that person to believe would require believing a contradiction. It applies to any similar case where divine foreknowledge conflicts with obligation.

Checkpoint
What is the logical contradiction in obligating Abu Lahab to believe?
A) He is asked to believe in God and deny God simultaneously.
B) He is asked to believe that he will believe and that he will not believe at the same time.
C) He is asked to believe the impossible exists.
D) He is asked to obey without capacity.

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument demonstrates that obligating faith in someone whom God has declared will not believe leads to an impossible contradiction, thereby challenging the coherence of such an obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a moment to reflect: Is there any report from God (in the Qur'an or authentic hadith) that you find yourself resisting or finding difficult to fully affirm? How can you reconcile that with the nature of faith as described in this lesson, which requires affirming all of God's reports?
?
Think about a time when you felt a religious obligation was too burdensome or seemed impossible. Does Razi's argument about the impossibility of contradictory obligations give you any reassurance about God's justice? How does it affect your trust in His commands?
?
If faith requires affirming God's reports even when they challenge your desires or expectations, what is one specific report that you need to embrace more fully today? Write down the barrier you feel and decide on a concrete step to affirm it in your heart.

Sixth Proof that Obliging the Impossible is Necessary
Razi presents the sixth proof and answers the objection with 'abandoning stratagem'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's sixth proof that obliging the impossible is necessary, and the strategy of abandoning stratagem in response to the objection.

Core Idea
The command to know God creates a dilemma: either it targets a knower (impossible) or an ignorant (equally impossible).

Razi presents the final proof in a series demonstrating that the obligation of what is impossible is a necessary consequence for all. The proof centers on the divine command to know God, showing that whether the command is addressed to someone who already knows God or to someone who does not, it leads to an impossibility. Following the proof, Razi answers the objection of what 'stratagem' can resolve this, with a striking response.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's structure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the dilemma Razi sets up: either the command is to a knower or to an ignorant. Consider why both horns lead to impossibility.
2
Identify the conclusion
Razi concludes that with these six aspects, the obligation of the impossible is established for all. Notice he refers to his fuller discussion in Usul al-Fiqh.
3
Examine the objection and answer
Read the objection: 'What stratagem can repel this?' and Razi's reply: 'The stratagem is to abandon stratagem.' Reflect on the theological implications of this answer.
4
Synthesize the lesson
Summarize the proof and the answer in your own words. Consider how this refutes the Mu'tazilite position that God cannot oblige the impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : أنه ورد الأمر بتحصيل معرفة الله تعالى . فنقول : إما أن يقال : إنه توجه هذا الأمر على من يعرف الله، أو على من لا يعرف الله تعالى . فإن كان الأول كان هذا أمرًا بتحصيل الحاصل . وإنه محال . وإن كان الثاني كان هذا توجها لأمر الله تعالى على من لم يكن عارفا بالله تعالى والجاهل بالذات جاهل بالصفات، فإذن هذا الأمر متوجه على شخص لا يمكنه حال بقاء ذلك الأمر أن يعرف الأمر وذلك عين تكليف ما لا يطاق. فثبت بهذه الوجوه الستة أن تكليف ما لا يطاق لازم على الكل والاستقصاء في هذا الباب مذكور فيما صنفناه في أصول الفقه. فإن قيل : هب أن هذا الإشكال لازم على الكل فما الحيلة لنا ولهم في دفعه؟ قلنا : الحيلة ترك الحيلة والاعتراف بأنه يفعل ما يشاء ويحكم ما يريد، وأنه لا يُسْأَلُ عما يفعل وهم يسألون. وهذا آخر المباحث المحصلة في هذا الباب . وبالله التوفيق.
The sixth aspect: The command to acquire knowledge of God Almighty has come. So we say: Either it is said that this command is directed to one who knows God, or to one who does not know God Almighty. If it is the first, then this is a command to obtain what is already obtained, and that is impossible. If it is the second, then this is a direction of God's command to one who was not cognizant of God Almighty, and the ignorant of the Essence is ignorant of the attributes. Thus this command is directed to a person who cannot, during the existence of that command, know the command, and that is exactly the obligation of what is not possible. So it is established by these six aspects that the obligation of what is not possible is incumbent upon all. The full treatment in this chapter is mentioned in what we wrote in Usul al-Fiqh. If it is said: Grant that this problem is incumbent upon all, so what is the stratagem for us and for them to repel it? We say: The stratagem is to abandon stratagem and to acknowledge that He does what He wills and judges what He desires, and that He is not asked about what He does, while they are asked. And this is the end of the investigations in this chapter. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage contains the sixth and final proof that the obligation of what is not possible (takleef ma la yutaq) is necessary for everyone, followed by an objection and its answer. Razi begins: the command to acquire knowledge of God is given. He then presents a dilemma: either this command is directed to a person who already knows God, or to one who does not know God. If it is directed to one who already knows, then it is a command to obtain what is already possessed, which is impossible. If it is directed to one who does not know God, then that person, being ignorant of the Essence, is also ignorant of the attributes; while the command remains in effect, he cannot possibly come to know the command (i.e., know God and thus understand the command). That is the very definition of obliging the impossible. Razi concludes that with these six proofs, the doctrine that obliging the impossible is necessary for all is established. He refers to his fuller treatment in his work on jurisprudence. Then an objection is raised: 'Granted this problem is necessary for all, what stratagem do we and they have to repel it?' Razi replies: 'The stratagem is to abandon stratagem and to acknowledge that God does as He wills and judges as He wishes, that He is not asked about what He does, while they (the created) are asked.' This ends the chapter.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
الأمر بتحصيل معرفة الله
الحيلة ترك الحيلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Razi is arguing that God cannot command the impossible, thus denying divine power.
Correction: Razi is actually arguing that obliging the impossible is necessary for all, refuting the Mu'tazilite position that God cannot do so. He affirms God's absolute sovereignty.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's sixth proof, why is the command to know God impossible to fulfill for the ignorant person?
A) Because the ignorant person cannot understand the command while being ignorant of God.
B) Because the command is directed to those who already know God.
C) Because ignorance of the essence implies ignorance of the attributes, preventing obedience.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
Razi's sixth proof shows that the command to know God creates an inescapable dilemma, and the only response is to abandon stratagem and submit to God's absolute will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face commands of God that seem overwhelming or beyond your capacity, do you seek a stratagem to escape or do you submit to His will as Razi advises? What specific command in your life right now are you tempted to rationalize away?
?
Razi says the command to know God is addressed either to the knower or the ignorant. Which category describes you right now? If you claim to know God, are you truly living in pursuit of deeper knowledge, or have you become complacent? If you admit ignorance, what will you do today to respond to that command?
?
The only solution to the dilemma is to abandon stratagem and acknowledge that God does as He wills. Where in your life are you still trying to 'manage' your relationship with God through excuses, delays, or theological hedging? What would it look like to truly surrender that area to Him today?

The Need for the Atom and Four Views on Division
Razi introduces the need for the atom and classifies views on body division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why establishing the indivisible atom is necessary for discussing resurrection, and the logical categorization of views on body division.

Core Idea
The indivisible atom is the foundation for the soul and resurrection.

Before tackling the resurrection, Imam Razi lays two conceptual foundations: knowledge of the soul and proof of void. Both depend on understanding the indivisible atom. He then classifies possible views on the division of bodies into four logical positions, though only three are enumerated. This passage sets the stage for a key kalam debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the opening to see why Razi discusses the atom before resurrection. Note the two prerequisites: knowledge of the soul and proof of void.
The atom is tied to the nature of the soul.
2
Grasp the logical division
Razi divides the possible divisions of bodies by two binary criteria: actual vs. potential, and finite vs. infinite. This yields four logical categories.
This is a classic kalam method of exhaustive division (taqsīm).
3
Identify the three enumerated views
Find the three views explicitly listed: (1) body composed of finite indivisible parts (most theologians), (2) composed of actually infinite parts (al-Nazzam), (3) body is one but potentially infinitely divisible.
The fourth view is not stated; think about what it might be.
4
Reflect on the closing remark
Read the sentence about actual infinity and God's power. It clarifies that actual infinites cannot exist, which relates to the impossibility of the second view.
This remark may hint at the implicit fourth view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السابعة والعشرون
في إثبات الجوهر الفرد
اعلم أنا قبل الخوض في مسألة المعاد، نفتقر إلى إثبات أصلين : أحدهما : معرفة النفس، ومعرفة النفس محتاجة إلى معرفة الجوهر الفرد. والأصل الثاني : إثبات الخلاء. ونحن قبل الخوض في مسألة المعاد، نذكر
هذه المسائل الثلاث :
أما مسألة الجوهر الفرد :
فنقول : لا شك أن هذه الأجسام المحسوسة قابلة للانقسامات، وهذه الانقسامات الممكنة إما أن تكون موجودة بالفعل، أو لا تكون وعلى كلا التقديرين فهي إما أن تكون متناهية أو غير متناهية . فحصل من هذا التقسيم أقسام أربعة . لا
مزيد عليها :
الأول : قول من يقول : الجسم المحسوس مركب من أجزاء متناهية وكل
واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة بوجه من الوجوه وهذا قول أكثر المتكلمين. الثاني: قول من يقول : الجسم المحسوس مركب من أجزاء غير متناهية.
بالفعل. وهذا هو القول المنسوب إلى (النظام).
والثالث : قول من يقول : هذا الجسم المحسوس واحد في نفسه، كما أنه واحد في الحس، إلا أنه قابل لانقسامات غير متناهية لا أنه يقبل انقسامات بمعنى غير متناهية دفعة واحدة بل بمعنى أن الجسم لا ينتهي في الصغر إلى حد إلا ويقبل
بعد ذلك الانقسام.
►
وإن كل ما يخرج من الانقسامات إلى الفعل أبدًا فهو متناه. كما أنا نقول: إنه تعالى قادر على ما لانهاية له لا بمعنى أنه يمكن أن يخلق أشياء لا نهاية لها . فإن
ذلك محال.
The twenty-seventh issue: On proving the indivisible atom. Know that before delving into the issue of the resurrection, we need to establish two principles: one: knowledge of the soul, and knowledge of the soul requires knowledge of the indivisible atom. And the second principle: proof of void. And we, before delving into the issue of the resurrection, mention these three issues: As for the issue of the indivisible atom: we say: there is no doubt that these sensible bodies are divisible. These possible divisions are either existent in actuality or not, and in both cases they are either finite or infinite. So from this division arise four divisions, no more: The first: the saying of those who say: the sensible body is composed of finite parts, each one of which is indivisible in any way. This is the view of most theologians (al-mutakallimin). The second: the saying of those who say: the sensible body is composed of actually infinite parts. This is the view attributed to al-Nazzam. The third: the saying of those who say: this sensible body is one in itself, just as it is one in sense perception, except that it is divisible into infinite divisions, not that it admits infinite divisions all at once, but in the sense that the body does not reach a limit in smallness except that it admits division after that. ► And indeed everything that emerges from divisions into actuality is always finite. Just as we say: He (Exalted) is capable of what has no limit, not in the sense that He can create things that have no end, for that is impossible.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on proving the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد). The author explains that before discussing resurrection, two principles must be established: knowledge of the soul (which depends on knowing the atom) and proof of void. Then he focuses on the atom. He notes that bodies are divisible, and the possible divisions can be categorized by actuality vs. potentiality and finiteness vs. infiniteness, yielding four logical divisions. However, only three views are explicitly listed: (1) most theologians hold that bodies are composed of finite, indivisible parts (atomism); (2) al-Nazzam holds that bodies are composed of actually infinite parts; (3) another view holds that bodies are one in themselves but potentially infinitely divisible. The fourth division is not explicitly given; the subsequent remark about any actually existent divisions being finite may indicate a criticism of the second view or an implicit fourth position. The passage closes with an analogy: God's power over infinity does not mean He can create an actual infinite, which is impossible. The commentary follows the author's sequence: motivation, categorization, three views, and a concluding observation.

المعاد
النفس
الخلاء
الانقسامات
النظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'four divisions' implies all four are explicitly stated, but the fourth may be inferred rather than listed.
Correction: Razi claims four logical divisions but only enumerates three. The fourth may be the combination 'potential and infinite' which is actually covered by the third view? Actually, the third view is potential but infinite, so maybe the fourth is 'potential and finite'? The text is ambiguous.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the view of most theologians (akthar al-mutakallimin) regarding the composition of bodies?
A) Bodies are composed of actually infinite parts.
B) Bodies are composed of finite, indivisible parts.
C) Bodies are infinitely divisible in a continuous manner.
D) Bodies are not composite at all.

Key Takeaway
The indivisible atom is a necessary presupposition for the resurrection; the possible divisions of bodies yield four logical views, of which three are explicitly given.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

How does this passage challenge you to refine your understanding of what 'all-powerful' truly means? Commit today to studying one argument that tests your assumptions about divine power.

The Soul as Brain Spirits and Permanent Body Parts
Razi presents the ninth and tenth views on the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional views on the soul: the soul as brain spirits and as permanent body parts, and how the latter view addresses objections to bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
The soul is either brain spirits or permanent body parts that answer resurrection doubts.

Imam Razi continues his survey of definitions of the soul by presenting two more views: the ninth identifies the soul with the spirits formed in the brain, while the tenth, favored by verified theologians, identifies it with the unchanging original parts of the body. This last view has important implications for the doctrine of bodily resurrection. Let's examine each view carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Notice that Razi presents two distinct views linked by 'wa-ashiruha'.
The passage is dense; let the author's rhythm come through.
2
Understand the ninth view
Focus on the description of the soul as spirits in the brain that travel through nerves and enable sensation, movement, and thought. Ask yourself: Is this view similar to any modern conception?
3
Understand the tenth view
Notice the distinction between original parts (ajza' asliyya) and accidental parts. The soul is identified with the original parts that persist unchanged. Why does this matter?
This view is attributed to the verified theologians.
4
Connect to resurrection
Reflect on how identifying the soul with permanent body parts provides a response to deniers of bodily resurrection. The text says this view makes the answer clear.
This connects the discussion of the soul to eschatology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتاسعها : أن النفس عبارة عن الأرواح المتكونة في الدماغ الصالحة لقبول قوة الحس والحركة والحفظ والفكر النافذة من الدماغ في شظايا الأعصاب النابتة منه إلى أقاصي البدن. وعاشرها أن أجزاء هذا البدن على قسمين بعضها أجزاء أصلية باقية من أول العمر إلى آخره، من غير أن يتطرق إليها شيء من التغيرات والانحلالات والزيادات والنقصانات، وبعضها أجزاء عارضة تبعية تارة تزداد وتارة تنتقص فالنفس والشيء المشار إليه لكل واحد بقوله (أنا) إنما هو القسم الأول من الأجزاء. وإذا عرفت هذا هو أجزاء أصلية بالنسبة إلى (زيد) كانت أجزاء فاضلة بالنسبة إلى ( عمرو) وبالعكس وهذا اختيار المحققين من المتكلمين وبهذا القول يظهر الجواب عن أكثر شبهات منكري الحشر والنشر.
And the ninth of them: that the soul is the spirits generated in the brain, suitable for receiving the power of sensation, movement, memory, and thought, passing from the brain through the fragments of nerves that sprout from it to the extremities of the body. And the tenth of them: that the parts of this body are of two types: some are original parts that remain from the beginning of life to the end, without any changes, decompositions, increases, or decreases affecting them; and some are accidental, subsidiary parts that sometimes increase and sometimes decrease. So the soul and the thing pointed to by each person's saying 'I' is only the first type of parts. And if you know that these are original parts relative to Zayd, they are surplus parts relative to Amr, and vice versa. And this is the choice of the verified theologians, and by this saying the answer to most of the doubts of the deniers of resurrection and gathering becomes apparent.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents the final two views of the first section. The ninth view identifies the soul with the spirits (arwah) formed in the brain, which are capable of receiving sensory, motor, memory, and cognitive powers, and travel through nerves to the body's extremities. The tenth view is more sophisticated: it distinguishes between two types of body parts – original (asliyya) parts that persist unchanged throughout life, and accidental (arida) parts that fluctuate. The soul, the referent of 'I', is identified with the original parts. Notably, what are original parts for one person may be surplus for another. This view is attributed to the verified theologians (al-muhaqqiqun min al-mutakallimin) and is said to provide a response to most objections raised by those who deny bodily resurrection and gathering. This connects the discussion of the soul to eschatology.

Key Terms
الأرواح المتكونة في الدماغ (spirits in brain)
شظايا الأعصاب (nerve fragments)
أجزاء أصلية (original parts)
أجزاء عارضة (accidental parts)
الحشر والنشر (resurrection and gathering)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is something separate from the body and not identified with any physical parts.
Correction: According to this view, the soul is not separate but identical to the permanent, unchanging parts of the body.

Checkpoint
According to the tenth view, what is the soul?
A) The accidental parts that increase and decrease.
B) The original parts of the body that remain unchanged throughout life.
C) The subtle bodies in the heart.
D) The temperamental balance.

Key Takeaway
Two views are presented: the soul as brain spirits (ninth) and as permanent body parts (tenth), with the latter offering a solution to resurrection doubts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I', do you think of your body as a temporary container or as your very self? This lesson identifies the soul with permanent body parts. How would your daily life change if you truly believed your body is not just a vessel but the core of your identity that will be resurrected?
?
If the original parts of your body are what make you 'you' and will be resurrected, are you treating those parts with the honor they deserve? What specific habit or attitude about your body do you need to repent from today?

Three Body Theories of the Soul
Al-Razi enumerates the seventh, eighth, and ninth theories of the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three distinct physicalist theories of the soul: as temperament, as subtle bodies in the heart, and as spirits in the brain.

Core Idea
The soul has been explained as a balanced blend, a subtle substance, or a set of vital spirits.

Al-Razi continues his enumeration of theories that identify the soul with the body itself. Here he presents three specific proposals: the soul as the balanced temperament of elements and humors, as subtle bodies originating in the heart, and as spirits formed in the brain. Each theory attempts to locate the soul within the physical constitution of the human being.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage carefully to identify the three distinct views.
2
Understand the first view (temperament)
The soul is identified with the balanced mixture of elements and humors, which gives life.
3
Understand the second view (subtle bodies in heart)
The soul is a set of subtle bodies that originate in the heart and travel through arteries.
4
Understand the third view (spirits in brain)
The soul is spirits in the brain that enable sensation, movement, memory, and thought.
5
Compare the three views
Notice how each locates the soul in a different part of the body or quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسابعها : أن النفس عبارة عن المزاج الذي هو عبارة عن اعتدال الأركان والأخلاط، وذلك لأن الأركان والأخلاط إذا امتزج بعضها ببعض حصلت هناك كيفية معتدلة متوسطة - وهي المزاج - وذلك الاعتدال عبارة عن النفس، وعن الحياة. وثامنها : أن النفس عبارة عن الأجسام اللطيفة المتكونة في جانب البطن الأيسر، من القلب النافذة في الشرايين النابتة منه إلى جملة أجزاء البدن . وتاسعها : أن النفس عبارة عن الأرواح المتكونة في الدماغ الصالحة لقبول قوة الحس والحركة والحفظ والفكر النافذة من الدماغ في شظايا الأعصاب النابتة منه إلى أقاصي البدن . وعاشرها أن أجزاء هذا البدن على قسمين بعضها أجزاء أصلية باقية من أول العمر إلى آخره، من غير أن يتطرق إليها شيء من التغيرات والانحلالات والزيادات والنقصانات، وبعضها أجزاء عارضة تبعية تارة تزداد وتارة تنتقص فالنفس والشيء المشار إليه لكل واحد بقوله (أنا) إنما هو القسم الأول من الأجزاء. وإذا عرفت هذا هو أجزاء أصلية بالنسبة إلى (زيد) كانت أجزاء فاضلة بالنسبة إلى ( عمرو) وبالعكس وهذا اختيار المحققين من المتكلمين وبهذا القول يظهر الجواب عن أكثر شبهات منكري الحشر والنشر. فهذا تفصيل قول من قال النفس جسم. وأما القسم الثاني : وهو قول من قال : النفس شيء جسماني - فهذا فيه أقوال : أحدها : قول من قال : إنها عبارة عن صفة الحياة. وثانيها : قول من قال : إنها عبارة عن الشكل والتخطيط. وثالثها قول من قال : إنها عبارة عن تناسب الأركان والأخلاط. وأما القسم الثالث : - وهو قول من قال : النفس ليست بجسم ولا بجسماني - فهذا القول اختيار جمهور الفلاسفة. ومن قدماء (المعتزلة) مذهب (معمر بن عباد السلمي) ومذهب أكثر الأجناس من (الإمامية) ومن المتأخرين ( الغزالي) و (أبي القاسم الراغب).
And the seventh: that the soul (nafs) is the temperament (mizāj), which is the moderation (iʿtidāl) of the elements (arkān) and humors (akhlāṭ), because when the elements and humors mix with each other, a moderate, intermediate quality (kayfiyya muʿtadila mutawassiṭa) arises – which is the temperament – and that moderation is the soul and life. And the eighth: that the soul is the subtle bodies (ajsām laṭīfa) formed in the left side of the cavity of the heart, penetrating into the arteries that grow from it to the whole body. And the ninth: that the soul is the spirits (arwāḥ) formed in the brain, capable of receiving the power of sensation, movement, memory, and thought, penetrating from the brain through the filaments of nerves that grow from it to the farthest parts of the body. And the tenth: that the parts of this body are of two types: some are original parts (ajzāʾ aṣliyya) that remain from the beginning of life to its end, without any changes, dissolutions, increases, or decreases affecting them; and some are accidental, subsidiary parts, sometimes increasing and sometimes decreasing. So the soul and the thing indicated by each one's saying 'I' (anā) is only the first type of parts. And if you know that these are original parts relative to Zayd, they are surplus parts relative to ʿAmr, and vice versa. This is the choice of the verifying scholars (muḥaqqiqūn) among the theologians (mutakallimūn), and by this saying the answer to most of the doubts of the deniers of resurrection and gathering becomes apparent. This is the detailed account of the one who said the soul is a body. As for the second division: the one who said the soul is something corporeal (jismānī) – there are several sayings about this: One of them: the saying of those who say it is the attribute (ṣifa) of life. Second: the saying of those who say it is shape and configuration (shakl wa takhṭīṭ). Third: the saying of those who say it is the proportion (tanāsub) of the elements and humors. As for the third division: the one who said the soul is neither a body nor corporeal – this saying is the choice of the majority of the philosophers (falāsifa). And from the early Muʿtazila: the school of Maʿmar ibn ʿAbbād al-Sulamī, and the school of most groups among the Imāmiyya, and from the later ones: al-Ghazālī and Abū al-Qāsim al-Rāghib.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the different theories about the soul (nafs). Al-Razi first completes the enumeration of views within the first group (those who say the soul is a body) by listing views seven through ten. The seventh view identifies the soul with the temperament (mizāj) – the balanced quality resulting from the mixture of elements and humors. The eighth view identifies it with subtle bodies formed in the heart and traveling through arteries. The ninth view identifies it with spirits in the brain responsible for sensation, movement, memory, and thought. The tenth view, which Al-Razi attributes to the verifying theologians, holds that the soul is the set of original, unchanging parts of the body that persist from birth to death, and these parts are what a person refers to as 'I'. This view is said to refute the deniers of bodily resurrection. Al-Razi then presents the second division: those who say the soul is something corporeal (jismānī) but not a body, with three sub-views: life as an attribute, shape and configuration, and the proportion of elements and humors. Finally, he presents the third division: those who deny that the soul is either a body or corporeal, listing the philosophers, Maʿmar ibn ʿAbbād, most Imāmiyya, Ghazālī, and al-Rāghib as adherents. The passage thus provides a comprehensive taxonomy of opinions on the soul.

Key Terms
المزاج
الأجسام اللطيفة
الأرواح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the eighth view, where do the subtle bodies originate?
A) brain
B) heart
C) liver
D) stomach

Key Takeaway
Three physicalist theories locate the soul in the body's temperament, subtle cardiac bodies, or cerebral spirits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to locate your soul, where would you place it: in a balanced quality, in subtle bodies from the heart, or in spirits from the brain? Or does your belief go beyond these physical categories? How certain are you?
?
These ancient theories reduce the soul to a physical phenomenon. Does that possibility disturb you? Why or why not? What does your reaction tell you about your own conviction regarding the soul's true nature?
?
After reading this lesson, can you now articulate a clear distinction between these three physicalist views? If someone challenged you to defend the spiritual nature of the soul, which of these theories would you need to refute first, and how would you begin?

The Soul as Original Unchanging Parts
Al-Razi presents the tenth theory of the soul as the original, unchanging bodily parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theory that the soul is the set of original bodily parts that remain unchanged throughout life, and how this refutes deniers of resurrection.

Core Idea
The soul is what we call 'I' – the permanent parts that never change.

Among the theories that consider the soul a body, the tenth view stands out for its theological significance. It identifies the soul with the original parts of the body that persist from birth to death, untouched by change or decay. This view, attributed to the verifying theologians, is said to provide a refutation of those who deny bodily resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage on the tenth view carefully.
2
Understand the division of body parts
The passage divides body parts into original (permanent) and accidental (changing).
3
Identify the soul with original parts
The soul is the original parts, referred to as 'I'.
4
Appreciate the theological benefit
This view provides an answer to doubts about resurrection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the soul is the entire body including changing parts.
Correction: The tenth view distinguishes the soul as only the permanent, original parts.

Checkpoint
According to the tenth view, what does a person refer to when saying 'I'?
A) Their entire body
B) Their original permanent parts
C) Their spirit
D) Their temperament

Key Takeaway
The soul is the permanent, unchanging core of the body, providing continuity for resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your 'I' is only the unchanging parts, what parts of you—your body, memories, habits—do you cling to that will inevitably pass away? Are you willing to let go of them for the core that remains?
?
Al-Razi says this theory answers doubts about resurrection. Do you personally doubt that your physical body can be raised? What in your daily life reveals that you truly believe your core self is preserved by Allah for that Day?
?
You would not exist without these original parts that Allah has kept unchanged since your birth. How does that reality change the way you treat your body today? Are you grateful for that continuity, or do you take it for granted?

Corporeal Theories of the Soul
Al-Razi enumerates three corporeal theories of the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three views that consider the soul corporeal but not a body: as the attribute of life, as shape, or as proportion of elements.

Core Idea
Corporeal theories see the soul as a quality, shape, or harmony.

After presenting body-theories, Al-Razi moves to the second major division: those who say the soul is something corporeal (jismānī) but not a body. This includes three distinct positions: the soul as the attribute of life, as shape and configuration, or as the proportion of elements and humors. These theories attempt to define the soul in terms of qualities or relations rather than as a substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage for the second division carefully.
2
Understand the first corporeal view
The soul is the attribute of life.
3
Understand the second corporeal view
The soul is shape and configuration.
4
Understand the third corporeal view
The soul is the proportion of elements and humors.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a corporeal theory of the soul according to this passage?
A) Attribute of life
B) Shape
C) Subtle bodies
D) Proportion

Key Takeaway
Corporeal theories treat the soul as a quality or relation, not as a substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that some philosophers say the soul is merely the attribute of life—a property that vanishes when the body dies. If that were true, what remains of you after death? Do you live as if this is the case, or do you truly believe in a soul that survives? What one change would prove the latter to your own heart?
?
Consider the view that the soul is just the shape or configuration of the body. When a loved one passes away, their shape remains briefly but then decays. Does your grief reveal that you see them only as a shape, or as something more? Be honest: in your daily interactions, do you treat people as bodies or as souls?
?
The third corporeal theory says the soul is a harmony of elements and humors. If your soul is simply a balance of chemicals, then a pill could change who you are. Have you ever acted as if your spiritual state depends only on physical factors—like your mood, health, or environment—ignoring the need for inner struggle? What will you do today to assert that your soul is more than a chemical balance?

Proponents of the Non-Corporeal Soul
Al-Razi lists the adherents of the view that the soul is non-corporeal and non-body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the prominent proponents of the view that the soul is neither a body nor corporeal, including philosophers, some Muʿtazila, the Imāmiyya, al-Ghazālī, and al-Rāghib.

Core Idea
Major thinkers reject both body and corporeal theories of the soul.

The final division in Al-Razi's taxonomy covers those who deny that the soul is either a body or corporeal. This view is favored by the majority of philosophers (falāsifa), as well as by certain Muʿtazilī scholars like Maʿmar ibn ʿAbbād, most Imāmiyya (Shiʿa), and later figures such as al-Ghazālī and al-Rāghib. Al-Razi does not argue for this view here but lists its adherents.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage for the third division carefully.
2
Identify the first group
The majority of philosophers hold this view.
3
Identify the second group
Among early Muʿtazila, Maʿmar ibn ʿAbbād is mentioned.
4
Identify the third group
Most Imāmiyya (Shiʿa) also hold this view.
5
Identify the later scholars
Al-Ghazālī and al-Rāghib are mentioned as later adherents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسابعها : أن النفس عبارة عن المزاج الذي هو عبارة عن اعتدال الأركان والأخلاط، وذلك لأن الأركان والأخلاط إذا امتزج بعضها ببعض حصلت هناك كيفية معتدلة متوسطة - وهي المزاج - وذلك الاعتدال عبارة عن النفس، وعن الحياة. وثامنها : أن النفس عبارة عن الأجسام اللطيفة المتكونة في جانب البطن الأيسر، من القلب النافذة في الشرايين النابتة منه إلى جملة أجزاء البدن . وتاسعها : أن النفس عبارة عن الأرواح المتكونة في الدماغ الصالحة لقبول قوة الحس والحركة والحفظ والفكر النافذة من الدماغ في شظايا الأعصاب النابتة منه إلى أقاصي البدن . وعاشرها أن أجزاء هذا البدن على قسمين بعضها أجزاء أصلية باقية من أول العمر إلى آخره، من غير أن يتطرق إليها شيء من التغيرات والانحلالات والزيادات والنقصانات، وبعضها أجزاء عارضة تبعية تارة تزداد وتارة تنتقص فالنفس والشيء المشار إليه لكل واحد بقوله (أنا) إنما هو القسم الأول من الأجزاء. وإذا عرفت هذا هو أجزاء أصلية بالنسبة إلى (زيد) كانت أجزاء فاضلة بالنسبة إلى ( عمرو) وبالعكس وهذا اختيار المحققين من المتكلمين وبهذا القول يظهر الجواب عن أكثر شبهات منكري الحشر والنشر. فهذا تفصيل قول من قال النفس جسم. وأما القسم الثاني : وهو قول من قال : النفس شيء جسماني - فهذا فيه أقوال : أحدها : قول من قال : إنها عبارة عن صفة الحياة. وثانيها : قول من قال : إنها عبارة عن الشكل والتخطيط. وثالثها قول من قال : إنها عبارة عن تناسب الأركان والأخلاط. وأما القسم الثالث : - وهو قول من قال : النفس ليست بجسم ولا بجسماني - فهذا القول اختيار جمهور الفلاسفة. ومن قدماء (المعتزلة) مذهب (معمر بن عباد السلمي) ومذهب أكثر الأجناس من (الإمامية) ومن المتأخرين ( الغزالي) و (أبي القاسم الراغب).
And the seventh: that the soul (nafs) is the temperament (mizāj), which is the moderation (iʿtidāl) of the elements (arkān) and humors (akhlāṭ), because when the elements and humors mix with each other, a moderate, intermediate quality (kayfiyya muʿtadila mutawassiṭa) arises – which is the temperament – and that moderation is the soul and life. And the eighth: that the soul is the subtle bodies (ajsām laṭīfa) formed in the left side of the cavity of the heart, penetrating into the arteries that grow from it to the whole body. And the ninth: that the soul is the spirits (arwāḥ) formed in the brain, capable of receiving the power of sensation, movement, memory, and thought, penetrating from the brain through the filaments of nerves that grow from it to the farthest parts of the body. And the tenth: that the parts of this body are of two types: some are original parts (ajzāʾ aṣliyya) that remain from the beginning of life to its end, without any changes, dissolutions, increases, or decreases affecting them; and some are accidental, subsidiary parts, sometimes increasing and sometimes decreasing. So the soul and the thing indicated by each one's saying 'I' (anā) is only the first type of parts. And if you know that these are original parts relative to Zayd, they are surplus parts relative to ʿAmr, and vice versa. This is the choice of the verifying scholars (muḥaqqiqūn) among the theologians (mutakallimūn), and by this saying the answer to most of the doubts of the deniers of resurrection and gathering becomes apparent. This is the detailed account of the one who said the soul is a body. As for the second division: the one who said the soul is something corporeal (jismānī) – there are several sayings about this: One of them: the saying of those who say it is the attribute (ṣifa) of life. Second: the saying of those who say it is shape and configuration (shakl wa takhṭīṭ). Third: the saying of those who say it is the proportion (tanāsub) of the elements and humors. As for the third division: the one who said the soul is neither a body nor corporeal – this saying is the choice of the majority of the philosophers (falāsifa). And from the early Muʿtazila: the school of Maʿmar ibn ʿAbbād al-Sulamī, and the school of most groups among the Imāmiyya, and from the later ones: al-Ghazālī and Abū al-Qāsim al-Rāghib.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the different theories about the soul (nafs). Al-Razi first completes the enumeration of views within the first group (those who say the soul is a body) by listing views seven through ten. The seventh view identifies the soul with the temperament (mizāj) – the balanced quality resulting from the mixture of elements and humors. The eighth view identifies it with subtle bodies formed in the heart and traveling through arteries. The ninth view identifies it with spirits in the brain responsible for sensation, movement, memory, and thought. The tenth view, which Al-Razi attributes to the verifying theologians, holds that the soul is the set of original, unchanging parts of the body that persist from birth to death, and these parts are what a person refers to as 'I'. This view is said to refute the deniers of bodily resurrection. Al-Razi then presents the second division: those who say the soul is something corporeal (jismānī) but not a body, with three sub-views: life as an attribute, shape and configuration, and the proportion of elements and humors. Finally, he presents the third division: those who deny that the soul is either a body or corporeal, listing the philosophers, Maʿmar ibn ʿAbbād, most Imāmiyya, Ghazālī, and al-Rāghib as adherents. The passage thus provides a comprehensive taxonomy of opinions on the soul.

معمر بن عباد
الإمامية

Key Takeaway
The view of the soul as non-body non-corporeal is held by philosophers, some Muʿtazila, most Imāmiyya, and later scholars like al-Ghazālī.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that major scholars from diverse traditions—philosophers, Mu'tazila, Imamiyya, even al-Ghazālī—all denied that your soul is a body or physical thing. If your soul is immaterial and will outlive your body, what have you done today that truly nourishes it? Will you look back on tonight and say you fed your soul, or only your body? Commit to one concrete action before you sleep.
?
Al-Razi lists these names without comment. But these are thinkers who staked their lives on the truth of this view. Does your daily schedule reflect even a fraction of that conviction? If your soul is not a body, why do you spend almost all your energy on bodily concerns? What would change if you acted as if your soul were your real self?
?
You now know that the majority of philosophers and most Imamiyya hold the soul to be non-corporeal. But knowing this fact costs you nothing. The question is: do you actually treat your soul as something separate from your body, or do you live as if you are just a physical being? If your actions betray your belief, what will you do about it starting tomorrow?

Four Premises Proving the Knower is Not a Body
Imam Razi presents four premises to prove the knower is not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the subject of knowledge (the soul) is not a body, based on four premises about indivisibility and divisibility.

Core Idea
The knower cannot be a body because knowledge is indivisible and bodies are divisible.

In this passage, Imam Razi builds a rigorous proof from the nature of knowledge to the nature of the knower. He argues that since knowledge does not admit division, its subject must also be indivisible; and since every body is divisible, the knower cannot be a body. The proof relies on a careful analysis of how accidents can or cannot inhere in divisible subjects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the full English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice that the passage begins by recapping a previous conclusion before introducing the third and fourth premises.
2
Examine the third premise in detail
Focus on the section starting with 'وأما المقدمة الثالثة'. Razi considers four possibilities for how an indivisible accident could inhere in a divisible subject. Work through each possibility and understand why three are impossible, leaving only the conclusion that the subject itself must be indivisible.
Use the commentary and guided breakdown to track each case.
3
Understand the fourth premise and conclusion
The fourth premise states that every body is divisible (based on the denial of atoms). Combine this with the earlier premises to deduce that the knower is neither a body nor an accident in a body.
Reflect on how this proof establishes the non-physical nature of the rational soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثامنة والعشرون

وإن لم تقبل القسمة فالعلم بهذا المعلوم، هو هذه الحالة الزائدة الحادثة بسبب الاجتماع، وأنه غير قابل للقسمة . فحينئذ يحصل بما ذكرنا أن المعلوم إذا لم يقبل القسمة، كان العلم به غير قابل للقسمة.

وأما المقدمة الثالثة : - وهي أن العلم إذا لم يكن قابلا للقسمة، وجب أن يكون الموصوف به غير قابل للقسمة - فالذي يدل عليه أن كل ما كان قابلا للقسمة افترض فيه الجزءان فالفرض الحاصل فيه إما أن يكون بتمامه حاصلا في كل واحد من النصفين، أو يكون بتمامه حاصلا في أحد النصفين دون الثاني، أو يكون نصفه حاصلا في أحد نصفيه والنصف الآخر منه حاصلا في النصف الثاني من المحل، أو لا يكون شيء من ذلك.

فأما الأول : - وهو أن يحصل بتمامه في هذا النصف وبتمامه في النصف الثاني - فهذا يقتضي حصول العرض الواحد في محلين دفعة واحدة. وهو محال. وأما الثاني: - وهو أن يحصل بتمامه في أحد النصفين منه دون الثاني - فحينئذ تنقل الكلام إلى ذلك النصف، فإن ذلك النصف إن كان منقسما، عاد الكلام فيه، فيلزم أن يكون حاصلا في نصف ذلك النصف وبالجملة فكل ما يكون منقسما فإن ذلك العرض لا يكون حالا فيه وهذا يلزمه من باب عكس النقيض : أن ما يكون ذلك العرض حالا فيه فإنه لا يكون منقسما.

وأما الثالث : - وهو أن يقال بتوزع الحال على المحل - فهذا يقتضي انقسام الحال . وقد بينا أنه محال.

وأما الرابع : وهو أن لا يكون شيء من هذه الأقسام - فهذا محال لأنه إذا كان كل واحد من أجزاء المحل خاليا عن الحال بالكلية وعن جملة أجزاء الحال، كان كل واحد من أجزاء المحل خاليا عن الحال بالكلية ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، امتنع كون المحل موصوفا بالحال والعلم به ضروري فثبت أن الحال إذا كان غير منقسم، كان المحل أيضا غير منقسم.

المقدمة الرابعة : - وهي قولنا : إن كل متحيز منقسم - فهذا بناء على مسألة نفي الجوهر الفرد وقد تقدم القول فيه.

وحينئذ يلزم القطع من مجموع هذه المقدمات الأربع أن الشيء الذي هو الموصوف منا بالمعارف والعلوم موجود ليس بمتحيز ولا حال في المتحيز وهو المطلوب.
The Twenty-Eighth Issue. And if [the object of knowledge] does not accept division, then knowledge of this known is this additional state that arises due to conjunction, and that it is not divisible. Then it follows from what we have mentioned that if the known does not accept division, knowledge of it is not divisible. As for the third premise – which is that if knowledge is not divisible, then that which is described by it must not be divisible – the proof of it is that everything that is divisible, assuming it has two parts, then the accident that occurs in it is either entirely present in each of the two halves, or entirely present in one of the two halves without the other, or half of it is present in one of its halves and the other half of it is present in the second half of the subject, or none of that applies. As for the first – that it is entirely present in this half and entirely in the second half – this would entail a single accident existing in two subjects at once, which is impossible. As for the second – that it is entirely present in one of the two halves without the other – then the argument is transferred to that half; if that half is divisible, the argument recurs, leading to it being present in half of that half, and in sum, everything that is divisible, that accident does not inhere in it; and this entails, by contrapositive, that that in which the accident inheres is not divisible. As for the third – that the accident is distributed over the subject – this entails division of the accident, and we have shown that it is impossible. As for the fourth – that none of these [cases] applies – this is impossible because if each of the parts of the subject is completely devoid of the accident and of all parts of the accident, then each part of the subject is completely devoid of the accident, and when this is the case, it is impossible for the subject to be described by the accident; and knowledge of it is necessary [i.e., this is self-evident]. So it is established that if the accident is indivisible, the subject is also indivisible. The fourth premise – which is our statement: every spatially extended thing is divisible – this is based on the issue of denying the indivisible atom, which has been discussed previously. Then from the combination of these four premises, it necessarily follows that the thing in us that is described by cognitions and knowledges exists, and it is neither spatially extended nor an accident in a spatially extended thing, which is what was sought.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a proof consisting of four premises. The author first recaps that when the object of knowledge is indivisible, knowledge of it is also indivisible. He then presents the third premise: if knowledge is indivisible, then its subject (the knower) must also be indivisible. To prove this, he considers the possible ways an accident can inhere in a divisible subject: (1) entirely in each half (impossible – one accident in two places), (2) entirely in one half (leads to infinite regress if the half is divisible), (3) distributed across parts (requires accident to be divisible), or (4) none of the above (subject would be devoid of the accident). Since knowledge is indivisible, options 1–3 are impossible, so option 4 forces the conclusion that the subject must be indivisible. The fourth premise asserts that every body is divisible. Combining all premises, the subject of knowledge (the soul) cannot be a body or an accident in a body.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
قسمة
حال
عرض
متحيز
جوهر فرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the soul is divisible because it resides in a divisible body.
Correction: The proof shows that since knowledge is indivisible, its subject must be indivisible, and because bodies are divisible, the knower cannot be a body.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth premise in this argument?
A) Knowledge is indivisible.
B) Every body is divisible.
C) The subject of knowledge is not a body.
D) The object of knowledge is indivisible.

Key Takeaway
The knower is neither a body nor an accident in a body; it is an indivisible substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your true self is an indivisible knower, what does that mean for how you treat your body and your soul? Are you feeding the soul with knowledge as much as you feed the body?
?
The proof shows that knowledge cannot be divided. When you share knowledge, you give it fully without losing it. How does this change your view of teaching and learning as an act of spiritual generosity?
?
If the soul is not a body, then death is not the end of your consciousness. How does this certainty affect your fear of death and your priorities in life today?

Schools on Recreating the Nonexistent
Introduces three schools on recreating the nonexistent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three major positions on whether a nonexistent thing can be recreated exactly, and the unique Ash'ari stance that affirms it despite complete annihilation.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris alone affirm recreating the nonexistent while holding that its essence is utterly destroyed.

This passage introduces a foundational disagreement about the resurrection: can a thing that has completely ceased to exist be brought back exactly as it was? Three schools answer differently, and the Ash'ari position is particularly striking because it combines full annihilation with the possibility of recreation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Let the author's presentation of the three positions sink in before analysing.
Pay attention to the technical term 'إعادة المعدوم'.
2
Identify each school's position
List the philosophers, Mu'tazila, and Ash'aris. For each, note whether they say recreation is possible or impossible, and what reasoning they give.
Notice that the Ash'aris uniquely affirm recreation while denying any surviving essence.
3
Compare underlying assumptions
Contrast the Mu'tazili view (the nonexistent is a thing with a persistent essence) with the Ash'ari view (the nonexistent is pure nothing). How do these assumptions affect their conclusions?
The philosophers' rejection is based on a different reasoning not elaborated here.

المسألة الثلاثون
وفيه فصول :
في المعاد
أم لا ؟
الفصل الأول في إعادة المعدوم
اختلف العقلاء في أن الشيء إذا عدم وفني. فهل يمكن إعادته بعينه مرة أخرى،
أما الفلاسفة فقد اتفقوا على أنه محال . وهو قول أبي الحسين البصري) و( محمود الخوارزمي).
وأما جملة مشايخ المعتزلة : فقد اتفقوا على أن إعادة المعدوم ممكنة، إلا أنهم فرعوا هذه المسألة على مذهبهم، وذلك بأن عندهم المعدوم شيء، والشيء إذا عدم لم تبطل ذاته المخصوصة، بل زالت صفة الوجود عنه، ولما كانت ذاته المخصوصة باقية حالتي العدم والوجود، لا جرم قالوا : إعادة المعدوم جائزة.
وأما أصحابنا فإنهم يقولون : الشيء إذا عدم فقد بطلت ذاته، وصار نفيا محضا وعدما صرفا ولم تبق له حال العدم هوية ولا خصوصية. ثم إنهم مع هذا المذهب قالوا : إنه لا يمتنع في قدرة الله إعادته بعينه. وهذا القول لم يقل به أحد من طوائف العقلاء إلا أصحابنا.
The Thirtieth Question
And it contains chapters:
On the Resurrection
Or not?
The First Chapter on Recreating the Nonexistent
The rational people disagreed about whether a thing, when it ceases to exist and perishes, can be recreated exactly as it was once again.
As for the philosophers, they agreed that it is impossible. This is the saying of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and Mahmud al-Khwarizmi.
As for the group of Mu'tazilite masters, they agreed that recreating the nonexistent is possible, except that they branched this question from their doctrine, because in their view the nonexistent is a thing, and when a thing ceases to exist its specific essence does not perish, rather the attribute of existence departs from it. And since its specific essence remains in the states of nonexistence and existence, they necessarily said: recreating the nonexistent is permissible.
As for our companions (the Ash'aris), they say: when a thing ceases to exist, its essence perishes, and it becomes pure negation and sheer nonexistence, and it has no identity or particularity in the state of nonexistence. Then despite this doctrine, they said: it is not impossible for God's power to recreate it exactly. And this saying has not been uttered by any group of rational people except our companions.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage introduces the thirtieth question of the book, which concerns the resurrection, specifically the first chapter on recreating the nonexistent. The author presents three positions. The philosophers (including Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and Mahmud al-Khwarizmi) consider it impossible. The Mu'tazila consider it possible because they hold that the nonexistent is a thing whose essence remains even after nonexistence; only the attribute of existence departs. The Ash'aris (أصحابنا) maintain that when a thing ceases to exist, its essence entirely perishes, becoming pure nothingness with no identity. Nevertheless, they assert that God's power can recreate it exactly as it was. The author emphasizes that this unique position is held only by the Ash'aris among all rational thinkers.

Key Terms

الفلاسفة
المعتزلة
أصحابنا
المعدوم شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because Ash'aris say the essence perishes, they deny resurrection.
Correction: The Ash'aris affirm that God can recreate exactly, despite the essence being completely destroyed.

Checkpoint

A) It remains but without existence.
B) It perishes entirely and becomes pure nonexistence.
C) It continues in another form.
D) It becomes a potential existence.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position on recreating the nonexistent is unique: it holds that the essence perishes completely, yet God can recreate it exactly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If the philosophers say resurrection is impossible, and the Mu'tazila say it's possible because something remains, which position makes you rely more on God's absolute power? Examine your heart: do you secretly think something of you must survive for resurrection to be possible?

Objection and Initial Answer on Resurrection Possibility
Razi presents an objection and gives his initial answer that non-existence does not prevent judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that possibility depends on the essence's survival and Razi's initial rebuttal that non-existence does not prevent judgment.

Core Idea
Non-existence does not block rational judgments of possibility or impossibility.

This lesson introduces a key objection to the possibility of resurrection: if possibility is tied to an essence, does the essence's disappearance at death destroy possibility? Razi acknowledges the strength of this objection but immediately counters by arguing that the nullification (buṭlān) of the essence does not prevent us from judging it as possible or impossible. This sets the stage for the five proofs that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims that if possibility is a necessary attribute of the essence, then it must persist as long as the essence persists. Thus, when the essence perishes, possibility ends, making resurrection impossible.
This is a sophisticated objection based on the metaphysics of essences.
2
Examine Razi's initial response
Razi notes that the objection relies on the assumption that the essence remains during non-existence—a claim the opponent may not be able to sustain. He then asserts that the invalidation of the essence does not prevent judging it.
The answer is brief here; the full argument unfolds in the five proofs.
3
Identify the key shift in focus
Notice how Razi moves the discussion from a metaphysical dependence to a logical one: the judgment of possibility does not require the subject to be existent.
This is the central insight that enables the proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قولكم هذا الجواز لازم لماهيته فيبقى الجواز ببقاء الماهية). قلنا : هذا الكلام متين إلا أنه مبني على أن الماهية باقية حال العدم وهذا لا يتم إلا مع [؟] والجواب : أن بطلان الماهية حال العدم لا يمنع من الحكم عليها بالجواز والامتناع ويدل عليه وجوه : أحدها: أن صريح العقل يحكم بأن هذا المحدث كان جائز الحدوث قبل أن حدث، وهذا الذي حكم العقل عليه بالجواز ليس هو الماهية ؛ لأن الماهية لو كانت واجبة التقرر والتحقق حالتي الوجود والعدم تكون ممتنعة التغير والتبدل من حيث إنها ماهية. ويمتنع أن يكون جواز التغير والتبدل نعتا وصفة لها بل لا بد أن يكون هذا الجواز نعتا للوجود فقط أو لموصوفية الماهية بالوجود ثم إن الوجود قبل تجدده البتة ما كان حاصلا فعلمنا أن عدم حصوله وعدم عدم حصوله وعدم تحققه في نفسه، لا يمنع من الحكم عليه بالجواز. وثانيها : إن الخصم يحكم على هذا الذي عدم وفتي بالكلية بأنه يمتنع عوده والمحكوم عليه بامتناع العود ليس إلا ذلك الشخص الذي فني فعلمنا : أن فناءه صحة الحكم عليه بأنه جائز أو ممتنع. وعدمه يمنع وثالثها: أن الذي فني فهو بعد فنائه إما أن يجوز الحكم عليه بشيء من الأحكام، أو لا يجوز . فإن جاز الحكم عليه فقد سقط هذا السؤال، وإن امتنع الحكم عليه كان ذلك متناقصا، لأن تخصيصه بأنه يمتنع الحكم عليه حكم عليه بهذا الامتناع. فثبت أن القول بأنه يمتنع الحكم عليه يوجب كونه محكوما عليه. وما أدى ثبوته إلى نفيه كان باطلا، فبطل القول بأن ما فني وعدم امتنع الحكم عليه. ورابعها : أنا نحكم على شريك الباري بأنه ممتنع وعلى الجمع بين الضدين بأنه ممتنع، فالمحكوم عليه بهذا الامتناع هو شريك الله تعالى، والجمع بين الضدين ثم الماهيات ليس لها تحقق أصلا البتة باتفاق العقلاء، فعلمنا أن الحكم على الشيء لا يستدعي كون المحكوم عليه ثبوتًا. لا يقال : المحكوم عليه بالامتناع هو الصورة المستحضرة في الذهن؛ لأنا نقول : تلك الصورة لما كانت حاضرة في الذهن موجودة هناك لم يكن الحكم عليها يكونها ممتنعة الوجود ، بل المحكوم عليه بالامتناع هو وجوده في الخارج، لكن وجوده في الخارج غير ثابت أصلاً، فإن المحكوم عليه بهذا الحكم لا تحقق له البتة. وخامسها : أنا نحكم بأن الوجود والعدم لا يجتمعان وهذا الحكم على مسمى العدم بأنه ينافي الوجود ويعانده فالمحكوم عليه بهذه المعاندة والمنافاة هو مسمى العدم، ومسمى العدم يمتنع أن يكون له تحقق؛ لأنه نقيض التحقق والتقرر ، وأحد هذين النقيضين لا يكون عين الثاني.
For it is said: 'Your statement that this possibility is necessary for its essence, so possibility remains with the survival of the essence.' We said: This statement is strong except that it is based on the essence remaining during the state of non-existence, and this is only completed with [؟]. And the answer: That the invalidation of the essence during the state of non-existence does not prevent judging it as possible or impossible, and evidence for it is [the following] aspects: First: That clear reason judges that this originated thing was possible of origination before it occurred, and that which reason judged as possible is not the essence; because if the essence were necessarily established and realized in the two states of existence and non-existence, it would be impossible to change and alter inasmuch as it is an essence. And it is impossible that the possibility of change and alteration be a description and attribute for it. Rather, this possibility must be a description of existence alone, or of the essence being described by existence. Then existence before its renewal at all was not obtained. So we know that its non-obtainment, and the non-non-obtainment of it, and its non-realization in itself, does not prevent judging it as possible. Second: That the opponent judges that this thing which has perished and been completely destroyed, its return is impossible. And the object of the judgment of impossibility of return is only that particular person who perished. So we know that its perishing does not prevent[?] the correctness of judging it as possible or impossible, and its non-existence prevents[?]. Third: That which perished—after its perishing, either it is permissible to judge it with any judgment, or it is not permissible. If judging it is permissible, then this question falls away. And if judging it is impossible, then that is contradictory, because specifying it as being impossible to judge constitutes a judgment upon it with this impossibility. So it is established that the statement that it is impossible to judge it necessitates its being judged. And that whose affirmation leads to its negation is invalid. So the statement that what has perished and become non-existent cannot be judged is invalidated. Fourth: That we judge the partner of the Creator as impossible, and the combination of two opposites as impossible. So the object of judgment with this impossibility is the partner of God Almighty and the combination of two opposites. Then the essences have no realization at all according to the agreement of the rational people. So we know that judging a thing does not require the object of judgment to be existent. Let it not be said: The object of judgment of impossibility is the form present in the mind. For we say: That form, since it is present in the mind and exists there, the judgment upon it that it is impossible of existence is not [valid]; rather, the object of judgment of impossibility is its existence in the external world. But its existence in the external world is not established at all. Indeed, the object of judgment with this judgment has no realization whatsoever. Fifth: That we judge that existence and non-existence do not combine. And this judgment is upon the meaning of non-existence, that it opposes existence and contradicts it. So the object of judgment with this opposition and contradiction is the meaning of non-existence. And the meaning of non-existence cannot have realization, because it is the contradictory of realization and establishment, and one of these two contradictories is not the same as the other.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage begins with a direct objection-answer structure. An objector argues that if possibility is a necessary concomitant of an essence, then that possibility must persist as long as the essence persists; thus, if the essence disappears at death, possibility also disappears, undermining the claim that God can resurrect the non-existent. Razi's initial reply acknowledges the strength of this objection but points out that it depends on assuming that the essence remains during non-existence—a view that requires granting that the non-existent is a thing (a position the opponents may not hold). He then provides a fuller answer: the nullification (buṭlān) of the essence during non-existence does not prevent us from judging it as possible or impossible. To support this, he gives five proofs (wujūh). The first proof appeals to clear reason: reason judges that an originated thing was possible before it came into existence; this judgment is not about the essence itself (which would be unchanging) but about existence or the attribution of existence to the essence. So even when existence is absent, the judgment of possibility remains valid. The second proof notes that opponents themselves judge a perished thing as impossible to return—that judgment applies to the very thing that perished, showing that non-existence does not preclude judgment. (The text here is somewhat garbled, but the argument is clear in context.) The third proof uses a dilemma: after a thing perishes, either it can be judged or not. If it can be judged, the objection fails; if it cannot, then the very statement 'it cannot be judged' is itself a judgment, leading to contradiction. Hence, the claim that a perished thing cannot be judged is invalid. The fourth proof considers judgments of impossibility—e.g., that a partner of God is impossible—and observes that the object of such a judgment (a partner, a combination of opposites) has no real existence in the external world, yet the judgment is still sound. This shows that a judgment does not require the object to be existent. An anticipated objection—that the judgment is actually about a mental form—is refuted: the mental form exists in the mind, so it is not impossible in itself; the real impossibility concerns external existence, which is non-existent. The fifth proof examines the judgment that existence and non-existence cannot coexist: this judgment applies to the meaning (musammā) of non-existence itself. But non-existence is the opposite of realization, so it cannot have any realization. Thus, even something with no existence can be the subject of a judgment. Together, these five proofs establish that non-existence does not prevent rational judgments of possibility or impossibility, supporting the possibility of resurrection.

Key Terms
جواز (jawāz)
ماهية (māhiyya)
معدوم (maʿdūm)
بطلان (buṭlān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility must cease when the essence perishes because possibility depends on the essence's existence.
Correction: Razi shows that the judgment of possibility does not require the subject to be existent; it can be validly applied even when the essence is non-existent.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the objection described in this passage?
A) Possibility is a divine attribute that cannot be transferred.
B) Possibility is necessary for the essence, so it disappears when the essence perishes.
C) The non-existent cannot be judged because it has no properties.
D) Resurrection is impossible because God does not repeat acts.

Key Takeaway
Razi's initial answer clears the way for rational judgments about non-existents, asserting that ontological absence does not block logical predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe in resurrection, but do you truly live as if your own non-existence is no barrier to God's power? Where do you still doubt that He can restore you exactly as you were—or better?
?
Razi says that even when an essence is nullified, we can still judge it as possible. When you consider your own death, do you reduce the reality of your soul to your current existence? How can this truth liberate you from fear of annihilation?
?
If logical predication does not require existence, then your sins, thoughts, and deeds are 'present' before God even when forgotten. Are you prepared for that accounting? What change must you make today in light of this?

First Two Proofs: Judgment Before Existence and the Opponent's Own Practice
Razi gives the first two of five proofs that non-existence does not prevent judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two proofs Razi gives to show that non-existence does not prevent rational judgment: the judgment of possibility before existence and the opponent's own judgment of impossibility on a perished thing.

Core Idea
Reason judges possibility of things before they exist, and even opponents judge perished things as impossible.

Razi now presents the first two of his five proofs. The first appeals to clear reason: we judge that a thing was possible before it came into existence—this judgment concerns existence, not the essence. The second proof turns the objection on its head: the opponent himself judges that a perished person cannot return, thereby demonstrating that judgment applies to the non-existent. These proofs establish the principle that non-existence does not invalidate judgment.

Guided Path
1
Study the first proof carefully
Razi argues that reason judges an originated thing as possible before it occurs. This judgment is not about the essence (which would be unchanging) but about existence, which is not yet present. Thus, the absence of existence does not hinder the judgment.
Note the distinction between the essence itself and the attribute of existence.
2
Analyze the second proof
Razi points out that the opponent himself judges that a perished person cannot return. This judgment applies to the very person who has perished—a non-existent entity. So even the opponent's own position implies that non-existence does not prevent judgment.
There is an OCR issue in the second proof: 'وعدمه يمنع' is garbled, but the overall argument is clear.
3
Compare the two proofs
Both proofs show that judgments about non-existents are common and accepted. The first uses a universal rational intuition; the second uses the opponent's own practice as evidence.

Commentary
This passage begins with a direct objection-answer structure. (The text here is somewhat garbled, but the argument is clear in context.) The third proof uses a dilemma: after a thing perishes, either it can be judged or not. What does the first proof use to show that non-existence does not prevent judgment?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
