In your own heart, do you treat the reality of your own death and what follows as a mere idea or as a certain reality that you are already making judgments about? How can you align your daily choices with that judgment?

If your essence is fixed, but your existence is where change happens, what does that mean for your accountability before God? Are you focusing on polishing your essence or on what your existence actually does?

Proofs Three to Five: Dilemma, Impossibility, and the Meaning of Non-Existence
Razi gives the third, fourth, and fifth proofs that non-existence does not prevent judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third, fourth, and fifth proofs that non-existence does not prevent judgment: the dilemma of judging a perished thing, the judgment of impossible entities like a partner of God, and the judgment that non-existence itself opposes existence.

Core Idea
Even impossible and non-existent entities can be subjects of rational judgments.

Razi completes his argument with three more proofs. The third proof uses a dilemma: either a perished thing can be judged or not, both cases lead to the conclusion that judgment applies. The fourth proof considers judgments of impossibility (e.g., a partner of God) whose objects have no external existence, yet the judgment stands. The fifth proof examines the judgment that existence and non-existence cannot coexist, which refers to non-existence itself—something with no realization. Together, these proofs firmly establish that rational predication does not require existential presence.

Guided Path
1
Work through the third proof's dilemma
Razi presents a dilemma: after a thing perishes, either it can be judged or not. If it can be judged, the objection fails. If it cannot be judged, then the claim 'it cannot be judged' is itself a judgment, leading to contradiction. Thus, the claim that judgment is impossible is self-defeating.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the fourth proof's example
Razi notes that we judge 'a partner of God' as impossible. Yet a partner of God has no existence at all. This shows that judgment does not require the subject to exist. He also refutes the counterargument that we are judging a mental form: the mental form exists, so the real impossibility concerns external existence.
This proof uses common theological examples.
3
Examine the fifth proof's focus on non-existence itself
We judge that existence and non-existence cannot coexist. This judgment is about the meaning (musammā) of non-existence. But non-existence is the opposite of realization, so it cannot have any realization. Thus, even something with no being can be a logical subject.
This proof goes to the heart of the matter: non-existence as a concept can be judged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يمنع من الحكم عليها بالجواز والامتناع ويدل عليه وجوه : أحدها: أن صريح العقل يحكم بأن هذا المحدث كان جائز الحدوث قبل أن حدث، وهذا الذي حكم العقل عليه بالجواز ليس هو الماهية ؛ لأن الماهية لو كانت واجبة التقرر والتحقق حالتي الوجود والعدم تكون ممتنعة التغير والتبدل من حيث إنها ماهية. ويمتنع أن يكون جواز التغير والتبدل نعتا وصفة لها بل لا بد أن يكون هذا الجواز نعتا للوجود فقط أو لموصوفية الماهية بالوجود ثم إن الوجود قبل تجدده البتة ما كان حاصلا فعلمنا أن عدم حصوله وعدم عدم حصوله وعدم تحققه في نفسه، لا يمنع من الحكم عليه بالجواز. وثانيها : إن الخصم يحكم على هذا الذي عدم وفتي بالكلية بأنه يمتنع عوده والمحكوم عليه بامتناع العود ليس إلا ذلك الشخص الذي فني فعلمنا : أن فناءه صحة الحكم عليه بأنه جائز أو ممتنع. وعدمه يمنع وثالثها: أن الذي فني فهو بعد فنائه إما أن يجوز الحكم عليه بشيء من الأحكام، أو لا يجوز . فإن جاز الحكم عليه فقد سقط هذا السؤال، وإن امتنع الحكم عليه كان ذلك متناقصا، لأن تخصيصه بأنه يمتنع الحكم عليه حكم عليه بهذا الامتناع. فثبت أن القول بأنه يمتنع الحكم عليه يوجب كونه محكوما عليه. وما أدى ثبوته إلى نفيه كان باطلا، فبطل القول بأن ما فني وعدم امتنع الحكم عليه. ورابعها : أنا نحكم على شريك الباري بأنه ممتنع وعلى الجمع بين الضدين بأنه ممتنع، فالمحكوم عليه بهذا الامتناع هو شريك الله تعالى، والجمع بين الضدين ثم الماهيات ليس لها تحقق أصلا البتة باتفاق العقلاء، فعلمنا أن الحكم على الشيء لا يستدعي كون المحكوم عليه ثبوتًا. لا يقال : المحكوم عليه بالامتناع هو الصورة المستحضرة في الذهن؛ لأنا نقول : تلك الصورة لما كانت حاضرة في الذهن موجودة هناك لم يكن الحكم عليها يكونها ممتنعة الوجود ، بل المحكوم عليه بالامتناع هو وجوده في الخارج، لكن وجوده في الخارج غير ثابت أصلاً، فإن المحكوم عليه بهذا الحكم لا تحقق له البتة. وخامسها : أنا نحكم بأن الوجود والعدم لا يجتمعان وهذا الحكم على مسمى العدم بأنه ينافي الوجود ويعانده فالمحكوم عليه بهذه المعاندة والمنافاة هو مسمى العدم، ومسمى العدم يمتنع أن يكون له تحقق؛ لأنه نقيض التحقق والتقرر ، وأحد هذين النقيضين لا يكون عين الثاني.
For it is said: 'Your statement that this possibility is necessary for its essence, so possibility remains with the survival of the essence.' We said: This statement is strong except that it is based on the essence remaining during the state of non-existence, and this is only completed with [؟]. And the answer: That the invalidation of the essence during the state of non-existence does not prevent judging it as possible or impossible, and evidence for it is [the following] aspects: First: That clear reason judges that this originated thing was possible of origination before it occurred, and that which reason judged as possible is not the essence; because if the essence were necessarily established and realized in the two states of existence and non-existence, it would be impossible to change and alter inasmuch as it is an essence. And it is impossible that the possibility of change and alteration be a description and attribute for it. Rather, this possibility must be a description of existence alone, or of the essence being described by existence. Then existence before its renewal at all was not obtained. So we know that its non-obtainment, and the non-non-obtainment of it, and its non-realization in itself, does not prevent judging it as possible. Second: That the opponent judges that this thing which has perished and been completely destroyed, its return is impossible. And the object of the judgment of impossibility of return is only that particular person who perished. So we know that its perishing does not prevent[?] the correctness of judging it as possible or impossible, and its non-existence prevents[?]. Third: That which perished—after its perishing, either it is permissible to judge it with any judgment, or it is not permissible. If judging it is permissible, then this question falls away. And if judging it is impossible, then that is contradictory, because specifying it as being impossible to judge constitutes a judgment upon it with this impossibility. So it is established that the statement that it is impossible to judge it necessitates its being judged. And that whose affirmation leads to its negation is invalid. So the statement that what has perished and become non-existent cannot be judged is invalidated. Fourth: That we judge the partner of the Creator as impossible, and the combination of two opposites as impossible. So the object of judgment with this impossibility is the partner of God Almighty and the combination of two opposites. Then the essences have no realization at all according to the agreement of the rational people. So we know that judging a thing does not require the object of judgment to be existent. Let it not be said: The object of judgment of impossibility is the form present in the mind. For we say: That form, since it is present in the mind and exists there, the judgment upon it that it is impossible of existence is not [valid]; rather, the object of judgment of impossibility is its existence in the external world. But its existence in the external world is not established at all. Indeed, the object of judgment with this judgment has no realization whatsoever. Fifth: That we judge that existence and non-existence do not combine. And this judgment is upon the meaning of non-existence, that it opposes existence and contradicts it. So the object of judgment with this opposition and contradiction is the meaning of non-existence. And the meaning of non-existence cannot have realization, because it is the contradictory of realization and establishment, and one of these two contradictories is not the same as the other.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage begins with a direct objection-answer structure. An objector argues that if possibility is a necessary concomitant of an essence, then that possibility must persist as long as the essence persists; thus, if the essence disappears at death, possibility also disappears, undermining the claim that God can resurrect the non-existent. Razi's initial reply acknowledges the strength of this objection but points out that it depends on assuming that the essence remains during non-existence—a view that requires granting that the non-existent is a thing (a position the opponents may not hold). He then provides a fuller answer: the nullification (buṭlān) of the essence during non-existence does not prevent us from judging it as possible or impossible. To support this, he gives five proofs (wujūh). The first proof appeals to clear reason: reason judges that an originated thing was possible before it came into existence; this judgment is not about the essence itself (which would be unchanging) but about existence or the attribution of existence to the essence. So even when existence is absent, the judgment of possibility remains valid. The second proof notes that opponents themselves judge a perished thing as impossible to return—that judgment applies to the very thing that perished, showing that non-existence does not preclude judgment. (The text here is somewhat garbled, but the argument is clear in context.) The third proof uses a dilemma: after a thing perishes, either it can be judged or not. If it can be judged, the objection fails; if it cannot, then the very statement 'it cannot be judged' is itself a judgment, leading to contradiction. Hence, the claim that a perished thing cannot be judged is invalid. The fourth proof considers judgments of impossibility—e.g., that a partner of God is impossible—and observes that the object of such a judgment (a partner, a combination of opposites) has no real existence in the external world, yet the judgment is still sound. This shows that a judgment does not require the object to be existent. An anticipated objection—that the judgment is actually about a mental form—is refuted: the mental form exists in the mind, so it is not impossible in itself; the real impossibility concerns external existence, which is non-existent. The fifth proof examines the judgment that existence and non-existence cannot coexist: this judgment applies to the meaning (musammā) of non-existence itself. But non-existence is the opposite of realization, so it cannot have any realization. Thus, even something with no existence can be the subject of a judgment. Together, these five proofs establish that non-existence does not prevent rational judgments of possibility or impossibility, supporting the possibility of resurrection.

Key Terms
امتناع (imtināʿ)
معدوم (maʿdūm)
بطلان (buṭlān)
وجود (wujūd)
عدم (ʿadam)
مسمى (musammā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Judgments of impossibility (like 'a partner of God is impossible') are about mental concepts, not about the non-existent.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the mental form exists in the mind, so the judgment of impossibility is actually about the external existence, which is non-existent.

Checkpoint
In the fifth proof, what is the object of the judgment that existence and non-existence do not combine?
A) The essence of the thing that perished.
B) The mental form of non-existence.
C) The meaning (musammā) of non-existence.
D) The existence of the opposite.

Key Takeaway
These three proofs together establish that non-existence does not obstruct rational judgment; even the most non-existent entities can be logical subjects of possibility and impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often judge yourself as 'unworthy' or 'a failure' – but do you realize that such a judgment does not require you to be ontologically fixed in that state? Can you separate the judgment from the reality, and instead judge yourself as capable of change, just as Razi shows judgment applies even to what is non-existent?
?
This lesson proves that impossibility can be truly judged without the object existing. Now, think of a situation in your life where you have labeled something as 'impossible' – a rekindled relationship, a career turn, repentance for a repeat sin. Have you been using 'impossible' as a shield to avoid submission to Allah's power, forgetting that He creates from nothing?
?
Razi argues that even the meaning of non-existence can be a subject of judgment. How many things in your life do you treat as 'non-existent' – your true intentions, your hidden faults, your neglected duties? Will you now bring them to the light of judgment and honest introspection, affirming that they have no reality except in your negligence?

The First Argument: Loss of Identity
First argument: identity condition for resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why opponents argue that resurrecting a non-existent thing is impossible because the thing loses its identity upon non-existence.

Core Idea
What has no identity cannot be restored.

The first argument targets the very possibility of restoring something that has ceased to exist. It claims that for resurrection to be meaningful, the thing must retain a specific identity—but after non-existence, there is nothing to identify. Let's examine this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical structure: a condition, a fact, and a conclusion.
The argument is concise—each word matters.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on 'takhakhkhuṣ' (specification) and 'tashakhkhuṣ' (individuation). Why are they necessary for a judgment of possible return?
3
Trace the logical flow
The argument says: for resurrection to be possible, X must be identical to itself. But after non-existence, X is pure negation with no specification. Therefore, the judgment is invalid. Reflect on this reasoning.
Is 'pure negation' a tenable description of a non-existent thing?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج من أنكر جواز إعادة المعدوم بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن الحكم على الشيء بأنه يجوز إعادته متوقف على كون ذلك الشيء هو هو، أعني على كونه متعينًا في نفسه متخصصا في ذاته، والشيء بعد عدمه نفي محض، وليس له تخصيص ولا تشخص، فكان الحكم عليه بجواز الإعادة باطلا. الحجة الثانية : أنه لو كانت إعادة المعدوم جائزة لكانت إعادة الوقت الذي أحدثه الله تعالى فيه أولا ،جائزة، وبتقدير أن يعيد الله تعالى الوقت الأول، ويعيده فيه كانت هذه الإعادة حدوثًا في وقته الأول، فيلزم أن يقال: إنه من حيث هو معاد ليس بمعاد، بل مبدأ، وذلك جمع بين النقيضين، وهو محال. الحجة الثالثة : إذا فرضنا أن جوهرًا قد عدم ثم فرضنا أن الله تعالى أعاده وفرضن أنه تعالى أحدث جوهرًا آخر فنسبة هذين الجوهرين إلى ذلك الذي عدم أولاً نسبة واحدة، فلم يكن كون أحد هذين الجوهرين عين ذلك الذي عدم، والآخر مثله أولى من العكس، فيلزم إما القول بأن كل واحد منهما هو عين ذلك الشيء الذي عدم، فيلزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد شيئين، وهو محال. وأما القول بأنه ليس كل واحد منهما عين ذلك الذي عدم، بل كان كل واحد منهما مغايرا له ومثلا له فذلك هو الحق، وهو يمنع من جواز إعادة المعدوم. ولا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن أحدهما أولى بأن يكون عين الذي عدم وذلك لأن هذا عين ما كان والآخر مثله فكان أحدهما أولى بأن يكون عين ما كان؟ لأنا نقول : إن هذين الجوهرين اللذين حدثا مثلان من جميع الوجوه، فكانت نسبة كل واحد منهما إلى الذي عدم كنسبة الآخر إليه، وإذا كانا كذلك لم يكن كون
And one who denied the possibility of resurrecting the non-existent argued with [the following] arguments: The first argument: That the judgment upon a thing that its return is possible depends on that thing being identical [to itself], that is, on its being determined in itself and specified in its essence. And the thing after its non-existence is pure negation, and it has no specification nor individuation. So the judgment upon it of the possibility of return is invalid. The second argument: That if the resurrection of the non-existent were possible, then the resurrection of the time in which God Almighty first originated it would be possible. And assuming that God Almighty restores the first time and restores it in it, then this restoration would be an origination in its first time. So it would necessarily be said: It, inasmuch as it is returned, is not returned but rather a beginning. And that is a combination of two opposites, and it is impossible. The third argument: If we assume that a substance has perished, then we assume that God Almighty returns it, and we assume that He Almighty originates another substance, then the relation of these two substances to that which perished first is one relation. So it would not be the case that one of these two substances is identical to that which perished and the other is like it, rather than the opposite. So it necessarily follows either the statement that each of them is identical to that thing which perished, which necessitates that one thing be two things, which is impossible; or the statement that each of them is not identical to that which perished, but rather each of them is other than it and like it—that is the truth, and it prevents the possibility of resurrecting the non-existent. And let it not be said: Why is it not permissible to say that one of them is more worthy of being identical to that which perished, because this one is exactly what it was and the other is like it, so one of them is more worthy of being identical to what it was? For we say: These two substances that came into existence are similar in all respects, so the relation of each of them to that which perished is like the relation of the other to it. And when they are like that, it is not that...
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage introduces three arguments (ḥujaj) from opponents who deny the possibility of resurrecting the non-existent (iʿādat al-maʿdūm). They are presented without Razi's immediate refutation within this window. The first argument concerns identity: for something to be restored, it must retain its specific identity (taʿayyun and takhaṣṣuṣ). But after non-existence, the thing is pure negation (nafy maḥḍ), with no individuation. Hence, a judgment of possible return cannot apply, because there is no identifiable subject to be restored. The second argument raises a temporal paradox: if revival is possible, then reviving the original time of creation is also possible. But if God restores the thing in its original time, then the event would be both a return (iʿāda) and a first origination (ḥudūth awwal) simultaneously—a contradiction, since a return presupposes a prior existence distinct from the original. Thus, the very concept of reviving a non-existent leads to an impossible conjunction of opposites. The third argument is a problem of indiscernible duplicates. Suppose a substance perishes, and then God creates two new substances, one allegedly the original and the other a duplicate. Both new substances are identical in every respect. There is no criterion to identify which one is the original. If both are claimed to be the original, one thing becomes two, which is absurd. If neither is the original, then the original is not restored at all, only a mere likeness—which does not constitute true resurrection. The opponent then anticipates a possible rebuttal: perhaps one is designated as the original based on some external criterion? But since the two substances are perfectly similar in every respect, their relation to the original is identical, so no preference can be made. The argument cuts off at 'لم يكن كون', but the intention is clear: the identification fails, making genuine resurrection impossible. These three arguments pose serious philosophical challenges to the doctrine of bodily resurrection based on rational considerations of identity, time, and individuation.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم (iʿādat al-maʿdūm)
تخصيص (takhaṣṣuṣ)
تشخص (tashakhkhuṣ)
نفي محض (nafy maḥḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the argument denies resurrection outright without reason.
Correction: The argument provides a specific logical reason: without identity, the subject of resurrection disappears.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what condition must hold for a thing to be judged possible to return?
A) The thing must be possible in itself.
B) The thing must be identical to itself, determined and specified.
C) The thing must have existed in the past.
D) The thing must be known to God.

Key Takeaway
The first argument denies resurrection on the grounds that a non-existent thing lacks the identity required for restoration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument claims that what has no identity cannot be restored. Yet the Quran affirms bodily resurrection. How does this tension force you to re-evaluate your reliance on human reasoning over divine revelation? In what specific area of belief are you tempted to let logic overrule the Quran?
?
If your identity is entirely erased at death, what part of you do you believe God will raise? Does your daily life reflect a trust in God's ability to restore you completely, or do you subconsciously think some things are beyond even His power to bring back?
?
The argument rests on the idea that after non-existence, a thing is 'pure negation'. How does this challenge your understanding of the soul's survival? Is your soul something that keeps its identity after death, or do you rely solely on God's promise? What one change can you make today to anchor your hope more firmly in that promise?

The Second Argument: Temporal Paradox
Second argument: temporal paradox in resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the temporal paradox argument that resurrecting a thing in its original time leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Resurrection in the original time would make it both a return and a first beginning.

The second argument introduces a subtle temporal problem. If resurrection is possible, then it should be possible to return the original time along with the thing. But that creates an impossible situation. Let's unpack this.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional statement and the paradoxical conclusion.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the time element
Why does the arguer bring in the 'time' (waqt) in which the thing was first created? How does that create a contradiction?
Consider that time is itself created.
3
Identify the contradiction
If God restores the thing in its original time, then from the perspective of the original time, this restoration is actually the first occurrence—so it is not a return but a beginning. Hence, the same event is both return and not-return—a contradiction.
This is a classic temporal identity problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج من أنكر جواز إعادة المعدوم بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن الحكم على الشيء بأنه يجوز إعادته متوقف على كون ذلك الشيء هو هو، أعني على كونه متعينًا في نفسه متخصصا في ذاته، والشيء بعد عدمه نفي محض، وليس له تخصيص ولا تشخص، فكان الحكم عليه بجواز الإعادة باطلا. الحجة الثانية : أنه لو كانت إعادة المعدوم جائزة لكانت إعادة الوقت الذي أحدثه الله تعالى فيه أولا ،جائزة، وبتقدير أن يعيد الله تعالى الوقت الأول، ويعيده فيه كانت هذه الإعادة حدوثًا في وقته الأول، فيلزم أن يقال: إنه من حيث هو معاد ليس بمعاد، بل مبدأ، وذلك جمع بين النقيضين، وهو محال. الحجة الثالثة : إذا فرضنا أن جوهرًا قد عدم ثم فرضنا أن الله تعالى أعاده وفرضن أنه تعالى أحدث جوهرًا آخر فنسبة هذين الجوهرين إلى ذلك الذي عدم أولاً نسبة واحدة، فلم يكن كون أحد هذين الجوهرين عين ذلك الذي عدم، والآخر مثله أولى من العكس، فيلزم إما القول بأن كل واحد منهما هو عين ذلك الشيء الذي عدم، فيلزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد شيئين، وهو محال. وأما القول بأنه ليس كل واحد منهما عين ذلك الذي عدم، بل كان كل واحد منهما مغايرا له ومثلا له فذلك هو الحق، وهو يمنع من جواز إعادة المعدوم. ولا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن أحدهما أولى بأن يكون عين الذي عدم وذلك لأن هذا عين ما كان والآخر مثله فكان أحدهما أولى بأن يكون عين ما كان؟ لأنا نقول : إن هذين الجوهرين اللذين حدثا مثلان من جميع الوجوه، فكانت نسبة كل واحد منهما إلى الذي عدم كنسبة الآخر إليه، وإذا كانا كذلك لم يكن كون
And one who denied the possibility of resurrecting the non-existent argued with [the following] arguments: The first argument: That the judgment upon a thing that its return is possible depends on that thing being identical [to itself], that is, on its being determined in itself and specified in its essence. And the thing after its non-existence is pure negation, and it has no specification nor individuation. So the judgment upon it of the possibility of return is invalid. The second argument: That if the resurrection of the non-existent were possible, then the resurrection of the time in which God Almighty first originated it would be possible. Suppose a substance perishes, and then God creates two new substances, one allegedly the original and the other a duplicate. Both new substances are identical in every respect. There is no criterion to identify which one is the original. If both are claimed to be the original, one thing becomes two, which is absurd. If neither is the original, then the original is not restored at all, only a mere likeness—which does not constitute true resurrection. The opponent then anticipates a possible rebuttal: perhaps one is designated as the original based on some external criterion? But since the two substances are perfectly similar in every respect, their relation to the original is identical, so no preference can be made. The argument cuts off at 'لم يكن كون', but the intention is clear: the identification fails, making genuine resurrection impossible. These three arguments pose serious philosophical challenges to the doctrine of bodily resurrection based on rational considerations of identity, time, and individuation.

Key Terms
وقت (waqt)
إعادة (iʿāda)
حدوث (ḥudūth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument is about God's inability to create time again.
Correction: The argument is logical, not about power: it shows that resurrecting the time leads to a conceptual contradiction.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the second argument highlight?
A) The thing cannot be both created and uncreated.
B) The restoration would be both a return and a first beginning.
C) The original time cannot be restored because it has passed.
D) The thing and its time are inseparable.

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that resurrecting a thing in its original time turns the event into a first occurrence, not a return.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that calling the resurrection a 'return' leads to contradiction. If you have ever thought of your own future resurrection as merely returning to a past state, does this challenge you to rethink what it means to be brought back by God? Will you trust in His power to create anew rather than cling to your limited concept of 'return'?
?
The argument relies on the principle that two opposites cannot be combined. In your daily life, are there beliefs or actions that try to hold together contradictions—like claiming to trust God while living in anxiety about death? How does this logical clarity push you to resolve such inconsistencies for the sake of your faith?

The Third Argument: Problem of Duplicates
Third argument: duplicate substances and identity criterion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the problem of indiscernible duplicates: if two identical substances are created after the first perishes, there is no way to identify which is the original, making true resurrection impossible.

Core Idea
Perfect duplicates make resurrection indistinguishable from new creation.

The third argument presents a puzzle about identity: if a substance perishes and then two exactly similar substances appear, which one is the resurrection? The arguer shows there is no rational way to decide, so resurrection cannot be verified. Let's follow this intricate reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the scenario: one original substance, two new ones, both identical.
The argument anticipates a rebuttal and answers it.
2
Understand the symmetry
Why are the two new substances 'similar in all respects'? What does that imply about their relation to the original?
The argument says there is no basis to favor one as the original.
3
Follow the dilemma
The arguer presents two options: either both are identical to the original (impossible, one thing becomes two) or neither is identical (then neither is a true return). The opponent suggests a third option: one is more worthy. The arguer rejects this because the two are symmetrical.
This is a classic problem of identity across time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج من أنكر جواز إعادة المعدوم بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن الحكم على الشيء بأنه يجوز إعادته متوقف على كون ذلك الشيء هو هو، أعني على كونه متعينًا في نفسه متخصصا في ذاته، والشيء بعد عدمه نفي محض، وليس له تخصيص ولا تشخص، فكان الحكم عليه بجواز الإعادة باطلا. الحجة الثانية : أنه لو كانت إعادة المعدوم جائزة لكانت إعادة الوقت الذي أحدثه الله تعالى فيه أولا ،جائزة، وبتقدير أن يعيد الله تعالى الوقت الأول، ويعيده فيه كانت هذه الإعادة حدوثًا في وقته الأول، فيلزم أن يقال: إنه من حيث هو معاد ليس بمعاد، بل مبدأ، وذلك جمع بين النقيضين، وهو محال. الحجة الثالثة : إذا فرضنا أن جوهرًا قد عدم ثم فرضنا أن الله تعالى أعاده وفرضن أنه تعالى أحدث جوهرًا آخر فنسبة هذين الجوهرين إلى ذلك الذي عدم أولاً نسبة واحدة، فلم يكن كون أحد هذين الجوهرين عين ذلك الذي عدم، والآخر مثله أولى من العكس، فيلزم إما القول بأن كل واحد منهما هو عين ذلك الشيء الذي عدم، فيلزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد شيئين، وهو محال. وأما القول بأنه ليس كل واحد منهما عين ذلك الذي عدم، بل كان كل واحد منهما مغايرا له ومثلا له فذلك هو الحق، وهو يمنع من جواز إعادة المعدوم. ولا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن أحدهما أولى بأن يكون عين الذي عدم وذلك لأن هذا عين ما كان والآخر مثله فكان أحدهما أولى بأن يكون عين ما كان؟ لأنا نقول : إن هذين الجوهرين اللذين حدثا مثلان من جميع الوجوه، فكانت نسبة كل واحد منهما إلى الذي عدم كنسبة الآخر إليه، وإذا كانا كذلك لم يكن كون
And one who denied the possibility of resurrecting the non-existent argued with [the following] arguments: The first argument: That the judgment upon a thing that its return is possible depends on that thing being identical [to itself], that is, on its being determined in itself and specified in its essence. And the thing after its non-existence is pure negation, and it has no specification nor individuation. So the judgment upon it of the possibility of return is invalid. The second argument: That if the resurrection of the non-existent were possible, then the resurrection of the time in which God Almighty first originated it would be possible. And assuming that God Almighty restores the first time and restores it in it, then this restoration would be an origination in its first time. So it would necessarily be said: It, inasmuch as it is returned, is not returned but rather a beginning. And that is a combination of two opposites, and it is impossible. The third argument: If we assume that a substance has perished, then we assume that God Almighty returns it, and we assume that He Almighty originates another substance, then the relation of these two substances to that which perished first is one relation. So it would not be the case that one of these two substances is identical to that which perished and the other is like it, rather than the opposite. So it necessarily follows either the statement that each of them is identical to that thing which perished, which necessitates that one thing be two things, which is impossible; or the statement that each of them is not identical to that which perished, but rather each of them is other than it and like it—that is the truth, and it prevents the possibility of resurrecting the non-existent. And let it not be said: Why is it not permissible to say that one of them is more worthy of being identical to that which perished, because this one is exactly what it was and the other is like it, so one of them is more worthy of being identical to what it was? For we say: These two substances that came into existence are similar in all respects, so the relation of each of them to that which perished is like the relation of the other to it. And when they are like that, it is not that...
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage introduces three arguments (ḥujaj) from opponents who deny the possibility of resurrecting the non-existent (iʿādat al-maʿdūm). They are presented without Razi's immediate refutation within this window. The first argument concerns identity: for something to be restored, it must retain its specific identity (taʿayyun and takhaṣṣuṣ). But after non-existence, the thing is pure negation (nafy maḥḍ), with no individuation. Hence, a judgment of possible return cannot apply, because there is no identifiable subject to be restored. The second argument raises a temporal paradox: if revival is possible, then reviving the original time of creation is also possible. But if God restores the thing in its original time, then the event would be both a return (iʿāda) and a first origination (ḥudūth awwal) simultaneously—a contradiction, since a return presupposes a prior existence distinct from the original. Thus, the very concept of reviving a non-existent leads to an impossible conjunction of opposites. The third argument is a problem of indiscernible duplicates. Suppose a substance perishes, and then God creates two new substances, one allegedly the original and the other a duplicate. Both new substances are identical in every respect. There is no criterion to identify which one is the original. If both are claimed to be the original, one thing becomes two, which is absurd. If neither is the original, then the original is not restored at all, only a mere likeness—which does not constitute true resurrection. The opponent then anticipates a possible rebuttal: perhaps one is designated as the original based on some external criterion? But since the two substances are perfectly similar in every respect, their relation to the original is identical, so no preference can be made. The argument cuts off at 'لم يكن كون', but the intention is clear: the identification fails, making genuine resurrection impossible. These three arguments pose serious philosophical challenges to the doctrine of bodily resurrection based on rational considerations of identity, time, and individuation.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
مثل (mithl)
عين (ʿayn)
تشخص (tashakhkhuṣ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God can simply designate one duplicate as the original, making resurrection possible.
Correction: The argument shows that designation requires a criterion; perfect similarity provides no such criterion, so designation would be arbitrary and meaningless.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument conclude about the possibility of resurrecting a non-existent substance?
A) It is possible if God creates a duplicate.
B) It is impossible because the original and duplicate cannot be distinguished.
C) It is possible only if the duplicate is exactly similar.
D) It is impossible because substances cannot be recreated.

Key Takeaway
The third argument demonstrates that without a criterion to pick out the original from perfect duplicates, resurrection cannot be reliably identified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your physical body were destroyed and God created an exact copy, would you still be 'you'? What part of you—your deeds, your repentance, your unique soul—will stand before Him on the Day of Judgment? Are you nurturing that part today, or are you only concerned with your outward self?
?
The argument shows that without a clear criterion, resurrection seems rationally impossible. Yet you affirm it by faith. Does your heart truly trust that God will raise you as the same person who lived on earth? Or are you secretly troubled by the thought that you might not be the same? Spend a moment to strengthen your certainty by reflecting on His name 'al-Muʿīd' (the Restorer).
?
Imagine that God created a thousand perfect copies of you. What is the one thing that only you have done today that could never be duplicated? Your sincere tawbah? A secret charity? A prayer only you and He know? Commit to doing something today that will be uniquely yours in the Hereafter.

Razi's Critique of Philosophical Proof and Scriptural Reconciliation
Razi critiques the philosophers' insufficient argument and reconciles it with Quranic eschatology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's critique of the philosophers' use of a limited proof for a universal claim and his reconciliation with Quranic descriptions of cosmic destruction.

Core Idea
A proof that only applies to the outermost sphere cannot disprove the Quran's universal destruction.

Imam Razi expresses astonishment at the philosophers' logical leap: they use a single proof that only covers the outermost sphere to deny perforation for all spheres. He then shows how this limited argument does not contradict the Quranic verses describing the destruction of the heavens at resurrection, since the Throne (outermost sphere) is exempt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp Razi's tone and argument.
Notice the phrase 'وأنا أتعجب' – Razi is expressing astonishment.
2
Identify the logical flaw
Recognize that Razi points out the philosophers' proof works only for the outermost sphere, yet they claim a universal conclusion. This is an error of using a particular proof for a universal claim.
The Arabic phrase 'دليل خاص' (particular proof) and 'دعوى عامة' (universal claim) are key.
3
Understand the reconciliation
See how Razi aligns the philosophers' argument with scripture by noting that the Throne (outermost sphere) is not destroyed, so there is no contradiction.
The scholars identify the outermost sphere with the Throne (العرش).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المصنف : وأنا أتعجب ههنا من هؤلاء الفلاسفة فإنهم إنما عولوا في امتناع الخرق والالتئام على الأفلاك على هذا الدليل فقط، ثم إن هذا الدليل لا ينتج هذا المطلوب، إلا في الفلك الأقصى، أما سائر الأفلاك فهذا الدليل لا يجري فيها البتة، وكيف اكتفوا في إثبات الدعوى العامة بالدليل الخاص؟ واعلم أن كثيرًا من علماء الشريعة وعلماء التفسير قالوا: إن في وقت قيام القيامة تنخرق الأفلاك وتنهدم الكواكب، إلا أن العرش لا يتخرب، والفلك الأقصى هو العرش عندهم، وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم من قولنا : العرش لا يتخرب قدح في خراب السماوات والعناصر، فثبت أن ما ذكروه - لو صح - لم يتطرق الطعن إلى ما ورد في القرآن.
The author said: And I marvel here at these philosophers, for they relied in proving the impossibility of perforation and rejoining on the spheres solely on this proof. Then this proof does not yield this desired conclusion except for the outermost sphere. As for the other spheres, this proof does not apply at all. How could they be content to prove a universal claim with a particular proof? And know that many scholars of sacred law and exegesis said: At the time of the resurrection, the spheres will be rent and the stars will be destroyed, except that the Throne will not be destroyed. The outermost sphere is the Throne in their view. If that is the case, our saying that the Throne will not be destroyed does not impugn the destruction of the heavens and elements. Thus it is established that what they mentioned—even if sound—does not invalidate what is in the Quran.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage contains the author's personal critique and a reconciliation with scripture. He first expresses astonishment that the philosophers relied on a single proof that only works for the outermost sphere to deny perforation for all spheres—a logical fallacy of using a particular proof (دليل خاص) to prove a universal claim (دعوى عامة). He then notes that many scholars interpret the Quranic descriptions of the destruction of the heavens at the resurrection as applying to all spheres except the Throne (العرش), which is identified with the outermost sphere. Therefore, even if the philosophers' argument were sound for the outermost sphere, it does not contradict the Quranic verses that speak of the destruction of other celestial bodies. Thus the philosophers' objection does not conflict with revelation.

Key Terms
خرق
التئام
فلك
دعوى عامة
دليل خاص
عرش
قيامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Students might think Razi completely rejects the philosophers' argument or that it contradicts Quranic verses.
Correction: Razi does not reject the argument outright; he critiques its logical scope and then shows it does not conflict with scripture when properly understood.

Checkpoint

C) The sphere of Mercury

Key Takeaway
Razi critiques the philosophers' use of a particular proof for a universal claim and shows their argument does not contradict Quranic descriptions of the destruction of the heavens.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you face an argument that seems to challenge your faith, do you immediately dismiss it, or do you take the time to understand its scope and limitations as Razi did? This lesson shows that a proof can be valid in its own domain without contradicting the Quran. Will you commit to intellectual honesty, even when it is uncomfortable?
?
The philosophers relied on a single proof to make a universal claim. In your own life, where are you making sweeping judgments based on limited evidence—about others, about Allah, or about yourself? How can you expand your perspective to see the bigger picture, trusting that Allah's truth is vast enough to accommodate nuanced understanding?
?
Razi reconciled reason and revelation by carefully distinguishing what the proof actually proves. Are you willing to re-examine your own assumptions and admit when they are limited? This lesson demands that you trust both your intellect and the Quran, without forcing them into false conflict. What would it cost you to truly seek truth in this way?

Razi's Critique of Penetration and Fusion in the Heavens
Razi critiques the philosophers' proof and defends the Quranic view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that the philosophers' proof against penetration and fusion in the heavens is insufficient and how he reconciles it with the Quranic account of the destruction of the heavens.

Core Idea
The philosophers' proof for the impossibility of breaching the heavens only applies to the outermost sphere, not all spheres.

Imam al-Razi presents a philosophical argument from the philosophers that the celestial spheres cannot be breached or fused, based on the nature of rectilinear motion. He then critically examines this argument, showing its limited scope, and relates it to the Islamic belief in the destruction of the heavens at the Resurrection. This lesson explores Razi's nuanced critique and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' argument
Read the passage and locate the philosophers' reasoning for why penetration and fusion are impossible for the celestial spheres.
Notice they say it depends on rectilinear motion.
2
Understand Razi's answer
See how Razi responds by limiting the argument to the outermost sphere only.
This is a key move in the critique.
3
Connect to Quranic verses
Reflect on how Razi uses the scholars' interpretation of the Throne to show no conflict with the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجب لو لم يكن كذلك لكان البسيط مركبا هذا خلف، وإذا كان كل واحد من جانبيه متساويين في تمام الماهية للجانب الآخر فكل ما صح على أحد الجانبين صح على الآخر، فكما أن ذلك القمر يصح أن يماس بمقعره النار وبمحدبه كرة عطارد و أن يكون عكسه ممكنا ومتى كان الخرق والالتئام جائزين على الأفلاك. وأما الفلاسفة فقد احتجوا على امتناع ذلك بأن قالوا: الخرق والالتئام لا يحصلان إلا بالحركة المستقيمة، لكن الحركة المستقيمة على أجرام الفلك ممتنعة، فوجب القطع بامتناع الخرق والالتئام عليها. والجواب : أن هذا الكلام - وإن صح - لكن لا يتمشى إلا في الفلك الذي هو الجسم المحدد للجهات، وهو الفلك الأقصى، وأما سائر الأفلاك فلا يجري فيها البتة. قال المصنف : وأنا أتعجب ههنا من هؤلاء الفلاسفة فإنهم إنما عولوا في امتناع الخرق والالتئام على الأفلاك على هذا الدليل فقط، ثم إن هذا الدليل لا ينتج هذا المطلوب، إلا في الفلك الأقصى، أما سائر الأفلاك فهذا الدليل لا يجري فيها البتة، وكيف اكتفوا في إثبات الدعوى العامة بالدليل الخاص؟ واعلم أن كثيرًا من علماء الشريعة وعلماء التفسير قالوا: إن في وقت قيام القيامة تنخرق الأفلاك وتنهدم الكواكب، إلا أن العرش لا يتخرب، والفلك الأقصى هو العرش عندهم، وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم من قولنا : العرش لا يتخرب قدح في خراب السماوات والعناصر، فثبت أن ما ذكروه - لو صح - لم يتطرق الطعن إلى ما ورد في القرآن.
It must be that if it were not so, then the simple would be composite, which is absurd. And since each of its two sides is equal in complete essence to the other side, then whatever is valid for one side is valid for the other. Thus, just as that moon can touch fire with its concave surface and the sphere of Mercury with its convex surface, and the opposite is possible, and when penetration and fusion are possible for the celestial spheres. As for the philosophers, they argued for the impossibility of that by saying: Penetration and fusion only occur through rectilinear motion, but rectilinear motion is impossible for celestial bodies, so one must definitively judge the impossibility of penetration and fusion upon them. The answer: This statement—even if true—only applies to the sphere that determines the directions, which is the outermost sphere; as for the other spheres, it does not apply at all. The author said: I wonder here about these philosophers: they relied only on this proof for the impossibility of penetration and fusion in the spheres, and then this proof does not yield this conclusion except for the outermost sphere; as for the other spheres, this proof does not apply to them at all. How did they content themselves with a specific proof for a general claim? And know that many scholars of Islamic law and exegesis said that at the time of the establishment of the Hour, the spheres will be breached and the stars will be destroyed, except that the Throne will not be destroyed, and the outermost sphere is the Throne according to them. If that is so, then our saying that the Throne will not be destroyed does not entail a criticism of the destruction of the heavens and elements. Thus it is established that what they mentioned—even if valid—does not allow criticism of what came in the Quran.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage critiques the philosophers' argument that the celestial spheres cannot undergo penetration (al-kharq) and fusion (al-ilti'ām). The author (al-Rāzī) first presents the philosophers' proof: penetration and fusion only happen through rectilinear motion, which is impossible for celestial bodies. He then answers that this proof only applies to the outermost sphere (al-falak al-aqṣā) that determines the directions, not to all spheres. Al-Rāzī expresses amazement that the philosophers generalized a specific proof to all spheres. He then notes that many Islamic scholars hold that at the Resurrection, the spheres will be breached and the stars destroyed, except the Throne (al-ʿarsh), which they identify with the outermost sphere. Thus, even if the philosophers' argument were valid, it does not contradict the Quranic description of the destruction of the heavens.

الالتئام
الفلك الأقصى
الحركة المستقيمة
العرش

Correction: Razi shows the argument only applies to the outermost sphere (the Throne), which according to scholars will not be destroyed, while the other spheres can be breached.

Key Takeaway

Two Views on the Annihilation of the World
Razi introduces the problem and states the two scholarly positions on annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly positions on the annihilation of the world and the distinction between rational possibility and actual occurrence.

Core Idea
Annihilation is rationally possible for all creation, but only revelation tells us if it will happen.

In this passage, Razi introduces a key theological question: will God annihilate the world or not? He clarifies that rational proof only establishes possibility, not actuality. He then presents the two main views among Islamic scholars, setting the stage for arguments based on revelation. This lesson lays the foundation for understanding how theologians distinguish between what reason can prove and what must be accepted from scripture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation to get an overview of the problem and the two positions.
Notice the structure: problem statement, recap of rational possibility, two opinions, methodological remark.
2
Identify the two positions
Note the two views: one argues for total annihilation and recreation, the other for disintegration and recomposition. Understand the difference between them.
Both views agree that the world will be reconstituted, but differ on whether the original entities are completely obliterated.
3
Grasp the methodological point
Razi emphasizes that rational possibility is proven by reason, but actual occurrence only by revelation. This is a key principle in kalam about the scope of reason and scripture.
This distinction is crucial for many theological arguments—possibility does not imply actuality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في أن الله تعالى هل يعدم أجسام العالم أم لا ؟ اعلم أنا وإن بينا بالدلالة العقلية أن كل ما سوى الله تعالى فإنه يصح العدم عليه، ولكن ليس كل ما صح وقع، وقد اختلف علماء الإسلام في ذلك، فقال قوم: إنه تعالى يعدم الذوات والأجزاء ثم يعيدها وقال آخرون إنه تعالى لا يعدمها، بل يفرق أجزاء السماوات والأرض ويخربها، ثم إنه تعالى يؤلفها مرة أخرى كما كانت، واعلم أن الجواز العقلي يمكن إثباته بدلائل العقل أما وقوع الجائز فلا سبيل إلى إثباته إلا بالسمع.
The Fourth Chapter: On whether God, exalted, annihilates the bodies of the world or not. Know that although we have explained through rational proof that everything besides God, exalted, is possible of non-existence, yet not everything possible occurs. The scholars of Islam have differed on this: some said that He, exalted, annihilates the entities and parts then recreates them. Others said that He, exalted, does not annihilate them, but rather disintegrates the parts of the heavens and earth and destroys them, then He, exalted, composes them again as they were. And know that rational possibility can be proven by rational proofs, but the occurrence of what is possible cannot be proven except by revelation.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage opens the fourth chapter of the book, which investigates whether God will annihilate the bodies of the world or not. Al-Rāzī first states that rational proof establishes the possibility of annihilation for everything other than God, but possibility does not entail actual occurrence. He then presents the two positions among Islamic scholars: (1) Some say God completely annihilates the entities and parts (al-dhāt wa al-ajzā') and then recreates them. (2) Others say He does not annihilate them, but only disintegrates and destroys the composition of the heavens and earth, and later recomposes them. He concludes that rational possibility is proven by reason, but the actual occurrence of what is possible can only be proven through revelation (al-samʿ).

الجواز العقلي

السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational possibility implies actual occurrence.
Correction: Razi explicitly says 'not everything possible occurs' and distinguishes between proving possibility (reason) and proving actuality (revelation).

Checkpoint
According to Razi, how can the actual occurrence of a rational possibility be proven?
A) By rational proof
B) By revelation
C) By consensus
D) By analogy

Key Takeaway
Razi introduces the two scholarly opinions on world annihilation and establishes that while rationality can prove possibility, only revelation can prove actual occurrence.

?
Al-Razi insists that rational possibility does not guarantee actual occurrence. Is your faith about the afterlife based on mere possibility, or on the firm testimony of the Scripture? What specific verse do you cling to when doubts arise?
?
The scholars differed on how annihilation will occur, but all agreed it is possible. Do you treat the reality of the end of the world as a certainty that should shape your choices today? What would change if you truly believed that this world will be destroyed and rebuilt?

The First Quranic Proof: Everything Perishes
Razi presents and defends the first proof from the verse 'Every thing is perishing' against the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the verse 'Everything is perishing' to argue for the annihilation of the world's substances, and how he refutes the reinterpretation of 'halak' as mere loss of benefit.

Core Idea
The Quran's 'everything perishes' necessarily means total annihilation, not just dissolution.

This lesson examines the first scriptural proof from the Quran that the entire world will be annihilated. Razi presents the verse and then defends it against those who claim 'perishing' merely means loss of function. Through a careful argument, he shows that even disintegrated parts retain benefit, so only absolute non-existence fits the verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Identify the verse and the initial argument that 'halak' means non-existence. Note the general term 'everything' and the evidence from Quranic usage.
The proof relies on the general scope of 'every thing'.
2
Consider the objection
Understand how opponents reinterpret 'halak' to avoid annihilation of substances. They say it means 'exit from being beneficial'.
3
Follow the refutation
See how Razi counters by showing parts retain benefit even after disintegration, so the reinterpretation fails. Only total annihilation fits the verse.
Pay attention to the argument about parts being reusable.
4
Conclude
Grasp the definitive judgment that the parts themselves must perish, not just the composite.
This establishes the proof for annihilation of substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القاطعون على أنه تعالى يعدم العالم بآيات : إحداها قوله تعالى : كُل شَيْءٍ هَالِكُ إِلَّا وَجْهَهُ [القصص: ۸۸] فقوله: كل شيء لفظ عام يتناول الكل والهلاك عبارة عن العدم والفناء بدليل قوله تعالى : وإِن أمرُوا هَلَكَ الله [النساء : ١٧٦] أي فني ولم يبق فلو تفرقت الأجزاء لكنها ما عدمت وما فنيت فحينئذ يصدق أن السماوات هلكت لكنه لا يصدق أن تلك الأجزاء وتلك الذوات هلكت، فلما قال كُل شَىءٍ هَالِكُ الله علمنا أن الذوات لا تبقى، بل تفنى وتصير معدومة. أجاب القائلون بأن الله تعالى لا يعدم الذوات فقالوا : الهلاك عبارة عن خروج الشيء عن كونه منتفعًا به، وإذا تفرقت أجزاء السماوات والأرض فقد خرجت عن كونها منتفعا بها، ويكفي هذا القدر في صدق قولنا : إنها هلكت. أجاب المستدل الأول بأنها إذا تفرقت فقد خرجت السماء عن كونها منتفعا بها، أما أنه ما خرجت تلك الأجزاء عن كونها منتفعا بها ؛ فلأن تلك الأجزاء صالحة لأن تتركب منها السماوات والعناصر والجنة والنار، وصالحة لأن يستدل بها على الصانع القديم، فثبت أن المركبات وإن خرجت عن كونها منتفعا بها بسبب التفريق فتلك الأجزاء والذوات ما خرجت عن كونها منتفعا بها، فثبت أن الأجزاء والذوات لو بقيت لما صدق عليها أنها هلكت والقرآن يدل على أن الكل يصير هالكا ، فوجب القطع بأن الأجزاء تفنى.
Those who definitively hold that He, exalted, annihilates the world adduced proofs: The first is His saying, exalted: 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (Qasas 88). His saying 'everything' is a general term encompassing all, and 'perishing' (halak) is an expression for non-existence and annihilation, as evidenced by His saying: 'If a person perishes (halaka)' (Nisa 176) meaning he died and did not remain. So if the parts disintegrate but are not annihilated and do not perish, then it is true that the heavens perished, but it is not true that those parts and entities perished. Since He said 'Everything is perishing', we know that the entities do not remain, but rather perish and become non-existent. Those who say that God does not annihilate the entities answered: 'Perishing' is an expression for a thing exiting from being beneficial. When the parts of the heavens and earth disintegrate, they have exited from being beneficial, and this suffices for the truth of our saying that they perished. The first proponent answered: When they disintegrate, the heaven has exited from being beneficial, but as for those parts, they have not exited from being beneficial, because those parts are suitable for being composed into the heavens, elements, paradise, and hell, and are suitable for being used as evidence for the Eternal Maker. So it is established that although the composites have exited from being beneficial due to disintegration, those parts and entities have not exited from being beneficial. Thus it is established that if the parts and entities remained, the term 'perishing' would not apply to them, and the Quran indicates that everything becomes perishing, so one must definitively judge that the parts perish.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents the first scriptural proof for the position that God annihilates the world: the verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face'. The proponent of annihilation argues that 'halak' means total non-existence (al-ʿadam wa al-fanā'), citing as evidence the use of 'halaka' in the Quran meaning 'died and did not remain'. If the parts merely disintegrated but did not cease to exist, then although the composite 'heavens' would be said to have perished, the individual parts would not be described as perished. Since the verse says 'everything' perishes, the parts themselves must perish. The opponents respond by redefining 'halak' as 'exiting from being beneficial' (khurūj al-shayʿi ʿan kawnihi muntafaʿan bihi). When the parts disintegrate, they are no longer beneficial in their current state, so they can be said to have perished. The first proponent then refutes this: although the composite (the heaven) may have lost its benefit, the individual parts retain benefit because they can be reassembled into other things and serve as evidence for the Creator. Therefore, if the parts remained, they would not be truly 'perished', contradicting the Quran. Hence, the parts must be annihilated entirely.

Key Terms
الهلاك
العدم
الفناء
الانتفاع
الأجزاء
الذوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'halak' can mean merely 'loss of benefit', allowing the world's substances to remain.
Correction: Razi shows that even disintegrated parts retain benefit, so 'halak' must mean absolute non-existence.

Checkpoint
What does the first proponent argue against the interpretation that 'halak' means 'exiting from being beneficial'?
A) The parts are still beneficial for other purposes
B) The word 'halak' never means loss of benefit in Arabic
C) The verse is metaphorical
D) The disintegrated parts are no longer beneficial

Key Takeaway
The Quranic word 'halak' must mean absolute annihilation because even disintegrated parts retain benefit; therefore the verse proves the world's substances cease to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage proves that everything you own, every relationship, every ambition—your very body—will be annihilated. If that is absolutely certain, what one attachment are you holding onto as if it were eternal? Will you let go of it today?
?
Al-Razi shows that even the parts of this world will cease to exist. What part of your identity—your knowledge, status, or even your good deeds—do you secretly rely on for security, forgetting that all but Allah's Face will perish? How would your life change if you truly lived as if only Allah remains?
?
If the verse 'Everything is perishing' means total annihilation, then your soul will face its Creator stripped of everything. What have you prepared for that moment that death cannot take away? Be specific: what act or belief will survive the annihilation of this world and remain with you?

Second Quranic Proof: Reproduction Implies Annihilation
Razi argues that reproduction implies annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues from the verse 'He is the One who originates creation, then reproduces it' that God must annihilate all creatures before recreating them.

Core Idea
Reproducing all creation requires its prior annihilation.

Razi continues his series of Quranic proofs for annihilation. In this proof, he focuses on the word 'reproduction' (al-iʿāda). The verse from Surat al-Rum states that God originates creation and then reproduces it. Razi argues that the term 'creation' includes all creatures, and since God reproduces all of them, they must first have been annihilated. Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and Razi's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi identifies the key words 'creation' and 'reproduces it'.
2
Understand the implications of 'reproduction'
Consider why reproduction logically requires prior annihilation. Think about what is meant by 'reproduction' in the context of creation.
Reproduction (al-iʿāda) refers to bringing something back after it has ceased to exist.
3
Confirm the conclusion
Razi concludes that the verse proves God annihilates all creatures. Reflect on whether this reasoning is sound.
The argument assumes that reproduction must be of the same entities that were annihilated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: التمسك بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَبْدَوُا الْخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يُعِيدُ الرُّوم: ٢٧] ولفظ "الخلق" متناول لجميع المخلوقات والضمير في قوله : " يعيده" عائد إلى الخلق، فدلت هذه الآية على أنه تعالى يعيد جميع مخلوقاته، والإعادة لا تعقل إلا بعد تقدم الإفناء، فدل هذا على أنه تعالى يفني جميع مخلوقاته. الحجة الثالثة : التمسك بقوله تعالى : وهو الأَوَّلُ وَالْآخِرُ ﴾ [الحديد: ٣] معنى كونه أنه تعالى كان موجودًا في الأزل، مع أنه ما كان معه غيره، وكذلك معنى الآخر أنه تعالى يبقى في الأبد مع أنه لا يكون معه غيره . وهذا يقتضي أنه تعالى يعدم جميع المخلوقات حتى يتحقق كونه آخراً، ثم يعيدها مرة أخرى ليتحقق صدق الآيات الدالة على أن الثواب والعقاب لا آخر لهما. الحجة الرابعة: التمسك بقوله تعالى: كَمَا بَدَأْنَا أَوَّلَ خَلْقٍ نُعِيدُهُ :[الأنبياء : ١٠٤] حكم بأن الإعادة على وفق الابتداء، ولما كان الابتداء عبارة عن خلق الذوات وخلق التركيب والتأليف فيها وجب أن يكون وقت الإعادة لخلق
The second proof: Adherence to His saying: 'And He is the One who originates creation, then reproduces it' (Rum 27). The word 'creation' encompasses all creatures, and the pronoun in 'reproduces it' refers back to creation. So this verse indicates that He, exalted, reproduces all His creatures, and reproduction is not conceivable except after prior annihilation. So this indicates that He, exalted, annihilates all His creatures. The third proof: Adherence to His saying: 'He is the First and the Last' (Hadid 3). The meaning of 'He is the First' is that He, exalted, existed in pre-eternity while nothing else existed with Him. Similarly, the meaning of 'the Last' is that He, exalted, remains in post-eternity while nothing else exists with Him. This requires that He, exalted, annihilates all creatures until His being 'Last' is realized, then reproduces them again to realize the truth of the verses indicating that reward and punishment have no end. The fourth proof: Adherence to His saying: 'As We began the first creation, We will reproduce it' (Anbiya 104). He ruled that the reproduction is in accordance with the beginning. Since the beginning was the creation of entities and the creation of composition and arrangement in them, it must be that at the time of reproduction, the creation...
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents three additional Quranic proofs for the annihilation of all creatures. The second proof uses the verse from Surat al-Rum, arguing that the word 'creation' (al-khalq) refers to all creatures, and God's 'reproduction' (al-iʿāda) of them implies that they were first annihilated. The third proof uses the verse 'He is the First and the Last' to argue that God will be the only existent in the end, requiring the annihilation of all creatures, after which He will recreate them so that reward and punishment can be eternal. The fourth proof uses the verse from Surat al-Anbiya, but the argument is cut off. The author is in the middle of arguing that just as the original creation involved the creation of entities and their composition, the re-creation must also involve the creation of entities from nothing, implying annihilation.

Misconception: One might think that 'reproduces it' means God simply continues the existence of creatures without them ever ceasing to exist.
Correction: Razi argues that the term 'reproduction' (al-iʿāda) specifically implies bringing back after cessation, so it requires prior annihilation.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why does the verse 'He originates creation then reproduces it' imply that God annihilates all creatures?
A) Because the word 'creation' refers only to humans.
B) Because reproduction is only possible if the original things have been annihilated.
C) Because God mentions both origination and reproduction.
D) Because the verse uses the word 'then' indicating a time gap.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that God's reproduction of all creation logically requires their prior annihilation.

?
Razi argues that 'reproduction is not conceivable except after prior annihilation.' This means your current existence will be completely undone before it is remade. How does this certainty reshape your view of your daily struggles and achievements? Are you living as if this annihilation is real, or as if this world will last forever?
?
The verse encompasses 'all creatures' – including your own body, your loved ones, and the world you know. If you have just one hour left before annihilation, what would you say to God, and what would you change about your last actions? Do not let this remain theoretical; commit to one specific change based on this reflection.

Third Quranic Proof: He is the First and the Last
Razi argues from 'the First and the Last' for annihilation and recreation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the divine attributes 'the First' and 'the Last' to argue for the annihilation and subsequent recreation of all creatures.

Core Idea
Being the Last means God is the only existent after annihilation.

Razi now turns to a profound argument based on two of God's names: al-Awwal (the First) and al-Akhir (the Last). He interprets these as meaning that God alone existed in pre-eternity and will alone exist in post-eternity. This implies that at some point all creatures must be annihilated so that God is the only existent. Then, because reward and punishment are eternal, He must recreate them. This lesson examines the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Notice the definitions of 'First' and 'Last'.
2
Understand the implication of 'the Last'
If God is the Last, that means in the end nothing else exists with Him. This requires the annihilation of all creatures.
The argument does not specify a timeline; it is a logical implication.
3
See the connection to recreation
Razi then notes that reward and punishment are eternal, so creatures must be recreated after annihilation to experience them.
This combines the attribute of being Last with the eternity of the afterlife.

Fourth Quranic Proof: Recreation as the Beginning (Incomplete)
Razi begins a proof from the analogy of creation and recreation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the beginning of Razi's fourth proof from the verse 'As We began the first creation, We will reproduce it', and recognize that the argument is incomplete in the text.

Core Idea
Recreation mirrors the original creation from nothing.

Razi's fourth proof draws on the verse from Surat al-Anbiya which explicitly links the beginning of creation with its reproduction. He argues that the process of reproduction must correspond to the initial creation. Since the initial creation involved bringing entities into existence from nothing and composing them, the reproduction must also involve a fresh creation from nothing—implying annihilation. Unfortunately, the passage is cut off, but we can still examine the opening of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the available passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the fourth proof. Notice the connection drawn between 'beginning' and 'reproduction'.
The text ends mid-sentence.
2
Understand the analogy of beginning and reproduction
Razi states that reproduction is according to the beginning. The beginning involved creating entities and their composition, so reproduction must similarly create them from nothing.
This implies the need for annihilation before recreation.
3
Reflect on the incomplete argument
Consider what the full argument might have concluded. How would Razi have completed this proof?
The original source is incomplete; this lesson only presents the beginning.

The second proof: Adherence to His saying: 'And He is the One who originates creation, then reproduces it' (Rum 27). The word 'creation' encompasses all creatures, and the pronoun in 'reproduces it' refers back to creation. So this verse indicates that He, exalted, reproduces all His creatures, and reproduction is not conceivable except after prior annihilation. So this indicates that He, exalted, annihilates all His creatures. The third proof: Adherence to His saying: 'He is the First and the Last' (Hadid 3). The meaning of 'He is the First' is that He, exalted, existed in pre-eternity while nothing else existed with Him. Similarly, the meaning of 'the Last' is that He, exalted, remains in post-eternity while nothing else exists with Him. This requires that He, exalted, annihilates all creatures until His being 'Last' is realized, then reproduces them again to realize the truth of the verses indicating that reward and punishment have no end. The fourth proof: Adherence to His saying: 'As We began the first creation, We will reproduce it' (Anbiya 104). He ruled that the reproduction is in accordance with the beginning. Since the beginning was the creation of entities and the creation of composition and arrangement in them, it must be that at the time of reproduction, the creation...
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents three additional Quranic proofs for the annihilation of all creatures. The second proof uses the verse from Surat al-Rum, arguing that the word 'creation' (al-khalq) refers to all creatures, and God's 'reproduction' (al-iʿāda) of them implies that they were first annihilated. The third proof uses the verse 'He is the First and the Last' to argue that God will be the only existent in the end, requiring the annihilation of all creatures, after which He will recreate them so that reward and punishment can be eternal. The fourth proof uses the verse from Surat al-Anbiya, but the argument is cut off. The author is in the middle of arguing that just as the original creation involved the creation of entities and their composition, the re-creation must also involve the creation of entities from nothing, implying annihilation.

D) That reproduction will happen only once

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof argues that reproduction follows the pattern of the initial creation, which involved creation from nothing, implying annihilation.
