Quranic Evidence for the Three Premises of Resurrection
Razi analyzes three Quranic verses to show they all support the three premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses three Quranic verses to establish the three rational premises for bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's own arguments for resurrection rest on possibility, power, and knowledge.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi shows that the Qur'an does not merely assert resurrection but builds it on three rational premises: the intrinsic possibility of the act, God's perfect power, and His complete knowledge. He then examines three verses from Surat An-Naml, Ya-Sin, and Ar-Rum, demonstrating how each verse implicitly or explicitly contains these premises. By seeing this, you will appreciate how revelation and reason converge in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three premises
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note Razi's general statement that every resurrection verse contains three premises. Identify what those premises are from his analysis.
The premises are: essential possibility (al-jawaz al-dhati), perfect power (kamal al-qudra), and perfect knowledge (kamal al-'ilm).
2
Examine the first verse: Surat An-Naml
Look at how Razi extracts the premises from 'who originates creation, then restores it' and 'none knows the unseen except Allah'. Notice that the first phrase gives two premises, the second gives one.
3
Examine the second verse: Surat Ya-Sin
Read Razi's analysis of 'who brought them forth the first time' and 'He is knowing of every creation', plus the subsequent verse. See how he repeatedly identifies power and knowledge.
This verse also has an additional implicit indication from the creation of heavens and earth.
4
Examine the third verse: Surat Ar-Rum
Analyze how 'originates creation, then restores it' indicates possibility and power, while 'the Almighty' and 'the Wise' indicate power and knowledge respectively.
5
Reflect on the consistency across verses
Consider why Razi insists that every resurrection verse contains these three premises. What does this tell us about the relationship between reason and revelation?
Razi is showing that the Qur'an itself provides rational proofs, not mere assertions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه سبحانه وتعالى كلما ذكر في القرآن هذه المسألة بني تقريرها على هذه المقدمات الثلاث. وكل آية وردت في هذه المسألة فهي مشتملة على تقرير هذه المقدمات الثلاث، ومن الآيات : الآية الأولى : قوله تعالى في سورة النمل : أَمَن يَبْدَوُا الخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يُعِيدُهُ وَمَن يَرْزُقُكُم منَ السَّمَاءِ وَالْأَرْضِ أَوَلَهُ مَعَ اللهِ قُل هاتوا برهنكم إن كنتم صادِقِينَ قُل لَا يَعْلَمُ مَن فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ الْغَيْبَ إِلَّا اللهُ ﴾ [النمل: ٦٥.٦٤] فقوله : أمَن يَبْدُوا الخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يُعِيدُهُ إشارة إلى مقدمتين : إحداهما : أن عوده ممكن في نفسه. والثانية : أنه تعالى قادر على هذا الممكن لأنه لو لم يكن الأمر كذلك لما كان الابتداء ممكنا وقوله تعالى : وقُل لَّا يَعْلَمُ مَن فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ الْغَيْبَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ [النمل: ٦٥]. والآية الثانية: في سورة يس وهي قوله تعالى: ﴿وَضَرَبَ لَنَا مَثَلًا وَنَسِي خَلَقَهُ قَالَ من يُحْيِ الْعِظَامَ وَهِيَ رَمِيمٌ قُلْ يُحْيِيهَا الَّذِي أَنشَأَهَا أَوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ خَلْقٍ عَليم ایس : ۷۹.۷۸] فقوله : أَنشَأَهَا أَوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ إشارة إلى الجواز الذاتي والقدرة، وقوله: ﴿وَهُوَ بِكُلِ خَلْقٍ عَلِيمٌ إشارة إلى كمال العلم، وأيضًا قوله تعالى: ر أَوَلَيْسَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ بِقَدِرٍ عَلَى أَن يخلق مِثْلَهُمْ ﴾ [يس: ۸۱] إشارة إلى الجواز الذاتي، أو إلى كمال القدرة ، ثم قال : بَلَى وَهُوَ الْخَلَّقُ الْعَلِيمُ﴾ [يس: ۸۱] إعادة لتلك المقدمة مقدمة العلم. والآية الثالثة : في سورة الروم، وهي قوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَبْدَوُا الْخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يعِيدُمُ وَهُوَ أَهْوَنُ عَلَيْهِ وَلَهُ الْمَثَلُ الْأَعْلَى فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَهُوَ الْعَزِيزُ الْحَكِيمُ [الروم: ۳۷] فقوله: ﴿يَبْدَوُا الْخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يُعِيدُول إشارة إلى الجواز الذاتي وكمال القدرة ، ثم قوله : ﴿وَهُوَ الْعَزِيزُ إشارة أيضًا إلى كمال القدرة والحكيم إشارة إلى كمال العلم، ونحن قد شرحنا في تفسيرنا أن كل آية وردت في هذه المسألة فهي مشتملة على تقرير هذه المقدمات الثلاث.
And know that He, glorified and exalted, whenever He mentions this issue in the Qur'an, He bases its establishment on these three premises. And every verse that occurs on this issue includes the establishment of these three premises. Among the verses: The first verse: His saying, exalted, in Surat An-Naml: 'Is He who originates creation, then restores it, and who provides you from the heaven and the earth? Is there a god with Allah? Say, bring your proof, if you are truthful.' 'Say, none in the heavens and the earth knows the unseen except Allah.' [27:64-65]. So His saying 'who originates creation, then restores it' refers to two premises: one: that its restoration is possible in itself, and the second: that He, exalted, is powerful over this possible, because if it were not so, the origination would not be possible. And His saying, exalted: 'And say, none in the heavens and the earth knows the unseen except Allah.' The second verse: in Surat Ya-Sin, His saying, exalted: 'And he strikes for Us an example and forgets his creation; he says: Who will give life to bones while they are decayed? Say: He will give them life who brought them forth the first time, and He is knowing of every creation.' [36:78-79]. So His saying 'brought them forth the first time' refers to essential possibility and power, and His saying 'and He is knowing of every creation' refers to perfect knowledge. Also His saying, exalted: 'Is not He who created the heavens and the earth able to create the like of them?' [36:81] refers to essential possibility, or to perfect power. Then He said: 'Yes, and He is the All-Knowing Creator.' [36:81] repeating that premise, the premise of knowledge. The third verse: in Surat Ar-Rum, His saying, exalted: 'And He it is who originates creation, then restores it, and it is easier for Him. And His is the highest attribute in the heavens and the earth. And He is the Almighty, the Wise.' [30:27]. So His saying 'originates creation, then restores it' refers to essential possibility and perfect power; then His saying 'and He is the Almighty' also refers to perfect power, and 'the Wise' refers to perfect knowledge. And we have explained in our commentary that every verse that occurs on this issue includes the establishment of these three premises.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage demonstrates how Quranic revelation itself supports the three premises of rational possibility. Razi asserts that whenever God mentions resurrection in the Qur'an, He bases it on these same three premises: the possibility of the act, His power, and His knowledge. He then analyzes three verses as examples. The first verse from Surat An-Naml (27:64-65): the phrase 'who originates creation, then restores it' indicates the intrinsic possibility and God's power; the phrase 'none knows the unseen except Allah' indicates God's exclusive knowledge. The second verse from Surat Ya-Sin (36:78-81): 'who brought them forth the first time' indicates essential possibility and power; 'He is knowing of every creation' indicates perfect knowledge; and the subsequent verse 'Is not He who created the heavens and the earth able to create the like of them?' reiterates power, and 'Yes, and He is the All-Knowing Creator' reiterates knowledge. The third verse from Surat Ar-Rum (30:27): 'He originates creation, then restores it' indicates possibility and power; 'the Almighty' indicates power; 'the Wise' indicates knowledge. Razi concludes by mentioning his own commentary where he explains that every resurrection verse contains these three premises.

Key Terms
الآيات
المقدمات الثلاث
الجواز الذاتي
كمال القدرة
كمال العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that Quranic verses about resurrection are merely statements of belief without any logical grounding.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that these verses contain implicit rational arguments for the possibility of resurrection, God's power to accomplish it, and His knowledge to re-create individuals exactly.

Checkpoint
In the verse from Surat Ar-Rum (30:27), which divine attribute in the verse corresponds to the premise of perfect knowledge?
A) 'Who originates creation'
B) 'the Almighty'
C) 'the Wise'
D) 'It is easier for Him'

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic arguments for resurrection consistently rely on the same three rational premises: intrinsic possibility, divine power, and divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi claims that every resurrection verse in the Quran contains these three premises. If you truly believe that, how should it change the way you read the Quran? Will you commit to looking for this structure in your next recitation?
?
Your certainty in resurrection—is it based on blind acceptance or on understanding the rational foundation the Quran itself provides? If you cannot articulate the three premises from this lesson, what does that reveal about the strength of your conviction?

The Three Premises: Definition and First Quranic Proof
Razi states the three-premise rule and analyzes al-Naml verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every Quranic resurrection verse is built on three rational premises—intrinsic possibility, divine power, and divine knowledge—as shown by Razi's analysis of Surah al-Naml.

Core Idea
Every resurrection verse is a concise logical argument with three premises.

Razi opens with a powerful hermeneutic rule: whenever the Quran mentions resurrection, the argument is structured around three premises. He then shows how the first verse (al-Naml 64-65) contains all three. This lesson unpacks that rule and the first proof, establishing the pattern for all resurrection verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage slowly
Read the passage from 'واعلم أنه' to the end of the al-Naml analysis. Notice the general claim and then the verse breakdown.
The passage is compact; take your time.
2
Identify the three premises
Look for Razi's explicit mention: two premises from the first phrase (possibility and power) and a third from the next phrase (knowledge).
He says 'إشارة إلى مقدمتين' then later implies the third.
3
Match each premise to the verse
See how 'أَمَن يَبْدَوُا الخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يُعِيدُهُ' indicates possibility and power, and 'قُل لَا يَعْلَمُ...' indicates knowledge.
4
Reflect on the logical necessity
Ask yourself: why are these three premises essential for proving resurrection? Without possibility, the argument fails; without power, it cannot happen; without knowledge, the restoration is not identical.
This connects to kalam reasoning.
5
Summarize the lesson
Write a one-sentence takeaway: 'The Quran argues for resurrection using three premises: possibility, power, and knowledge.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه سبحانه وتعالى كلما ذكر في القرآن هذه المسألة بني تقريرها على هذه المقدمات الثلاث. وكل آية وردت في هذه المسألة فهي مشتملة على تقرير هذه المقدمات الثلاث، ومن الآيات : الآية الأولى : قوله تعالى في سورة النمل : أَمَن يَبْدَوُا الخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يُعِيدُهُ وَمَن يَرْزُقُكُم منَ السَّمَاءِ وَالْأَرْضِ أَوَلَهُ مَعَ اللهِ قُل هاتوا برهنكم إن كنتم صادِقِينَ قُل لَا يَعْلَمُ مَن فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ الْغَيْبَ إِلَّا اللهُ ﴾ [النمل: ٦٥.٦٤] فقوله : أمَن يَبْدُوا الخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يُعِيدُهُ إشارة إلى مقدمتين : إحداهما : أن عوده ممكن في نفسه. والثانية : أنه تعالى قادر على هذا الممكن لأنه لو لم يكن الأمر كذلك لما كان الابتداء ممكنا وقوله تعالى : وقُل لَّا يَعْلَمُ مَن فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ الْغَيْبَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ [النمل: ٦٥]. والآية الثانية: في سورة يس وهي قوله تعالى: ﴿وَضَرَبَ لَنَا مَثَلًا وَنَسِي خَلَقَهُ قَالَ من يُحْيِ الْعِظَامَ وَهِيَ رَمِيمٌ قُلْ يُحْيِيهَا الَّذِي أَنشَأَهَا أَوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ خَلْقٍ عَليم [يس: ۷۹.۷۸] فقوله : أَنشَأَهَا أَوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ إشارة إلى الجواز الذاتي والقدرة، وقوله: ﴿وَهُوَ بِكُلِ خَلْقٍ عَلِيمٌ إشارة إلى كمال العلم، وأيضًا قوله تعالى: أَوَلَيْسَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ بِقَدِرٍ عَلَى أَن يخلق مِثْلَهُمْ ﴾ [يس: ۸۱] إشارة إلى الجواز الذاتي، أو إلى كمال القدرة ، ثم قال : بَلَى وَهُوَ الْخَلَّقُ الْعَلِيمُ﴾ [يس: ۸۱] إعادة لتلك المقدمة مقدمة العلم. والآية الثالثة : في سورة الروم، وهي قوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَبْدَوُا الْخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يعِيدُمُ وَهُوَ أَهْوَنُ عَلَيْهِ وَلَهُ الْمَثَلُ الْأَعْلَى فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَهُوَ الْعَزِيزُ الْحَكِيمُ [الروم: ۳۷] فقوله: ﴿يَبْدَوُا الْخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يُعِيدُول إشارة إلى الجواز الذاتي وكمال القدرة ، ثم قوله : ﴿وَهُوَ الْعَزِيزُ إشارة أيضًا إلى كمال القدرة والحكيم إشارة إلى كمال العلم، ونحن قد شرحنا في تفسيرنا أن كل آية وردت في هذه المسألة فهي مشتملة على تقرير هذه المقدمات الثلاث.
Know that He, glorified and exalted, whenever He mentions this issue in the Quran, He establishes its proof upon these three premises. And every verse that has come down on this issue includes the establishment of these three premises. Among the verses: The first verse: His saying, exalted, in Surah al-Naml: 'Is He who originates creation then brings it back, and who provides for you from the heaven and the earth? Is there a god with Allah? Say: Bring your proof, if you are truthful. Say: None in the heavens and the earth knows the unseen except Allah' (al-Naml 64-65). His saying: 'Is He who originates creation then brings it back' indicates two premises: one: that its return is possible in itself. The second: that He, exalted, is powerful over this possible thing, because if it were not so, then the origination would not be possible. And His saying, exalted: 'Say: None in the heavens and the earth knows the unseen except Allah' (al-Naml 65). And the second verse: in Surah Ya Sin, His saying, exalted: 'And he strikes for Us a simile and forgets his own creation. He says: Who gives life to bones when they are decayed? Say: He will give them life who created them the first time, and He is Knower of every creation' (Ya Sin 78-79). His saying: 'who created them the first time' indicates intrinsic possibility and power. And His saying: 'and He is Knower of every creation' indicates perfection of knowledge. And also His saying, exalted: 'Is not He who created the heavens and the earth able to create the likes of them?' (Ya Sin 81) indicates intrinsic possibility, or perfection of power. Then He said: 'Yes, and He is the All-Creator, the All-Knowing' (Ya Sin 81) repeating that premise, the premise of knowledge. And the third verse: in Surah al-Rum, His saying, exalted: 'And He is who originates creation then brings it back, and that is easier for Him. And His is the highest attribute in the heavens and the earth, and He is the Almighty, the All-Wise' (al-Rum 27). His saying: 'originates creation then brings it back' indicates intrinsic possibility and perfection of power. Then His saying: 'and He is the Almighty' indicates also perfection of power, and 'the All-Wise' indicates perfection of knowledge. And we have explained in our commentary that every verse that has come down on this issue includes the establishment of these three premises.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage defines and analyzes the Quranic proof for the resurrection (ma'ad). Razi states that whenever Allah mentions the issue of resurrection in the Quran, He builds its proof upon three premises. These three premises are: (1) the intrinsic possibility of return (i.e., that recreating the body is possible in itself), (2) divine power to bring about that possible thing, and (3) divine knowledge to restore the exact same body. Razi then demonstrates that every specific verse on resurrection contains these three premises. He gives three examples: from Surah al-Naml, Ya Sin, and al-Rum. For each verse, he points out which phrases indicate which premises. For instance, in al-Naml, the phrase 'Is He who originates creation then brings it back' indicates both the possibility of return (since it asks about the one who can do it) and divine power (since He is able to originate, He is able to bring back). The phrase 'None knows the unseen except Allah' indicates divine knowledge. Similarly for the other verses. Razi concludes by noting that he has already explained in his own tafsir that every resurrection verse contains these three premises.

الجواز الذاتي
قدرة
علم
المعاد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Quranic resurrection verses are merely rhetorical or emotional appeals.
Correction: Razi shows they are precise logical arguments with three distinct premises.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what are the three premises found in the al-Naml verse on resurrection?
A) Creation, provision, and knowledge
B) Intrinsic possibility, divine power, and divine knowledge
C) Power, will, and wisdom
D) Life, death, and rebirth

Key Takeaway
The Quranic argument for resurrection is not mere rhetoric but a precise logical structure using three premises: possibility, power, and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse about resurrection, do you instinctively trust it as a promise, or do you demand the logical proof that Razi shows is already there? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on reason versus revelation?
?
If resurrection is logically proven through these three premises, why do you still sometimes doubt your own standing before Allah? Which premise do you struggle to internalize—possibility, power, or knowledge?
?
The verse asks 'Is there a god with Allah?' after establishing His exclusive power and knowledge. Who or what do you rely on besides Allah, even subconsciously, when you think about your future after death?

Three More Quranic Proofs: Surah Ya Sin and al-Rum
Razi analyzes two more Quranic verses to demonstrate the three premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how Razi extracts the same three premises from two additional resurrection verses (Ya Sin and al-Rum), confirming that the three-premise structure is universal in the Quran.

Core Idea
The three premises reappear in every resurrection verse, proving they are essential to the Quranic argument.

Having established the pattern with al-Naml, Razi now tests it on two more verses. The verses from Surah Ya Sin and Surah al-Rum differ in wording but contain the same three premises. This lesson traces his analysis and confirms the rule, showing the Quran's remarkable consistency in logical argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Review the three premises
Recall from Lesson 1: intrinsic possibility, divine power, and divine knowledge.
Keep these in mind as you read.
2
Analyze the Ya Sin verse
Read Razi's breakdown of Ya Sin 78-81. Identify which phrases indicate each premise.
He mentions 'أنشأها أول مرة' for possibility/power, and 'وهو بكل خلق عليم' for knowledge.
3
Analyze the al-Rum verse
Read the al-Rum 27 analysis. Note how 'يبدو الخلق ثم يعيده' gives possibility/power, and 'الحكيم' gives knowledge.
Also note 'العزيز' reinforces power.
4
Compare the three verses
Notice that each verse uses different phrases but all cover the same three premises. This confirms Razi's rule.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi ends by asserting his earlier commentary that every resurrection verse contains these premises. Consider the strength of this pattern.
It shows the Quran's internal coherence.

Unseen Matters

The First Philosophical Objection: Recreating the Non-Existent
Razi presents the first philosophical objection, a syllogism with two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the first philosophical objection against bodily resurrection and its two premises.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection seems to require bringing back what no longer exists.

Philosophers who deny bodily resurrection often base their argument on a logical puzzle: resurrecting a body that has completely ceased to exist. Imam Razi begins by presenting this objection, which rests on two premises. Let us examine how the objection is constructed, so we can later understand the counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's conclusion
Read the opening sentence. Razi states that the philosophers argue that bodily resurrection is impossible. Find the stated conclusion.
2
Understand the first premise
The first premise says that gathering bodies (hashr al-ajsad) cannot happen without affirming the validity of recreating the non-existent. Notice that Razi explains this premise in two scenarios: annihilation or disassembly.
Whether God annihilates or disassembles, in either case something that was non-existent must be brought back.
3
Recognize the second premise
The second premise claims that recreating the non-existent is impossible. Razi mentions that he has already discussed the sophisms supporting this claim.
This premise is the crux of the objection.
4
Trace the syllogism
Combine the premises: if P (resurrection) requires Q (recreating non-existent), and Q is impossible, then P is impossible. This is a standard logical form.
The objection is a valid argument; its soundness depends on the second premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة المنكرون لصحة المعاد الجسماني فقد احتجوا بوجهين : الأول : أن حشر الأجساد لا يتم إلا مع القول بصحة إعادة المعدوم، وهذا محال، فذلك محال . أما المقدمة الأولى : فقد بينا فيما تقدم أنا سواء قلنا إنه تعالى يعدم الأجزاء ثم يعيدها ، أو قلنا : إنه تعالى يفرقها ثم يركبها فإنه لا بد من القول بصحة إعادة المعدوم. وأما المقدمة الثانية: وهي أن إعادة المعدوم ممتنعة، فقد سبقت حكاية شبهاتهم فيها.
As for the philosophers who deny the validity of bodily resurrection, they have argued by two points: The first: that the gathering of bodies is not accomplished except with the affirmation of the validity of recreating the non-existent (i'ādat al-ma'dūm), and this is impossible, so that is impossible. As for the first premise: we have explained previously that whether we say that He, exalted, annihilates the parts then recreates them, or we say that He, exalted, separates them then recombines them, it is necessary to affirm the validity of recreating the non-existent. And as for the second premise: which is that recreating the non-existent is impossible, the recounting of their sophisms concerning it has preceded.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage presents the first philosophical objection against bodily resurrection. Razi states that the philosophers who deny bodily resurrection argued by two points (or two lines of reasoning?). The first argument is: the gathering of bodies (hashr al-ajsad) cannot be accomplished unless we accept the validity of recreating the non-existent (i'ādat al-ma'dūm). But recreating the non-existent is impossible; therefore, the gathering of bodies is impossible. Razi then unpacks this argument into two premises. The first premise: whether one holds that Allah annihilates the parts and then recreates them, or that He disassembles them and then reassembles them, in either case one must affirm the validity of recreating the non-existent. The second premise: recreating the non-existent is impossible. Razi notes that he has already recounted the philosophers' sophisms (shubahāt) regarding the impossibility of recreating the non-existent in an earlier discussion. This objection sets up the debate about whether God can bring back something that has ceased to exist entirely.

Key Terms
المعاد الجسماني
إعادة المعدوم
حشر الأجساد
مقدمتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection is about God's power being limited.
Correction: Rather, it is about the logical conceivability of restoring exact non-existence; the philosophers argue it is logically impossible, not merely beyond power.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise of the first philosophical objection against bodily resurrection?
A) That resurrection requires recreating the non-existent
B) That God cannot reassemble disintegrated bodies
C) That the soul is the real self, not the body
D) That the Quran does not explicitly mention resurrection

Key Takeaway
The first philosophical objection against bodily resurrection is a syllogism that frames resurrection as requiring the impossible—recreating what no longer exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine your own body being resurrected from dust, do you ever feel a doubt—like it might be too far-fetched? Where does that doubt live in your heart, and what does it reveal about your trust in God's power to recreate?
?
If you truly believed that God can bring you back from complete non-existence, how would that change the way you treat your body today? Are you living as if this earthly body is all you have?
?
The philosophers argue that recreating the non-existent is impossible. Have you ever actually confronted that argument with real conviction, or do you just assume it's false? What would you say to someone who truly believes it's impossible—and does your life reflect that answer?

Second Objection: The Problem of the Eaten Body
Razi presents the second philosophical objection: conflicting ownership of body parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second philosophical objection against bodily resurrection, known as the 'cannibalism problem', and why it challenges the coherence of material resurrection.

Core Idea
If a body's parts are eaten by another, both cannot be resurrected with the same matter.

This lesson presents the second objection from Imam Razi's al-Arba'in. It raises a logical difficulty: if a human body is consumed by another, how can both individuals be resurrected with their original material parts? This objection targets the physical continuity needed for bodily identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the argument flow naturally without analysis.
2
Understand the scenario
Razi describes a scenario: a person is killed and eaten by another. The victim's body parts become part of the eater's body through nourishment.
This is the 'cannibalism' scenario.
3
Identify the logical problem
At resurrection, those identical parts must be returned to one body, leaving the other incomplete. This is the core difficulty.
The same matter cannot belong to two persons.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Razi concludes that the doctrine of bodily resurrection (hashr al-ajsām) is impossible. Note that this is a philosophical objection, not a theological assertion.
Islamic theologians later answered this objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إذا قتل إنسان وأكله آخر فقد صارت أجزاء بدن المقتول أجزاء بدن هذا الذي أكله؛ لأن من أكل شيئًا واغتذى به فقد صارت أجزاء الغذاء أجزاء بدن المغتذي، فيوم القيامة لا بد أن ترد تلك الأجزاء إلى بدن أحد هذين الشخصين، فلا بد أن يضيع الثاني، فعلمنا أن القول بحشر الأجسام محال
The second: If a person is killed and another ate him, then the parts of the body of the killed become parts of the body of the one who ate him; because whoever eats something and is nourished by it, the parts of the food become parts of the body of the nourished one. On the Day of Resurrection, those parts must be returned to the body of one of these two persons, so the second must be lost. Thus we know that the statement of the gathering of bodies is impossible.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents the second philosophical objection against bodily resurrection, often called the 'cannibalism problem.' Razi states the scenario: if a person is killed and another person eats him, then the body parts of the victim become part of the eater's body through digestion and nourishment. On the Day of Resurrection, those identical parts cannot be returned to both bodies simultaneously; they must be assigned to either the victim or the eater. Whichever body receives the parts, the other body will be missing those parts and thus be incomplete. The conclusion is that the gathering of bodies (hashr al-ajsām) is impossible. This argument assumes that physical continuity of matter is necessary for identity, and that the same particles cannot be in two places at once.

Key Terms
أكل
أجزاء البدن
اغتذى
حشر الأجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is about God's justice or fairness.
Correction: The objection is about the physical impossibility of the same material parts being simultaneously present in two bodies, not about moral justice.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, why is bodily resurrection impossible when one person eats another?
A) Because God cannot decide who gets the parts
B) Because the parts of the victim become parts of the eater, and cannot be returned to both bodies
C) Because the eater's body is destroyed by cannibalism
D) Because the Quran forbids cannibalism

Key Takeaway
The cannibalism objection argues that bodily resurrection is impossible because the same material particles cannot be returned to two different bodies without incompleteness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were the victim in Razi's scenario, would you be content to be resurrected without your original physical matter? What does your answer reveal about what you truly believe defines 'you'?
?
This objection assumes that your identity depends on having the same particles that once composed your body. How does your daily life—your changing cells, your aging—challenge that assumption? Are you comfortable with a resurrection that doesn't restore your exact body?
?
The objection seems to make bodily resurrection logically impossible. But if God can do anything, why does this logical puzzle trouble you? Is your faith strong enough to accept that God's power transcends such dilemmas, or do you need an answer? Spend a moment in prayer about your trust in God's promise.

The Third Objection: Identity and Justice in Bodily Resurrection
Razi presents a dilemma about which body parts are restored.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against bodily resurrection based on the dilemma of which body parts are restored and the resulting moral absurdities.

Core Idea
If God restores all body parts, justice becomes impossible.

Imam Razi presents a powerful dilemma against bodily resurrection. If God restores only the parts at death, then the blind and amputee are resurrected defective. If He restores all parts from the entire life, then rewarding or punishing a person becomes unjust. This passage forces us to think about the nature of identity and divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the structure of the dilemma: two options are presented, each followed by a reason for its invalidity.
The passage uses conditional phrasing (فإما أن... أو) to set up the two possibilities.
2
Identify the two options of the dilemma
Razi states that God either restores (1) the parts exactly as they were at the moment of death, or (2) all the parts that ever belonged to the person during his lifetime. Write down these two options in your own words.
The first option leads to resurrecting defects like blindness or amputation.
3
Analyze why both options fail
For the first option, even agreed-upon beliefs reject resurrecting defective people. For the second, follow the example of a person who changed from fat to thin and changed religious state. Understand how rewarding or punishing the wrong parts would be unjust.
Razi uses two symmetrical cases: Muslim-then-disbeliever and disbeliever-then-Muslim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه تعالى إذا أعاد بدن شخص فإما أن يعيد هذه الأجزاء التي كانت موجودة وقت الموت أو يعيد جملة الأجزاء التي كانت أجزاء له في جميع مدة الحياة، والأول يقتضي أن يعاد الأعمى والأقطع والمجذوم على هذه الصورة، وذلك باطل بالاتفاق، والثاني أيضًا باطل ؛ لأن الإنسان إذا كان وقت إسلامه سمينا، ثم كفر وصار هزيلا فإذا حشر هذا الإنسان سمينا وعذب في النار لزم وصول العذاب إلى تلك الأجزاء التي كانت موصوفة بصفة الإسلام، وذلك ظلم، وعلى العكس من هذا، لو كان كافرًا سمينا ثم أسلم فصار هزيلا لزم إيصال الثواب على الأجزاء الكافرة، وهو محال.
The third: that if He, exalted, restores the body of a person, then either He restores those parts that were present at the time of death, or He restores the totality of parts that were parts of him throughout the entire duration of life. The first requires that the blind, the amputee, and the leper be restored in that condition, and that is invalid by agreement. The second is also invalid, because if a person at the time of his Islam was fat, then he disbelieved and became thin, then if this person is resurrected fat and is tormented in the Fire, it would necessitate that the torment reach those parts that were described with the attribute of Islam, and that is injustice. And contrary to this: if he was a disbeliever fat, then embraced Islam and became thin, it would necessitate giving reward to the disbelieving parts, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against bodily resurrection, focusing on identity over time. Razi lays out a dilemma: if God restores a person's body, He must either restore the exact parts present at the moment of death, or restore all the parts that ever belonged to that person throughout his entire life. The first option leads to absurdity: a person who was blind, an amputee, or a leper at death would be resurrected in that defective state, which is universally rejected. The second option also fails: consider a person who was fat when he was Muslim, then became a disbeliever and thin. If he is resurrected fat (with the parts from his Muslim period), and then tormented in Hell, the torment would afflict those parts that were characterized by Islam, which is unjust. Conversely, if a disbeliever was fat then became Muslim and thin, resurrecting him fat would mean rewarding the parts that were in a state of disbelief, which is impossible. Thus both options lead to contradictions, so bodily resurrection must be rejected.

Key Terms
إعادة البدن
أجزاء
وقت الموت
مدة الحياة
ثواب
عذاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection is a denial of God's power to resurrect bodies.
Correction: Razi is not denying God's power; he is arguing that bodily resurrection would entail logical and moral inconsistencies, so it must be understood in a different way.

Checkpoint
What is one problem with restoring all parts from the entire lifetime, according to the third objection?
A) It would require infinite parts
B) It would lead to rewarding or punishing parts that were in a different religious state
C) It would make the person unrecognizable
D) God does not remember all parts

Key Takeaway
The third objection argues that bodily resurrection leads to either resurrecting defects or rewarding/punishing parts that were in a different religious state, both of which are absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every body part you ever had—including those that committed sin or disbelief—could be held accountable, which parts of your life would you be most ashamed to have reshown? What will you repent from today?
?
The objection assumes that a person's faith and actions are tied to specific body parts. Is every part of your body currently submitting to Allah, or are there 'disbelieving parts' that still act against His commands?
?
If you were resurrected with the body that represents your most consistent state of faith, would it be a body of obedience or hypocrisy? What change must you make this week so that your entire life's parts become those of a true believer?

Fourth Objection: Vapor Particles Moving Between Organs
Razi presents the fourth objection based on vapor particle migration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to bodily resurrection based on the movement of fine vapor particles between organs.

Core Idea
Fine vapor particles disrupt bodily identity, challenging resurrection.

Razi presents a philosophical objection grounded in the humoral theory of the time. He argues that fine vapor particles from each organ can migrate and become part of other organs, making complete reassembly at resurrection impossible. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical flow from premises to conclusion.
The passage is a concise argument; pay attention to 'fa' (then) connectors.
2
Understand the premises
The body is described as a hot, moist substance. When heat affects moisture, fine vapor particles rise. This is the starting assumption.
This relies on the humoral theory prevalent in medieval medicine.
3
Follow the process
These vapor particles rise from every organ and may attach to another organ, becoming part of it. So a single particle can belong to multiple organs over time.
This creates a problem of shared identity.
4
See the dilemma and conclusion
At resurrection, returning the particle to one organ deprives the other. Since restoration of all parts is impossible, resurrection leads to absurdity, so it must be false.
The objection aims to show internal contradiction in the doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : البدن جوهر حار رطب والحرارة إذا أثرت في الرطوبة لزم أن تصعد من الجوهر الرطب أجزاء بخارية لطيفة فعلى هذا التقدير لا بد أن يرتفع عن كل عضو أجزاء بخارية لطيفة، وربما التصق بعضو آخر وصار جزءًا لذلك العضو الآخر، فإذن هذا الجزء الواحد قد كان جزءاً لأحد العضوين، ثم صار للعضو الآخر، ففي زمان الحشر لو أعيد ذلك الجزء إلى العضو الأول ضاع الثاني، ولو أعيد إلى العضو الثاني لضاع العضو الأول، ولما كان القول بالحشر متأديًا إلى هذا الباطل وجب أن يكون باطلا.
The fourth: The body is a hot, moist substance. When heat affects moisture, fine vapor parts necessarily rise from the moist substance. On this basis, fine vapor parts must rise from every organ, and perhaps they attach to another organ and become a part of that other organ. Thus this single part was a part of one of the two organs, then became for the other organ. So at the time of resurrection, if that part is returned to the first organ, the second is lost; and if returned to the second organ, the first is lost. And since the statement of resurrection leads to this absurdity, it must be false.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth philosophical objection, based on the physical composition of the body. Razi describes the body as a hot, moist substance (jawhar hār rāṭib). When heat acts on moisture, fine vapor particles (ajzā' bukhāriyya laṭīfa) rise from each organ. These particles may detach and attach to another organ, becoming part of that organ. Consequently, a single particle may have been part of two different organs at different times. On the Day of Resurrection, if that particle is returned to the first organ, the second organ loses it; if returned to the second, the first loses it. Since restoration of all parts to their original organs is impossible, the doctrine of bodily resurrection is invalid. This objection exploits the ancient medical theory of humors and vapors to argue that bodily continuity is disrupted even within a single lifetime, making perfect reassembly impossible.

Key Terms
جوهر حار رطب
أجزاء بخارية
الحرارة
الرطوبة
التصاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection is a general scientific claim that disproves resurrection.
Correction: The objection relies specifically on the humoral theory of medieval medicine; its force depends on that framework, not on empirical science.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth objection, why can't all parts be restored to their original organs?
A) Because God does not know which organ each part belongs to
B) Because fine vapor particles may have moved between organs and become part of multiple organs
C) Because bodies decompose completely
D) Because the soul has left the body

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection uses the physical theory of vapors to argue that bodily continuity is violated, making resurrection logically impossible within that framework.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a sophisticated argument against your beliefs, like Razi's objection here, what is your first internal reaction—defensiveness, curiosity, or fear? How do you usually handle the tension between what your intellect finds plausible and what your faith affirms?
?
This lesson presents an argument that seems logically sound within its own framework. Have you ever encountered a critique of your faith that you couldn't immediately answer? Did you investigate the underlying assumptions, or did you dismiss it? What does your response reveal about the strength of your conviction?
?
Razi's objection assumes a particular physical theory to disprove resurrection. In your own life, are there areas where you unknowingly assume a worldview that contradicts Islamic teachings? Are you willing to examine those assumptions, even if it means uprooting cherished ideas?

Two Views on Human Identity in Razi's Response
Razi distinguishes two views on human identity to answer the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi categorizes two responses to the objection about recreating the non-existent, based on different views of human identity.

Core Idea
Your view of what a human is determines how you answer the objection.

Razi begins his answer to the first objection by noting that the key issue depends on one's definition of a human being. He distinguishes two groups: those who identify the human with the physical body, and those who see the human as distinct from it. This distinction sets the stage for the arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi sets up the two groups.
The passage is short but foundational.
2
Identify the two groups
Find the two groups described: those who say man is the physical structure (al-haykal) and those who say man is not this structure.
Razi notes the second view is 'al-aqrab' (more correct).
3
Understand the implication
For each group, note what their answer to the objection would be. The first group must prove i'adat al-ma'dum is possible. The second group has two proofs previously mentioned.
This passage sets up the next lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أن نقول : أما الذين قالوا : إن الإنسان هو الهيكل فلا جواب لهم عن هذه الشبهة إلا بيان أن إعادة المعدوم غير ممتنعة، ونحن قد أحكمنا الكلام في هذه المسألة. وأما الذين قالوا : الإنسان ليس هو هذا الهيكل، وهذا هو الأقرب فقد احتجوا عليه بوجهين - أوردناهما فيما تقدم.
This, and the answer to the first objection is that we say: As for those who said that the human being is the [physical] structure (al-haykal), they have no answer to this objection except to show that recreating the non-existent is not impossible. And we have already thoroughly discussed this issue. And as for those who said that the human being is not this structure, and this is the more correct view, they have argued by two points — which we have presented previously.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage begins Razi's response to the first philosophical objection (the impossibility of recreating the non-existent). He divides scholars into two groups based on their view of human identity. The first group holds that the human being is identical to the physical body (al-haykal). For them, the only way to answer the objection is to prove that recreating the non-existent (i'ādat al-ma'dūm) is not impossible. Razi notes that he has already thoroughly discussed this issue (presumably in an earlier section). The second group holds that the human being is not this physical structure; rather, the soul is the true self, and the body is merely an instrument. Razi considers this view to be more correct (al-aqrab). He states that they have argued for their position with two proofs (wajhayn), which have been presented earlier. This sets the stage for Razi to adopt the second position and present its arguments in subsequent passages.

Key Terms
الهيكل
إعادة المعدوم
الإنسان
الجواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Razi rejects the first group entirely, dismissing their view as wrong.
Correction: Razi does not reject the first group outright; he simply notes their only recourse is to argue for the possibility of i'adat al-ma'dum. He acknowledges that the second group's view is 'more correct' (al-aqrab), implying the first is not completely false.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the only way for those who identify man with the physical body to answer the first objection?
A) To deny bodily resurrection altogether
B) To prove that recreating the non-existent is possible
C) To accept the view that man is the soul
D) To argue that the body is not important

Key Takeaway
Razi’s response to the objection hinges on whether the human is identical to the body or not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about who you truly are, do you primarily identify with your physical body or with something deeper? How has that belief shaped your priorities and your relationship with Allah?
?
If your body is not the core of your identity, how does that change your perspective on aging, illness, and death? Are you preparing for the journey of the soul or merely maintaining the structure?
?
Razi notes that one view has a straightforward but controversial answer, while the other is considered closer to the truth. In matters of faith, do you tend to accept the easier explanation or are you willing to engage with deeper proofs, even if they challenge your comfort?

Four Objections Against Bodily Resurrection
Razi enumerates four shubahat (objections) against bodily resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four philosophical objections that Razi presents against the possibility of bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection faces four distinct logical and moral paradoxes.

Razi, a master of dialectical theology, here assembles four formidable objections against the doctrine of bodily resurrection. These challenges are not mere sophistry but deep philosophical puzzles about identity, material continuity, and divine justice. Studying them prepares you to understand how later theologians defended resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Let Razi's own argumentative flow come through before you analyze.
Notice Razi uses a numbered sequence: 'second', 'third', 'fourth'. The first is a reference to earlier premises.
2
Identify each objection
Locate the four objections in the text. The first is about the impossibility of returning the non-existent. The second is the cannibalism paradox. The third concerns identity and moral consistency. The fourth deals with physical part transfer.
The first objection is only referenced, not fully stated here.
3
Understand the logic of each
For each objection, extract the key premises and conclusion. Ask: why does Razi think this leads to impossibility?
For example, the second objection hinges on the fact that consumed body parts cannot belong to two persons simultaneously.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why these objections mattered in Islamic theology. They force defenders of resurrection to address deep issues of identity, justice, and physical continuity.
These objections are not trivial; they shaped the responses of theologians like al-Ghazali and al-Razi himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقدمة الأولى : فقد بينا فيما تقدم أنا سواء قلنا إنه تعالى يعدم الأجزاء ثم يعيدها ، أو قلنا : إنه تعالى يفرقها ثم يركبها فإنه لا بد من القول بصحة إعادة المعدوم. وأما المقدمة الثانية: وهي أن إعادة المعدوم ممتنعة، فقد سبقت حكاية شبهاتهم فيها.
الثاني : إذا قتل إنسان وأكله آخر فقد صارت أجزاء بدن المقتول أجزاء بدن هذا الذي أكله؛ لأن من أكل شيئًا واغتذى به فقد صارت أجزاء الغذاء أجزاء بدن المغتذي، فيوم القيامة لا بد أن ترد تلك الأجزاء إلى بدن أحد هذين الشخصين، فلا بد أن يضيع الثاني، فعلمنا أن القول بحشر الأجسام محال
الثالث: أنه تعالى إذا أعاد بدن شخص فإما أن يعيد هذه الأجزاء التي كانت موجودة وقت الموت أو يعيد جملة الأجزاء التي كانت أجزاء له في جميع مدة الحياة، والأول يقتضي أن يعاد الأعمى والأقطع والمجذوم على هذه الصورة، وذلك باطل بالاتفاق، والثاني أيضًا باطل ؛ لأن الإنسان إذا كان وقت إسلامه سمينا، ثم كفر وصار هزيلا فإذا حشر هذا الإنسان سمينا وعذب في النار لزم وصول العذاب إلى تلك الأجزاء التي كانت موصوفة بصفة الإسلام، وذلك ظلم، وعلى العكس من هذا، لو كان كافرًا سمينا ثم أسلم فصار هزيلا لزم إيصال الثواب على الأجزاء الكافرة، وهو محال. الرابع : البدن جوهر حار رطب والحرارة إذا أثرت في الرطوبة لزم أن تصعد من الجوهر الرطب أجزاء بخارية لطيفة فعلى هذا التقدير لا بد أن يرتفع عن كل عضو أجزاء بخارية لطيفة، وربما التصق بعضو آخر وصار جزءًا لذلك العضو الآخر، فإذن هذا الجزء الواحد قد كان جزءاً لأحد العضوين، ثم صار للعضو الآخر، ففي زمان الحشر لو أعيد ذلك الجزء إلى العضو الأول ضاع الثاني، ولو أعيد إلى العضو الثاني لضاع العضو الأول، ولما كان القول بالحشر متأديًا إلى هذا الباطل وجب أن يكون باطلا. هذا
As for the first premise: we have already explained earlier that whether we say He (exalted) annihilates the parts and then returns them, or we say He separates them and then recombines them, it is necessary to affirm the validity of returning the non-existent. As for the second premise: that returning the non-existent is impossible, the narration of their doubts about it has preceded.
Second: if a person kills another and another person eats him, then the parts of the murdered person's body become parts of the body of the one who ate him; because whoever eats something and nourishes himself, the parts of the food become parts of the nourished person's body. On the Day of Resurrection, those parts must be returned to the body of one of these two persons, so the second must be lost. Thus we know that the claim of the resurrection of bodies is impossible.
Third: if He (exalted) returns a person's body, then either He returns those parts that existed at the time of death, or He returns all the parts that were his parts throughout his entire life. The first would require that the blind, the amputee, and the leper be returned in that form, and that is invalid by consensus. The second is also invalid; because if a person at the time of his Islam was fat, then disbelieved and became thin, then if he is resurrected fat and punished in the Fire, punishment would reach those parts that were characterized by the attribute of Islam, and that is injustice. Conversely, if he was a disbeliever fat, then became Muslim and became thin, it would require delivering reward to the disbelieving parts, which is impossible.
Fourth: the body is a hot, moist substance; when heat affects moisture, it necessitates that fine vaporous parts rise from the moist substance. According to this estimation, fine vaporous parts must rise from each limb, and perhaps they adhere to another limb and become part of that other limb. Thus this single part was once part of one limb, then became part of another limb. At the time of resurrection, if that part is returned to the first limb, the second is lost; if returned to the second limb, the first is lost. Since the claim of resurrection leads to this impossibility, it must be false. This.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage lists four objections (shubahat) against the possibility of bodily resurrection. The first objection is based on the impossibility of returning the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum), referring to earlier discussion. The second objection is the problem of cannibalism: if one person eats another, the parts of the eaten person become part of the eater's body; on resurrection, those parts cannot be returned to both bodies without loss. The third objection concerns identity over time: if God returns only the parts at death, then the resurrected person would be blind, amputee, etc.; if He returns all parts from the whole life, then rewarding or punishing parts that belonged to a different state (e.g., Muslim parts vs. disbeliever parts) leads to injustice. The fourth objection is based on physical change: the body is hot and moist, producing fine vapors that may transfer between limbs; thus a single particle may belong to multiple limbs, making reassignment impossible. The passage concludes that because resurrection leads to these impossibilities, it must be false.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم (i'adat al-ma'dum: returning the non-existent)
حشر الأجسام (hashr al-ajsam: resurrection of bodies)
شبهات (shubahat: doubts/objections)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These objections are trivial sophisms easily dismissed by faith.
Correction: These are serious philosophical challenges that forced theologians to develop sophisticated arguments about identity, justice, and material continuity. Even the strongest defenders of resurrection had to address them carefully.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, why is bodily resurrection impossible in the case of cannibalism?
A) Because the eaten person's parts are annihilated and cannot be restored.
B) Because the parts of the eaten person become parts of the eater, and returning them to both bodies is impossible.
C) Because the soul is separate from the body and does not need resurrection.
D) Because God cannot recreate the same parts twice.

Key Takeaway
Razi raises four distinct logical and moral paradoxes against bodily resurrection, each rooted in problems of identity, material continuity, and divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading these four objections, has any doubt about the bodily resurrection crept into your heart? What is your immediate reaction to that doubt, and what will you do to address it?
?
The third objection suggests that God's action in resurrection could be unjust if parts are rewarded or punished based on a previous state. Do you ever find yourself placing God's wisdom within the limits of human logic? How does this lesson expose that tendency?
?
If someone tomorrow challenges you with the cannibalism problem, will you be able to respond? Commit now to looking up how scholars like Razi himself refuted these objections. What is the first step you will take?

The Human Is Not the Physical Body: Two Proofs
Razi presents two proofs that the human is not the physical body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key proofs that establish the human being as distinct from the physical body, forming the basis for a coherent account of bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
The self is a persistent unity; the body is in constant flux — they cannot be identical.

Razi begins his reply by distinguishing two groups: those who identify the human with the body and those who do not. The latter, he argues, have two powerful proofs showing that the human self is not the same as the physical structure. This lesson examines these proofs, setting the stage for Razi's own positive theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Read the opening lines where Razi classifies responses to the objection. Notice which group he favors and why.
The first group has no answer unless they prove the possibility of returning the non-existent.
2
Study the first proof
Look at the argument from persistence: the self remains one throughout life, while the body constantly changes. Understand why this implies distinctness.
This is an argument from identity over time.
3
Study the second proof
Consider the argument from self-awareness: one can be aware of oneself while unaware of one's body parts. The known and unknown must be different.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: if the self is not the body, what is it? How does this help solve the problem of bodily resurrection?
This will be answered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أن نقول : أما الذين قالوا : إن الإنسان هو الهيكل فلا جواب لهم عن هذه الشبهة إلا بيان أن إعادة المعدوم غير ممتنعة، ونحن قد أحكمنا الكلام في هذه المسألة.
وأما الذين قالوا : الإنسان ليس هو هذا الهيكل، وهذا هو الأقرب فقد احتجوا عليه بوجهين - أوردناهما فيما تقدم.
أحدهما : أن الإنسان شيء واحد باق من أول عمره إلى آخر عمره، وهذا الهيكل غير باق من أول العمر إلى آخره، فالإنسان شيء مغاير لهذا الهيكل. والثاني : أن الإنسان قد يكون عالما بذاته المخصوصة حال ما يكون غافلا جميع أجزائه الظاهرة والباطنة والمعلوم مغاير لما ليس بمعلوم.
وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن الشيء الذي هو الإنسان في الحقيقة أجزاء لطيفة سارية في هذا البدن باقية من أول العمر إلى آخره، إما لأجل أن تلك الأجسام اللطيفة مخالفة بالماهية لهذه الأجسام العنصرية الكائنة الفاسدة المتحللة - وتلك الأجسام حية لذواتها مضيئة شفافة، فلا كانت مصونة عن التبدل والتحلل - وإما لأنها وإن كانت مساوية لهذه الأجسام العنصرية في الماهية إلا أن الفاعل المختار صانها عن التغير والانحلال بقدرته، وجعلها باقية دائمة أول العمر إلى آخره.
ثم عند الموت تنفصل تلك الأجزاء الجسمانية التي هي الإنسان وتبقى على حالتها حية مدركة عاقلة فاهمة وتتخلص إما إلى منازل السعداء كما قال الله تعالى: {وَلَا تَحْسَبَنَّ الَّذِينَ قُتِلُوا فِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ أَمْوَاتًا بَلْ أَحْيَاءٌ عِندَ رَبِّهِمْ} [آل عمران: ١٦٩] وإما إلى منازل الأشقياء كما قال الله تعالى: {النَّارُ يُعْرَضُونَ عَلَيْهَا غُدُوا وَعَشِيًّا} [غافر: ٤٦] ثم إنه تعالى يضم يوم القيامة إلى هذه الأجزاء الأصلية التي هي الإنسان في الحقيقة أجزاء أخر زائدة كما فعل ذلك في الدنيا، ويوصل الثواب والعقاب إليه، وعلى هذا التفسير لا يبقى في المعاد الجسماني شبهة وإشكال أصلا البتة.
وعلى هذا التقدير يكون المثاب والمعاقب في القيامة عين من كان مطيعا وعاصيا في الدنيا، وهذا على القول بأن الإنسان جسم مخصوص سار في هذا البدن، وأما على قول من يقول : الإنسان عبارة عن جوهر مجرد عن الحجمية والمقدار فالقول فيه أيضًا هكذا فظهر أن على هذا التلخيص لا تبقى شبهة البتة في صحة القول بالمعاد الجسماني.
The answer to the first doubt: we say: as for those who said that the human being is the [physical] structure, they have no answer to this doubt except to demonstrate that returning the non-existent is not impossible, and we have already thoroughly discussed this issue.
As for those who said that the human being is not this structure, and this is the more correct view, they have argued for it with two proofs – which we have presented earlier.
The first: that the human being is one thing that persists from the beginning of his life to its end, while this structure does not persist from the beginning of life to its end; so the human being is something other than this structure.
The second: that the human being may be aware of his specific self while being unaware of all his parts, both outward and inward; the known is other than what is not known.
And when this is established, we say: why is it not permissible to say that the thing which is the human being in reality is subtle particles permeating this body, persisting from the beginning of life to its end? Either because those subtle bodies are different in essence from these elemental, generated, corruptible, dissolving bodies – and those bodies are alive by themselves, luminous, transparent, so they are protected from change and dissolution – or because, although they are equal to these elemental bodies in essence, yet the free Agent protects them from change and dissolution by His power, and makes them persistent and lasting from the beginning of life to its end.
Then at death, those bodily parts which are the human being separate and remain in their state, alive, perceiving, rational, understanding, and depart either to the abodes of the blessed, as God said: {And do not think that those who are killed in the way of Allah are dead; rather, they are alive with their Lord} [Aal-e-Imran 3:169], or to the abodes of the wretched, as God said: {The Fire, they are exposed to it morning and evening} [Ghafir 40:46]. Then on the Day of Resurrection, He (exalted) joins to these original parts which are the human being in reality additional other parts, as He did in this world, and delivers reward and punishment to it. According to this interpretation, there remains no doubt or difficulty in bodily resurrection at all.
On this estimation, the rewarded and punished one on the Day of Resurrection is the very one who was obedient and disobedient in the world. This is according to the view that the human being is a specific body permeating this [physical] body. As for the view of those who say that the human being is a substance abstracted from volume and magnitude, the statement is also like this. So it becomes clear that on this summary, there remains no doubt at all about the validity of the doctrine of bodily resurrection.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's answer to the first objection against bodily resurrection. He begins by distinguishing two positions: those who identify the human being with the physical body (al-haykal) have no escape except to prove the possibility of returning the non-existent, which Razi says he has already established. The more correct view, he argues, is that the human being is not the physical body. He gives two proofs for this: first, the human self is a persistent unity from birth to death, while the physical body constantly changes; therefore the self is distinct from the body. Second, one can be aware of one's own self while being unaware of one's body parts, indicating that the self is known and the body is not. Having established this, Razi proposes that the real human being consists of subtle particles (ajza' latifa) that permeate the body and persist throughout life. These particles are either different in essence from the elemental bodies (thus not subject to corruption) or, though similar, are preserved by God's power. At death, these particles separate and remain alive and conscious, going to intermediate abodes as indicated by Quranic verses. On resurrection, God will add other parts to these original particles to form a complete body, and reward or punishment will be given to this enduring self. This solves the identity problem: the same person is rewarded or punished. Razi notes that this explanation works both for the view that the human is a subtle body and for the view that the human is a separate substance (jism mujarrad).

Key Terms
الهيكل (al-haykal: the physical structure)
إعادة المعدوم (i'adat al-ma'dum: returning the non-existent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The human being is identical to the physical body.
Correction: Razi shows that the self persists while the body changes, and one can be aware of the self without knowing the body, so they are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the first proof that the human being is not the physical body?
A) The physical body is composed of four elements, but the human self is simple.
B) The human being persists as one thing from birth to death, while the physical body changes constantly.
C) The human being has knowledge of God, but the body does not.
D) The physical body dies and decays, while the soul is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The self's persistence and self-awareness demonstrate that the human being is not reducible to the physical body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look in the mirror and see your body ageing, does a part of you feel that the real 'you' remains unchanged? How does Razi's first proof affirm or challenge that feeling? What would it mean for your daily life if you truly believed your identity is not tied to your physical form?
?
Think of a moment when you were deeply focused—maybe in prayer or study—and you forgot about your body. Did you feel more 'yourself' then? According to Razi's second proof, that awareness of self without awareness of body parts shows the self is distinct. How can you use that insight to strengthen your connection with Allah, who is aware of your innermost self?
?
If your body is not the essence of who you are, then what part of you will you be accountable for on the Day of Judgment? Does that change what you prioritize today? What is one concrete step you can take this week to nurture your soul over your body?

The Subtle Particles and the Mechanism of Bodily Resurrection
Razi expounds his theory of subtle particles and the resurrection process.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's theory of subtle particles and how it provides a coherent account of bodily resurrection, satisfying identity and continuity.

Core Idea
The real human consists of subtle particles that persist through life, death, and into the afterlife.

Having established that the human is not the physical body, Razi now proposes his positive theory: the human is a set of subtle particles permeating the body. These particles are immune to corruption and survive death, later re-embodied on the Day of Resurrection. This lesson unpacks that theory and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the subtle particles proposal
Read the section where Razi says the human is 'ajza' latifa' permeating the body. Note the two possible explanations for their persistence: different essence or divine preservation.
2
Understand the afterlife process
Follow the sequence: death separates the particles, they go to intermediate abodes, then on resurrection God adds other parts to them for re-embodiment.
Razi quotes Quranic verses for support.
3
Consider the identity issue
Note how this theory ensures that the same person who was obedient is the same who is rewarded or punished.
4
See how it resolves doubts
Read the concluding sentences where Razi says this removes all doubts about bodily resurrection, whether one holds the subtle body view or the abstract substance view.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception: Razi denies bodily resurrection entirely.

Razi's Response to the Annihilation of the Soul
Razi answers the objection that the soul must perish like all contingent things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi distinguishes between the rational possibility of annihilation and its actual occurrence, and how he uses the principle of specification of general texts to defend the survival of the soul.

Core Idea
General rules may be specified; the soul's survival is a revealed exception.

In this passage, Razi tackles a powerful objection: if everything other than God perishes, why not the soul? His answer hinges on a subtle but crucial distinction between rational possibility and actual occurrence, and the role of revelation in specifying general principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the challenge: if all contingent things perish, the soul must too, undermining resurrection.
2
Notice Razi's distinction
He separates rational possibility (ja'iz al-'adam) from actual occurrence, which is known through revelation.
3
Learn the principle of takhsis al-'umum
Specification of general texts by specific evidence is a key methodological tool in kalam.
4
See the appeal to revelation
Razi cites Quran and prophetic reports as evidence for the soul's survival.
The answer is cut off in the source, but the direction is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ألستم قد دللتم على أن كل ما سوى الله تعالى فإنه يفنى ويصير معدوماً، وعلى هذا التقدير فالنفس الإنسانية أيضًا تفنى وتعدم وحينئذ يعود الإشكال. قلنا : نحن قد أثبتنا بالدليل العقلي أن كل ما سوى الله تعالى فإنه جائز العدم، أما أن هذا الجائز يقع فإنا إنما عولنا فيه على ظواهر العمومات، وتخصيص العموم بالدليل جائز وثبت بنصوص القرآن والأخبار المتواترة عن الأنبياء - صلوات الله
If it is said: have you not proven that everything other than God (exalted) perishes and becomes non-existent? And on this estimation, the human soul also perishes and becomes non-existent, and then the difficulty returns. We say: we have proven by rational proof that everything other than God (exalted) is possible of non-existence. As for the occurrence of this possible, we have relied only on the apparent meanings of general texts [al-‘umumat], and specifying the general by evidence is permissible, and it has been established by the texts of the Quran and the widely transmitted reports from the prophets – prayers of Allah
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents a final objection and its incomplete answer. The objector argues that if everything other than God is destined to perish and become non-existent (as Razi has argued in other contexts), then the human soul must also perish. This would revive the original problem of bodily resurrection because there would be no persisting self to be resurrected. Razi's response begins by acknowledging the rational principle: everything other than God is possible (ja'iz) of non-existence – that is, its non-existence is conceivable. However, he distinguishes between possibility and actual occurrence. The claim that all things actually become non-existent is based on general scriptural statements (zawahir al-‘umumat). But it is permissible to specify a general text with evidence (takhsis al-‘umum bi’l-dalil). He then appeals to specific Quranic texts and prophetic reports (ahl al-tawatur) that indicate the soul's survival. The answer is cut off, but the direction of the argument is clear: the general statements about annihilation are qualified by revelation regarding the soul.

Key Terms
جائز العدم (ja'iz al-'adam)
تخصيص العموم (takhsis al-'umum)
ظواهر العمومات (zawahir al-'umumat)
النفس الإنسانية (al-nafs al-insaniyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi claims the soul is eternal or necessary being.
Correction: Razi does not claim that the soul is eternal or necessary; rather, it is possible but preserved by God's specific decree as per revelation.

Checkpoint
How does Razi respond to the objection that the human soul must perish like all contingent things?
A) He denies that the soul is contingent.
B) He argues that the general rule of annihilation applies only to bodies, not souls.
C) He says the general texts are subject to specification by specific evidence from revelation.
D) He claims the soul is necessary being and therefore cannot perish.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends the soul's survival by distinguishing rational possibility from actual occurrence, and by specifying general annihilation verses with specific revelation.

Two Proofs That the Human Self Is Distinct from the Body
Razi presents two rational proofs that the human self is distinct from the physical body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational arguments demonstrating that the human soul is distinct from the physical body.

Core Idea
You are not your body — two proofs show the self is constant and self-aware.

Imam Razi presents two concise rational proofs to establish that the human self (nafs) is not identical to the physical body. These arguments rely on everyday observations: the continuity of personal identity despite bodily change, and the ability to be aware of oneself while unaware of one's own body parts. This lesson unpacks both proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the full Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the structure: two proofs introduced with 'أحدهما' and 'والثاني'.
The passage is in classical kalam style.
2
Analyze the first proof
Focus on the argument: the human is a single thing persisting from birth to death, while the body constantly changes. Therefore, the human is distinct from the body.
This is an argument from constancy versus change.
3
Analyze the second proof
Consider the state where a person is aware of their own essence but unaware of their external and internal parts. Since the known (self) is other than the unknown (parts), the self is distinct.
This is an argument from self-awareness.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Both proofs point to the same conclusion: the human self is a non-physical entity separate from the body. Reflect on how these arguments establish the existence of the soul independently.
5
Test your understanding
Use the checkpoint question to check if you grasp the key point of the first proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما : أن الإنسان شيء واحد باق من أول عمره إلى آخر عمره، وهذا الهيكل غير باق من أول العمر إلى آخره، فالإنسان شيء مغاير لهذا الهيكل. والثاني : أن الإنسان قد يكون عالما بذاته المخصوصة حال ما يكون غافلا جميع أجزائه الظاهرة والباطنة والمعلوم مغاير لما ليس بمعلوم.
One of them: that the human is one thing remaining from the beginning of his life to the end of his life, and this structure is not remaining from the beginning of life to its end, so the human is something other than this structure. And the second: that the human may be knowing his specific essence while being unaware of all his external and internal parts, and the known is other than what is not known.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents two rational proofs that the human self (nafs) is distinct from the physical body. The first proof: the human is a single, continuous entity from birth to death, whereas the physical body (haykal) constantly changes and is not the same from beginning to end. Therefore, the human being is something other than this transient body. The second proof: a human can be aware of his own self (makhsusa) at a moment when he is completely unaware of all his external and internal parts. Since he knows his self but not his parts, the known self must be different from the parts that are not known. Thus, the self is distinct from the body.

Key Terms
نفس
هيكل
ذات مخصوصة
مغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The human self is identical to the physical body, as bodily changes are just part of personal identity.
Correction: Razi argues that the self is constant while the body changes, and self-awareness can occur without awareness of body parts, so the self is distinct.

Checkpoint

A) The self remains constant while the body changes
B) The self can be aware of itself without awareness of body parts
C) The body is composed of four elements
D) Both A and B are the proofs

Key Takeaway
Razi uses two simple rational arguments—constancy of self and self-awareness—to prove that the human soul is not identical to the physical body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Think of a moment when you were deeply absorbed in thought, unaware of your heartbeat or breathing. Did that experience make you feel more ‘you’ than usual? If your self can be present without awareness of your body, what does that say about where your true focus should be? Commit to one change in your daily routine that prioritizes your soul over your body.
?
If your self is distinct and survives the body, how should that reshape your fear of death? Are you more afraid of losing your body or losing your self? Write down one concrete way you can prepare your soul for what comes after.
