Three Views on the Soul's Survival
Razi classifies three views on the soul's survival after death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions on the survival of rational souls after death as classified by Razi, and why he considers only one view as held by genuine investigators.

Core Idea
Only the view that all rational souls survive has scholarly weight; the other views are untenable.

Razi opens his discussion on spiritual resurrection by first establishing the premise that the soul is immaterial. He then presents a threefold classification of opinions on what happens to the soul after death. This classification is not just historical—it reveals the theological stakes: if the soul perishes, spiritual resurrection becomes meaningless. Read carefully to see which positions Razi endorses and which he dismisses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing to analyze. Then read the English translation to get a clear sense of the three groups Razi mentions.
Notice the concise structure: he states the premise, then lists the three groups.
2
Identify the three groups
Find each group's position: (1) all rational souls survive, (2) all perish, (3) only virtuous survive. Note which group Razi associates with 'most investigators'.
Razi explicitly dismisses the second group as having no support among investigators.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: why does Razi reject the second view so strongly? What does that say about the necessity of the soul's survival for spiritual resurrection?
This rejection sets the stage for the subsequent discussion on which specific souls survive.
4
Consider the third group
The third group restricts survival to virtuous souls. Think about what 'virtuous' means here—those who perfected their theoretical power.
This introduces a qualitative difference among souls, which will be important later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس : في المعاد الروحاني اعلم أن هذا البحث متفرع على إثبات أن النفس جوهر مجرد، ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني، فنقول : القائلون بإثبات هذه النفس فرق ثلاث : أحدها : الذين يقولون : جملة النفوس الناطقة باقية بعد الموت، وهو قول أكثر المحققين من الحكماء. وثانيها : الذين يقولون : النفوس الناطقة تفنى عند فناء الأبدان، وهذا قول لم يقل به أحد من المحققين. وثالثها : الذين يقولون : النفوس الفاضلة تبقى وهي التي استكملت قوتها النظرية
Chapter Six: On Spiritual Resurrection. Know that this investigation branches off from the proof that the soul is a pure substance, neither a body nor corporeal. We say: Those who affirm this soul are three groups: The first are those who say: All rational souls remain after death; this is the view of most investigators among the philosophers. The second are those who say: The rational souls perish with the perishing of bodies; this is a view that none of the investigators have held. The third are those who say: The virtuous souls remain—those that have perfected their theoretical power.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on spiritual resurrection (al-ma'ad al-ruhani). Razi notes that this topic depends on prior proof that the soul (nafs) is a pure substance (jawhar mujarrad)—i.e., immaterial, not a body and not bodily. He then classifies those who affirm this soul into three groups. The first group holds that all rational souls (al-nufus al-natiqa) survive bodily death; this is the position of most investigators (al-muhaqqiqun) among the philosophers (al-hukama'). The second group holds that rational souls perish when the body perishes; Razi dismisses this as a view that no real investigator holds. The third group holds that only virtuous souls survive—those that have perfected their theoretical power (quwwatuhum al-nazariyya). This classification sets the stage for further discussion on which view is correct and what the nature of spiritual resurrection entails.

Key Terms
نفس ناطقة
جوهر مجرد
نفس فاضلة
قوة نظرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The survival of the soul is a matter of mere philosophical speculation with no theological importance.
Correction: Razi presents it as a theological topic that requires prior proof (the soul is immaterial) and classifies views to determine the correct basis for spiritual resurrection.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's classification, which group of philosophers holds that all rational souls survive after death?
A) The second group, who say souls perish with the body.
B) The third group, who say only virtuous souls remain.
C) The first group, which includes most investigators.
D) No group holds this view.

Key Takeaway
Razi classifies three views on the soul's survival, endorsing the view that all rational souls survive as the position of most investigators, and dismissing the idea that souls perish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only virtuous souls survive, what does that mean for you? Have you honestly assessed whether your soul is among them, or have you assumed survival is automatic?
?
Razi says the view that souls perish is held by no serious investigator. Yet many live as if this life is all that matters. Which view does your daily life actually reflect—and does that match what you claim to believe?
?
The soul's survival is not a philosophical luxury; it is the foundation of every eternal hope or fear. If you truly believed your rational soul will live on, what would you change about today?

Three Views on the Survival of the Rational Soul After Death
Razi classifies the opinions of philosophers on whether the rational soul survives death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions among philosophers regarding whether the rational soul survives death, and which view is held by the verifiers.

Core Idea
The survival of the soul is not a single debate—there are three distinct positions.

Imam al-Razi begins the sixth chapter on spiritual resurrection by reminding us that this discussion rests on a prior proof: the soul is a simple, non-material substance. Only then does he classify the opinions among those who affirm the soul. In this passage, he presents three groups, ranging from universal survival to selective survival to universal annihilation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic and English side by side to get a sense of the classification.
Notice the three groups are introduced with 'one of them... second... third'.
2
Identify the three groups
List each group and the key criterion they use for survival or annihilation.
The third group distinguishes between virtuous and deficient souls.
3
Understand 'verifiers' (المحققين)
Note that Razi uses the term 'verifiers' to indicate the authoritative philosophers. Which view do they hold?
It is the first view: all rational souls are immortal.
4
Reflect on why this classification matters
Consider how these positions lead to different theological implications about justice, resurrection, and the purpose of life.
This classification sets the stage for Razi's defense of universal survival.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس : في المعاد الروحاني. اعلم أن هذا البحث متفرع على إثبات أن النفس جوهر مجرد، ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني، فنقول : القائلون بإثبات هذه النفس فرق ثلاث : أحدها : الذين يقولون : جملة النفوس الناطقة باقية بعد الموت، وهو قول أكثر المحققين من الحكماء. وثانيها : الذين يقولون : النفوس الناطقة تفنى عند فناء الأبدان، وهذا قول لم يقل به أحد من المحققين. وثالثها : الذين يقولون : النفوس الفاضلة تبقى وهي التي استكملت قوتها النظرية بمعرفة الحق وقوتها العملية بعمل الخير، وأما النفوس التي لا تكون كذلك فإنها تفنى عند فناء الأبدان.
The Sixth Chapter: On the Spiritual Resurrection. Know that this investigation is branched upon establishing that the soul is a simple substance, not a body nor corporeal. So we say: Those who affirm this soul are three groups: First: those who say: all rational souls subsist after death — this is the view of most of the verifiers among the philosophers. Second: those who say: the rational souls perish upon the perishing of the bodies — this is a view that none of the verifiers have held. Third: those who say: the virtuous souls remain — those which have perfected their theoretical power by knowledge of the truth and their practical power by doing good — and as for souls that are not like that, they perish upon the perishing of the bodies.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a classification. It introduces the sixth chapter on spiritual resurrection, noting that the discussion depends on first establishing that the soul is a simple (non-material) substance. Then it enumerates three positions among philosophers who affirm the existence of the soul (i.e., those who do not deny it outright). The first position, held by most verifiers (المحققين), is that all rational souls (النفوس الناطقة) survive bodily death. The second position, which the author dismisses as not held by any verifier, is that all souls perish with the body. The third position distinguishes between souls: virtuous souls that have perfected both their theoretical faculty (through knowledge of truth) and practical faculty (through good deeds) remain, while deficient souls perish. This classification sets up the subsequent arguments where the author defends the first view and refutes the second.

Key Terms
المعاد الروحاني
النفس الناطقة
جوهر مجرد
القوة النظرية
القوة العملية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Razi himself holds the third view (only virtuous souls survive).
Correction: Razi presents the third view but later defends the first view (all souls survive) and refutes the second.

Checkpoint
According to the classification in this passage, which view do most verifiers among the philosophers hold?
A) All rational souls perish with the body.
B) All rational souls subsist after death.
C) Only virtuous souls subsist after death.
D) Only wicked souls perish.

Key Takeaway
The philosophical debate on the afterlife among those who affirm the soul revolves around three distinct positions: universal survival (majority), universal annihilation (rejected), and selective survival (conditional on virtue).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the survival of your soul depended on your having perfected your theoretical and practical powers, where would you stand right now? What specific deficiency would you most urgently need to address?
?
The 'verifiers' (المحققين) hold that all rational souls survive death. Does that truth comfort you, or does it make you uneasy about facing the accountability of the hereafter?
?
The third view makes survival conditional on knowing truth and doing good. If that were the reality, what would you change about how you spend your time this week?

Two Proofs That the Soul Is Not Eternal
Imam Razi presents two arguments for the temporal origination of the human soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's two arguments that the human soul cannot be eternal: the impossibility of an actual infinite number of souls, and the soul's intrinsic possibility of non-existence.

Core Idea
The soul is not eternal: infinite souls cannot exist, and its essence admits non-existence.

In this passage, Imam Razi builds on his earlier discussion to prove that the human soul, though originated, cannot be eternal (azali). He offers two distinct arguments: one from the logical impossibility of an infinite number of souls, and another from the soul's own nature as something that came into being after non-existence. Both lead to the conclusion that the soul is not eternal and that its permanence cannot be asserted with certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without overthinking. Notice the flow: 'إذا ثبت هذا فنقول...' introduces the claim, then 'ويدل عليه وجهان' signals two proofs.
The passage is dense; take your time with each sentence.
2
Identify the first proof
Find the first 'الوجه الأول' and trace the argument: celestial cycles are beginningless → bodies are generated infinitely → if souls survive, infinite souls now → impossible because every number is finite.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming eternal souls leads to a contradiction.
3
Identify the second proof
Find 'الوجه الثاني' and follow: souls came from non-existence → their essence is receptive to non-existence → this receptivity is permanent → we cannot be certain they will never perish.
This argument draws on the soul's contingency, not on external causes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how both proofs together establish that the soul is not eternal. The first proof attacks the possibility of its everlasting existence backward (no beginning), the second attacks its forward permanence.
The passage ends with a strong conclusion: 'امتنع القطع بأنها لا تعدم البتة' – we cannot be certain it will never perish.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : وجب أن لا تكون أبدية، ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول : أن سبب حدوث النفس عن العلل الفلكية العالية ليس إلا حدوث الأبدان المستعدة لقبولها لمن دورات الفلك لا أول لها فحدوث الأبدان لا أول له، فحدوث النفوس لا أول له فلو كانت النفوس باقية لزم أن يكون قد حصل الآن نفوس غير متناهية وذلك محال ؛ لأن كل عدد موجود فإنه قابل للزيادة والنقصان، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه. الثاني : أن هذه النفوس لما وجدت بعد أن كانت معدومة كانت ماهياتها قابلة العدم، وهذه القابلية من لوازمها فوجب أن تكون قابلة للعدم أبداً، وإذا كان كذلك امتنع القطع بأنها لا تعدم البتة.
When this is established, we say: It is necessary that it [the soul] not be eternal. This is supported by two aspects: First: The cause of the generation of the soul from the high celestial causes is only the generation of bodies prepared to receive it, because the cycles of the heavens have no beginning, so the generation of bodies has no beginning, so the generation of souls has no beginning. If the souls were permanent, it would necessitate that there exist now infinite souls, and that is impossible, because every existing number is receptive to increase and decrease, and whatever is like that is finite. Second: Since these souls came into existence after having been non-existent, their essences are receptive to non-existence, and this receptivity is among their necessary concomitants, so it is necessary that they be receptive to non-existence forever. When this is so, it is impossible to be certain that they will never become non-existent.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs that the human soul is not eternal (azali) but originated (haditha). The first proof is based on infinite regress: the soul's generation depends on the body, and since celestial cycles are beginningless, bodies have been generated infinitely, leading to an infinite number of souls if each soul survived. But an infinite actual number is impossible because any number we consider can be increased or decreased—a sign of finitude. The second proof is based on the soul's intrinsic possibility of non-existence: once it existed after not existing, its essence is open to annihilation; this possibility never leaves it, so we cannot be certain it will never perish. The passage concludes that both proofs show the soul cannot be eternal.

Key Terms
النفس
أزلية
أبدية
حدوث
عدد غير متناه
قابلية العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the soul is eternal because it is immaterial and simple.
Correction: Razi argues that even if the soul is immaterial, its generation from celestial causes and its coming into existence after non-existence show that it is not eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why would an infinite number of souls be impossible?
A) Because souls are immaterial and cannot be counted.
B) Because every existing number is finite due to its capacity for increase and decrease.
C) Because souls are not subject to generation and corruption.
D) Because the celestial cycles are finite.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's two proofs demonstrate that the human soul is not eternal: the first shows that an infinite number of souls is impossible, and the second shows that the soul's intrinsic possibility of non-existence prevents certainty of its perpetual existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is not eternal and its essence includes the possibility of non-existence, what does that mean for your attachment to this life? Are you living as if your soul is temporary or permanent?
?
The first proof shows that an infinite number of souls cannot exist. Does that affect how you view the value of each individual soul, including your own? How does that change the urgency of guiding others?
?
Since the soul's perpetuity is not guaranteed by its nature, on what do you base your hope of eternal happiness? Have you truly considered that your soul's continued existence depends on something beyond itself?

First Objection: Could Souls Be Pre-Eternal?
Imam Razi presents and refutes the objection that souls may be pre-eternal and distinct by essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the argument that souls are originated, and how Imam Razi refutes it by challenging unsupported claims and exposing circularity in Ibn Sina's position.

Core Idea
The objection that souls could be pre-eternal fails because essence-distinction and metempsychosis arguments are unsound.

Imam Razi presents the first objection to his proof that souls are not pre-eternal. The objector suggests that souls could be multiple and pre-eternal, distinguished by essence. Imam Razi refutes this by challenging the unsupported claims and exposing a circularity in Ibn Sina's argument against metempsychosis. This lesson will guide you through the exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening where the objector denies that souls cannot be pre-eternal, proposing they could be multiple by essence.
Notice the objector challenges the very premise that souls are originated.
2
Understand the author's response
The author first dismisses the claim that all human souls are one species as pure assertion. Then he challenges the idea that distinction cannot be by accidental attributes.
The author argues that the objector's assumptions are not proven.
3
Examine the circularity claim
The author points out that Ibn Sina's argument against metempsychosis depends on the soul's origination, while his argument for origination depends on rejecting metempsychosis—creating a vicious circle.
This undermines the objection's reliance on metempsychosis as an alternative.
4
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how the objection's premises are unsupported, and its logical structure contains circularity. The author shows the objection does not stand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الأول في الاعتراض: لا نسلم أنها ليست أزلية ولم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنها في الأزل متعددة، وكان امتياز بعضها عن بعض بنفس الماهية، وقولهم : النفوس البشرية واحدة بالنوع فهو محض الدعوى. لا يقال : هب أنها ليست واحدة في النوع لكن لاشك أن كل نفس تفرض فإنها قد تحصل نفس أخرى لتساويها في تمام الماهية، وحينئذ يتم الدليل. لأنا نقول : هذا تمسك بالظن، ولم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن كل نفسين موجودين فهما مختلفان بالماهية إلا أن اختلافهما في الماهية لا يمنع من اشتراكهما في صفات كثيرة ؟ وأيضًا لم لا يجوز أن يقال: كل واحد من تلك الأعداد يمتاز بعضها عن بعض بالصفات العرضية ؟ قوله : "التباين بالصفات العرضية لا يحصل إلا بسبب اختلاف المواد" قلنا هذا ممنوع فما الدليل عليه ؟ سلمنا ذلك، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : النفس قبل تعلقها بهذا البدن كانت ببدن آخر - كما يقوله أصحاب التناسخ ؟ واعلم أن الشيخ أبا علي بن سينا إنما أبطل التناسخ بدليل مبني على حدوث النفس، ودليله على حدوث النفس مبني على بطلان التناسخ - كما بيناه ههنا - فوقع الدور.
The first aspect of objection: We do not accept that they [the souls] are not pre-eternal. Why is it not permissible to say: They were multiple in pre-eternity, and their distinction from each other was by their very essence? And their statement: 'Human souls are one in species' is pure assertion. It should not be said: 'Suppose they are not one in species, but there is no doubt that every soul you suppose can be matched by another soul equal to it in complete essence, and then the proof is complete.' For we say: This is an appeal to conjecture. Why is it not permissible to say: Every two souls that exist are different in essence, yet their difference in essence does not prevent them from sharing many attributes? Also, why is it not permissible to say: Each one of those numbers is distinguished from some others by accidental attributes? His statement: 'Distinction by accidental attributes occurs only through difference of substrates'—we say this is denied; what is the evidence for it? We concede that, but why is it not permissible to say: The soul before its attachment to this body was with another body, as the proponents of metempsychosis claim? And know that Shaykh Abu Ali ibn Sina invalidated metempsychosis only by a proof based on the origination of the soul, and his proof for the origination of the soul is based on the invalidity of metempsychosis—as we have shown here—so a circularity occurred.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to the author's proof that souls are not pre-eternal. The objector suggests souls could be pre-eternal and multiple, distinguished by their very essences, so the argument that an infinite number would accumulate fails. The author refutes this by noting that the claim that all human souls are one in species is not proven; even if they are not, the objector cannot assume that any two souls are exactly alike in essence. Distinction by accidental attributes is also possible, and the claim that such distinction requires different substrates is denied. Furthermore, the author points out that Ibn Sina's argument against metempsychosis (transmigration) relies on the soul's temporal origination, while his argument for origination relies on rejecting metempsychosis—a circularity. Thus the objection is not sound.

Key Terms
أزلية
امتياز
ماهية
صفات عرضية
تناسخ
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is strong because it offers plausible alternative possibilities (souls pre-eternal, metempsychosis).
Correction: The objection fails because the offered possibilities are unproven assumptions or rely on circular reasoning; mere possibility is insufficient to refute a proof.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi consider Ibn Sina's argument on metempsychosis and the soul's origination to be circular?
A) Because it assumes the soul is both immaterial and eternal.
B) Because it uses the origination of the soul to disprove metempsychosis and then uses the invalidity of metempsychosis to prove origination.
C) Because it relies on empirical evidence from recollection.
D) Because it defines the soul as a substance without matter.

Key Takeaway
The first objection to souls being originated fails because its claims about essence-distinction are unproven, and the argument from metempsychosis involves circular reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the objector simply 'denies' the premise without evidence, does your own heart ever dismiss divine truth because it feels uncomfortable or inconvenient? What would it take for you to submit to a proof even when it challenges your assumptions?
?
The argument against metempsychosis is shown to be circular. Are there beliefs you hold about yourself—about your worth, your past, or your future—that rely on circular reasoning rather than the clear truth of revelation? Will you honestly examine them today?
?
Razi calls the objector's claim 'pure assertion'. How often do you make assertions about your own soul—'I can't change', 'this is just how I am'—without evidence? If your soul is originated by Allah and not pre-eternal, what does that mean for your ability to transform through His guidance?

Second Objection: Memory of Past Lives
Imam Razi refutes the memory objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's response to the objection that if souls are pre-eternal, we would remember past lives.

Core Idea
Memory requires a body; without it, no recollection.

The philosopher argues that if souls were eternal, we should recall past experiences. Imam Razi challenges this inductive argument by pointing out that recollection is tied to the body in which the experience occurred. Once that body perishes, memory is impossible. Let's examine his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, focusing on the philosopher's claim and Imam Razi's reply.
Notice that Imam Razi uses the term 'استقراء' (induction) to characterize the argument.
2
Identify the key premise
Imam Razi argues that recollection requires attachment to a specific body. Find where he states this condition.
The condition is 'مشروط بتعلق النفس بالبدن'.
3
Consider the refutation
Why does this condition make the objection weak? Think about what happens when the body perishes.
If the body is gone, the condition is not met, so memory is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثانية - وهي قوله : الأرواح لو كانت قديمة لكنا نتذكر الآن شيئًا من الأحوال الماضية - قلنا : هذا تمسك بالاستقراء، وهو ضعيف أيضًا، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : تذكر النفس للأحوال الماضية مشروط بتعلق النفس بالبدن الذي معه حدثت تلك الأحوال، فإذا فني ذلك البدن امتنع تذكر الأحوال التي حصلت مع ذلك البدن. الوجه الثاني في الاعتراض على هذه الحجة سلمنا أنها أزلية، فلم قلتم بأنها لا تكون أبدية؟ قوله : لو كانت أبدية لكانت النفوس الموجودة الآن غير متناهية. قلنا : هذا بناء على أن الأدوار الماضية غير متناهية وقد بينا إبطال ذلك في مسألة الحدوث. سلمنا ، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال: حصل الآن نفوس غير متناهية ؟ قوله : كل عدد موجود فهو قابل للزيادة والنقصان وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه. قلنا : هذه المقدمة فيها بحث دقيق ذكرناه في الكتب الفلسفية.
As for the second argument—which is his statement: 'If the spirits were pre-eternal, we would now remember something of past states'—we say: This is an appeal to induction, and it is also weak. Why is it not permissible to say: The soul's recollection of past states is conditional upon the soul's attachment to the body with which those states occurred; so if that body perishes, recollection of the states that occurred with that body is impossible? The second aspect of objection to this proof: We concede that they are pre-eternal, but why do you say they are not endless? His statement: 'If they were endless, the souls existing now would be infinite.' We say: This is based on the past cycles being infinite, and we have already shown the invalidity of that in the issue of temporal origination. We concede, but why is it not permissible to say: There exist now infinite souls? His statement: 'Every existing number is receptive to increase and decrease, and whatever is like that is finite.' We say: This premise involves a subtle inquiry which we have mentioned in the philosophical books.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage contains two further objections and their responses. The first objection (designated as the 'second argument') claims that if souls were pre-eternal, we would recall past lives; the author responds that this inductive claim is weak—recollection depends on the body with which the experiences occurred, so after that body's destruction, no memory remains. The second objection (second aspect) concedes pre-eternity but questions why souls cannot be endless. The objector argues that if souls were endless, the current number would be infinite—which is impossible. The author challenges the premise that past cycles are infinite (already refuted in the discussion on temporality) and also questions the assumption that an infinite number cannot exist, referring to his more detailed treatment in philosophical works. Thus both objections fail.

Key Terms
استقراء
تذكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Souls can remember all past events independent of a body.
Correction: Memory of past events is tied to the body in which they occurred; without that body, recollection is impossible.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi respond to the objection that we do not remember past lives?
A) He affirms that souls do remember but are unaware.
B) He says recollection depends on the body present when the events occurred.
C) He argues that memory is irrelevant to the soul's eternity.
D) He admits the objection is valid but argues it is only probable.

Key Takeaway
The memory objection fails because recollection depends on the body, and without it, no memory of past lives remains.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your memory every day to know who you are. But what if your memory of this life is tied only to this body? If your soul existed before this body, why do you not remember? Does that shake your confidence in the reality of your own existence?
?
Imam Razi says recollection depends on the body. Your body is temporary. Are you living as if your soul's true identity is only what you remember? Or are you preparing for a state where your body—and memory—will be renewed?
?
You have moments you wish you could forget, and moments you fear losing. But if memory is body-dependent, then your attachment to worldly memories may be misplaced. What are you clinging to that will perish with your body? What eternal memory are you building that no body-loss can erase?

Third Objection: Endlessness and Infinite Souls
Imam Razi responds to the endlessness objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's two-pronged response to the objection that endless souls would lead to an infinite number.

Core Idea
An infinite number of souls is not impossible if past cycles are finite.

The second objection concedes pre-eternity but questions why souls cannot be endless. The philosopher argues that if souls were endless, the current number would be infinite, which he considers impossible. Imam Razi challenges two premises: the infinity of past cycles and the impossibility of an actual infinite number. He refers to earlier discussions and to his philosophical works for deeper treatment.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the philosopher's argument and Imam Razi's two counterpoints.
The passage starts with 'الوجه الثاني في الاعتراض على هذه الحجة'.
2
Identify the two responses
Imam Razi first challenges the premise that past cycles are infinite. Then he questions why an infinite number cannot exist.
The second response is a concession but raises a subtle point.
3
Consider the subtle inquiry
Imam Razi does not fully resolve the infinite number issue here; he points to his philosophical works for a detailed discussion.
This shows the argument is not fully closed in this text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the second argument—which is his statement: 'If the spirits were pre-eternal, we would now remember something of past states'—we say: This is an appeal to induction, and it is also weak. We say: This premise involves a subtle inquiry which we have mentioned in the philosophical books.

Commentary
This passage contains two further objections and their responses. The first objection (designated as the 'second argument') claims that if souls were pre-eternal, we would recall past lives; the author responds that this inductive claim is weak—recollection depends on the body with which the experiences occurred, so after that body's destruction, no memory remains. The second objection (second aspect) concedes pre-eternity but questions why souls cannot be endless. The objector argues that if souls were endless, the current number would be infinite—which is impossible. According to Imam Razi, what is the first problem with the objection about endless souls?
A) Past cycles may not be infinite.
B) Souls are not pre-eternal.

?

Basitius on the Survival of Virtuous Souls
Imam Razi presents Basitius's view on soul survival based on knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Basitius's argument that only souls with knowledge of God survive bodily death, while ignorant souls perish.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God makes the soul immortal; ignorance renders it mortal.

The question of the soul's fate after death admits several answers. Imam Razi now presents a third view attributed to the sage Basitius, which distinguishes between souls based on their cognitive and moral state. This passage offers two philosophical proofs for why only knowledgeable souls endure, linking the soul's strength directly to its knowledge of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a statement of the view followed by two numbered proofs.
The term 'نفس فاضلة' refers to virtuous souls.
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof argues that if ignorant souls survived, they would remain either idle or tormented forever, which contradicts divine wisdom. Reflect on why ongoing idleness or torment would be unbecoming of a wise God.
3
Understand the second proof
The second proof connects knowledge to strength: knowing something gives inner strength, especially knowledge of God. Such souls become robust and survive bodily death, while weak ignorant souls perish.
Consider the analogy of a knowledgeable person versus an ignorant one in any field.
4
Contrast with the first view
Recall that earlier views held all rational souls survive. Basitius's view is more restrictive. How does this affect the understanding of divine justice?
This will be discussed in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول الثالث : - وهو قول من يقول : النفوس الفاضلة العالمة تبقى بعد موت البدن والنفوس الجاهلة تفنى مثل نفوس الأطفال والجهال - فهذا القول منقول عن الحكيم باسيطيوس، واحتج عليه بوجهين : الحجة الأولى : أن النفوس الخالية عن العلوم والأخلاق الفاضلة، لو بقيت بعد الموت لبقيت إما معطلة أو معذبة، وإبقاء الشيء في التعطيل أو في التعذيب أبد الآباد غير لائق بحكمة الحكيم. الحجة الثانية : أن العلم سبب للقوة، والجهل سبب للضعف، ألا ترى أن العالم بالشيء إذا خاض فيه كان قوي القلب والجاهل بالشيء إذا خاض فيه كان ضعيف القلب، ولا شك أن أجل العلوم وأعظمها هو العلم بالله تعالى، فالنفس إذا تحلت بهذه المعرفة قويت واشتدت فلا يضرها خراب البدن، ولذلك فإن العارفين إذا تخلوا في أنوار جلال الله تعالى لم يلتفتوا إلى شيء ولم يقيموا لشيء وزنًا. إذا عرفت هذا فالنفوس العالمة قوية فلا تفنى بموت البدن والنفوس الجاهلة تكون ضعيفة، فلا جرم تموت بموت البدن.
As for the third opinion—which is the view of those who say: The virtuous, knowledgeable souls remain after the death of the body, while ignorant souls perish, like the souls of children and the ignorant—this opinion is transmitted from the sage Basitius, and he argued for it with two proofs: The first proof: That the souls devoid of sciences and virtuous morals, if they remained after death, would remain either idle or tormented. Keeping a thing in idleness or torment forever is not befitting the wisdom of the Wise. The second proof: That knowledge is a cause of strength, and ignorance is a cause of weakness. Do you not see that the one who knows a thing, when he engages in it, is strong-hearted, while the one ignorant of a thing, when he engages in it, is weak-hearted? There is no doubt that the most sublime and greatest of sciences is knowledge of Allah the Exalted. When the soul adorns itself with this knowledge, it becomes strong and firm, so the destruction of the body does not harm it. For this reason, the knowers, when they bathe in the lights of the majesty of Allah the Exalted, pay no attention to anything and give no weight to anything. If you understand this, then the knowledgeable souls are strong and do not perish with the death of the body, while the ignorant souls are weak, so they inevitably die with the death of the body.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents a third view on the afterlife of the soul, attributed to the philosopher Basitius, who distinguishes between virtuous (knowledgeable) and ignorant souls. Two proofs are given: First, if ignorant souls survived, they would be left either idle (without activity) or tormented, and neither is compatible with divine wisdom forever. Second, knowledge imparts strength—a knower is strong-hearted, especially the knowledge of God—so such souls become robust and survive bodily death, whereas ignorant souls are weak and perish. The passage thus argues for a differentiated survival based on intellectual and moral attainment.

Key Terms
نفس فاضلة
نفس جاهلة
تعطيل
تعذيب
علم
قوة
معرفة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All rational souls survive death equally regardless of their moral or intellectual state.
Correction: This view holds that survival depends on knowledge and virtue; ignorant souls like those of children or the uneducated perish.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof of Basitius, why do knowledgeable souls survive death?
A) Because they have a different essence from ignorant souls.
B) Because knowledge of God makes the soul strong so bodily destruction does not affect it.
C) Because they have no attachment to the body.
D) Because they are rewarded by God.

Key Takeaway
Basitius argues that only souls possessing knowledge of God survive death, because such knowledge makes the soul strong and impervious to bodily decay, while ignorant souls are weak and perish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Basitius says that a soul without knowledge would be eternally idle or tormented. Is your soul currently in a state of idleness—spending time on trivialities—or tormented by worldly worries? What would it take to move toward the 'knowledge of God' that strengthens the soul?
?
The 'knowers' bathe in the lights of God's majesty and pay no attention to anything, giving nothing weight. Is there anything in your life—a relationship, a possession, a status—that you are clinging to as if it had ultimate weight? What would it mean to let go of that today?
?
If your soul's strength is measured by your knowledge of God, how would you assess the state of your most dominant thoughts? Do they revolve around Him or around distractions? What concrete step can you take this week to adorn your soul with more of His knowledge?

First Group: Rational Souls Perceive Universals
Razi presents the first subgroup's argument that rational souls perceive universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first group's argument that rational souls perceive universals, using the example of the judgment 'this person is a human'.

Core Idea
Rational souls survive because they grasp universals, not just particulars.

Imam Razi now examines detailed positions on spiritual resurrection. The first major opinion states that all rational souls persist after death. Within this, there is a subgroup that argues souls must perceive universals, as shown by our ability to make judgments that combine particular subjects with universal predicates. This passage introduces their foundational proof, which relies on the relationship between conceptualization and affirmation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Razi structures the argument step by step.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage has two parts: first, the classification of groups; second, the specific claim and proof of the first subgroup. Locate where the proof begins.
3
Analyze the proof
The proof uses the proposition 'this person is a human'. Identify the subject (particular) and predicate (universal). Then note that affirmation (tasdiq) requires prior conceptualization (tasawwur). What does this imply about the soul?
The proof shows the soul must have a concept of the universal to make such a judgment.
4
Reflect on the implication
If the soul can perceive universals, it is not limited to material particulars. This supports its immaterial nature and survival after death.
This argument is foundational for the spiritual resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا تفصيل مذاهب الناس في المعاد الروحاني . أما الأولون - وهم القائلون بأن النفوس الناطقة كلها باقية بعد موت الأبدان - فهؤلاء أيضًا طوائف : الطائفة الأولى : الذين يقولون : النفوس الناطقة مدركة للجزئيات. واحتجوا عليه بأنه يمكننا أن نحكم بأن هذا الشخص هو إنسان فهذه قضية موضوعها شخص معين - وهو جزئي - ومحمولها هو الإنسان - وهو كلي - والتصديق مسبوق بالتصور، فصاحب هذا التصديق عنده إدراك المحمول الذي هو الكلي،
This is the detail of the schools of thought regarding the spiritual resurrection. As for the first ones—those who say that all rational souls remain after the death of bodies—these are also groups: The first group: Those who say that rational souls perceive particulars. They argued for it by [the fact] that we can judge that 'this person is a human'; this is a proposition whose subject is a specific person—which is a particular—and whose predicate is 'human'—which is a universal—and affirmation is preceded by conceptualization. So the one holding this affirmation has a perception of the predicate, which is the universal.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the spiritual resurrection (al-ma'ad al-ruhani). It first summarizes the major division: all rational souls persist after bodily death. Then it introduces the first subgroup within that opinion: they claim that rational souls perceive particulars (juz'iyyat). Their proof is based on our ability to form judgments like 'this person is a human.' In such a judgment, the subject is a particular (this person) and the predicate is a universal (human). Since judgment requires prior conceptualization, the soul must have a concept of the universal. This shows the soul can grasp universals, not just particulars. The passage ends as the proof continues.

Key Terms
معاد روحاني
نفس ناطقة
جزئي
كلي
تصديق
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about sense perception; the soul perceives universals through the senses.
Correction: The argument is about intellectual cognition. The soul's ability to judge using universals indicates its immaterial nature and survival, not sensory perception.

Checkpoint
What is the premise of the first group's proof that rational souls perceive universals?
A) The soul remembers past lives.
B) We can judge 'this person is a human', which involves a universal predicate.
C) The soul is unaffected by bodily changes.
D) The soul has knowledge of God.

Key Takeaway
The first subgroup proves the soul's survival by showing it must perceive universals, as demonstrated in the judgment 'this person is a human'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say to yourself 'this person is a human', you are already performing a judgment that involves a universal. Does this realization make you more aware that your soul is not merely material, but possesses an intellectual capacity that transcends the physical? How will you live differently knowing that your very act of thinking proves your soul's immortality?
?
If your rational soul perceives universals and is therefore immaterial and survives death, what does that imply about the weight of your intellectual and spiritual development compared to your physical pursuits? Are you investing more in that which will perish or that which will remain?
?
The argument uses a simple everyday judgment to prove a profound truth. Are there other ordinary moments in your life where you overlook signs of the unseen reality? What would it mean for you to start seeing your daily acts of reasoning as evidence of your soul's lasting nature?

Only Virtuous Souls Remain: Third View on Soul Survival
Razi presents the third view on soul survival with two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third view on the survival of the soul after death, including its two proofs from the sage Basitius.

Core Idea
Knowledge strengthens the soul, ignorance weakens it, determining survival after death.

Having considered views that all souls survive or that only divine souls survive, Razi now presents a third view attributed to the sage Basitius. This view distinguishes between knowledgeable and ignorant souls, offering two proofs that rely on divine wisdom and the intrinsic strength from knowledge of God. Let's examine this position and its arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Note the structure: the view stated, then two proofs introduced with 'الحجة الأولى' and 'الحجة الثانية'.
The name Basitius likely refers to Pythagoras or a similar ancient sage.
2
Identify the first proof
The first proof argues that if ignorant souls remained after death, they would be either idle or tormented, which contradicts divine wisdom. Understand the reasoning: remaining in idleness or torment forever is not fitting for a wise God.
This proof appeals to the attribute of wisdom (الحكمة).
3
Identify the second proof
The second proof uses an observation: knowledge gives strength, ignorance gives weakness. Since knowledge of God is the greatest science, the soul that knows God becomes strong and unaffected by bodily death. Ignorant souls, being weak, perish with the body.
Notice the analogy with worldly knowledge and heart strength.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this view emphasizes the importance of acquiring knowledge of God. The survival of the soul is tied to its virtue and knowledge, not just its existence as a substance.
This view is distinct from the first two views mentioned earlier in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول الثالث : - وهو قول من يقول : النفوس الفاضلة العالمة تبقى بعد موت البدن والنفوس الجاهلة تفنى مثل نفوس الأطفال والجهال - فهذا القول منقول عن الحكيم باسيطيوس، واحتج عليه بوجهين : الحجة الأولى : أن النفوس الخالية عن العلوم والأخلاق الفاضلة، لو بقيت بعد الموت لبقيت إما معطلة أو معذبة، وإبقاء الشيء في التعطيل أو في التعذيب أبد الآباد غير لائق بحكمة الحكيم. الحجة الثانية : أن العلم سبب للقوة، والجهل سبب للضعف، ألا ترى أن العالم بالشيء إذا خاض فيه كان قوي القلب والجاهل بالشيء إذا خاض فيه كان ضعيف القلب، ولا شك أن أجل العلوم وأعظمها هو العلم بالله تعالى، فالنفس إذا تحلت بهذه المعرفة قويت واشتدت فلا يضرها خراب البدن ولذلك فإن العارفين إذا تخلوا في أنوار جلال الله تعالى لم يلتفتوا إلى شيء ولم يقيموا لشيء وزنًا. إذا عرفت هذا فالنفوس العالمة قوية فلا تفنى بموت البدن والنفوس الجاهلة تكون ضعيفة، فلا جرم تموت بموت البدن.
As for the third view—and it is the view of those who say: the virtuous, knowledgeable souls remain after the death of the body, while ignorant souls perish like the souls of children and the ignorant—this view is transmitted from the sage Basitius, and he argued for it with two proofs: The first proof: that souls devoid of sciences and virtuous morals, if they remained after death, would remain either idle or tormented, and keeping a thing in idleness or torment forever is not befitting the wisdom of the Wise. The second proof: that knowledge is a cause of strength, and ignorance is a cause of weakness. Do you not see that the knowledgeable person, when he engages in something, is strong-hearted, while the ignorant person, when he engages in something, is weak-hearted? There is no doubt that the greatest and most magnificent of sciences is knowledge of God Almighty. So when the soul adorns itself with this knowledge, it becomes strong and intense, and the destruction of the body does not harm it. Therefore, the knowers (arifun) when they immerse themselves in the lights of God's majesty pay no attention to anything and give no weight to anything. If you understand this, then the knowledgeable souls are strong and do not perish with the death of the body, while ignorant souls are weak, so inevitably they die with the death of the body.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents the third opinion on the fate of souls after death, attributed to the sage Basitius. The view distinguishes between knowledgeable/virtuous souls and ignorant souls. The first proof argues that if ignorant souls persisted after death, they would be either idle (without purpose) or tormented, which contradicts divine wisdom. The second proof draws an analogy: knowledge gives strength, ignorance gives weakness. The greatest knowledge is knowledge of God. A soul adorned with this knowledge becomes strong and unaffected by bodily death, just as knowers are indifferent to worldly things. Therefore, knowledgeable souls survive, while ignorant souls perish with the body.

Key Terms
النفوس الفاضلة
النفوس الجاهلة
بقاء النفس
فناء النفس
العلم بالله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this view denies bodily resurrection or that all souls are immortal by nature.
Correction: This view does not deny bodily resurrection; it focuses on the soul's survival based on its knowledge. Also, it teaches that not all souls are immortal; ignorant souls perish.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof for the view that only knowledgeable souls remain?
A) Knowledge strengthens the soul, ignorance weakens it
B) Wisdom requires that the soul not be idle
C) Ignorant souls would be tormented
D) The body harms the soul

Key Takeaway
The fate of the soul after death depends on its knowledge of God: virtuous souls survive, ignorant souls perish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the state of your own soul right now—its attachment to this world, its fears and anxieties—do you see signs of strength from knowledge of God or weakness from ignorance? What concrete step will you take today to feed your soul with knowledge of God?
?
If you were to die tonight, would your soul be among the 'knowledgeable and virtuous' that survive, or would it be vulnerable to annihilation? What evidence from your daily life supports your answer?
?
The passage says that the knowers (arifun) when immersed in God's majesty pay no attention to anything and give no weight to anything. What is it that you still give weight to that prevents you from being genuinely immersed in the knowledge of God? Will you let go of it today?
